10082-8{_}792{_}When at length it turned the corner and came into view, she rushed downstairs to meet Mrs. Holymead.
10082-8{_}793{_}She opened the street door before the lady of the house could ring.
10082-8{_}794{_}Her gaze was fixed on a hand-bag which Mrs. Holymead carried--a comparatively big hand-bag which the lady had taken the precaution to purchase before driving out to Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}795{_}The French girl's face lighted up with a smile as she saw by the shape of the bag that it was not empty.
10082-8{_}796{_}"Have you got them?"
10082-8{_}797{_}she whispered.
10082-8{_}798{_}"Yes," was the reply.
10082-8{_}799{_}"I followed out your plan--it worked without a hitch."
10082-8{_}800{_}"Ah, I knew you would manage it," said the girl.
10082-8{_}801{_}"I would have gone, but it was best that you should go.
10082-8{_}802{_}These police agents do not like foreigners--they would be suspicious if I had gone."
10082-8{_}803{_}"There was a big red-faced man in charge--Inspector Chippenfield, they called him," said Mrs. Holymead.
10082-8{_}804{_}"He was in the library as you said he would be--he was sitting there calmly as if he did not know what nerves were.
10082-8{_}805{_}He knew me as a friend of the family and was quite nice to me.
10082-8{_}806{_}I saw as soon as I went in that the desk was open--he had been examining Sir Horace's private papers.
10082-8{_}807{_}I asked him to tell me about the--about the tragedy.
10082-8{_}808{_}He piled horror on horror and then I pretended to faint.
10082-8{_}809{_}He ran down stairs for a glass of water, and that gave me time to open the secret drawer.
10082-8{_}810{_}They are here," she added, patting the hand-bag affectionately; "let us go upstairs and burn them."
10082-8{_}811{_}CHAPTER VI   There was unpleasant news for Inspector Chippenfield when Miss Fewbanks arrived at Riversbrook accompanied by the housekeeper, Mrs. Hewson.
10082-8{_}812{_}In the first place, he learnt with considerable astonishment that it was Miss Fewbanks's intention to stay at the house until after the funeral, and for that purpose she had brought the housekeeper to keep her company in the lonely old place.
10082-8{_}813{_}Although they had taken up their quarters in the opposite wing of the rambling mansion to that in which the dead body lay, it seemed to Inspector Chippenfield--whose mind was very impressionable where the fair sex was concerned--that Miss Fewbanks must be a very peculiar girl to contemplate staying in the same house with the body of her murdered father for nearly a week.
10082-8{_}814{_}He was convinced that she must be a strong-minded young woman, and he did not like strong-minded young women.
10082-8{_}815{_}He preferred the weak and clinging type of the sex as more of a compliment to his own sturdy manliness.
10082-8{_}816{_}His unfavourable impression of Miss Fewbanks was deepened when he saw her and heard what she had to tell him.
10082-8{_}817{_}The girl had come up from the country filled with horror at the crime which had deprived her of a father, and firmly determined to leave no stone unturned to bring the murderer to justice.
10082-8{_}818{_}It was true that she and her father had lived on terms of partial estrangement for some time past because of his manner of life, but all the girl's feelings of resentment against him had been swept away by the news of his dreadful death, and all she remembered now was that he was her father, and had been brutally murdered.
10082-8{_}819{_}When she sent for Inspector Chippenfield she had visited the room in which lay the body of her father.
10082-8{_}820{_}It had been placed in a coffin which was resting on the undertaker's trestles in the bay embrasure of the big room with the folding doors.
10082-8{_}821{_}There was nothing in the appearance of the corpse to suggest that a crime had been committed, but it had been impossible for the undertaker's men to erase entirely the distortion of the features so that they might suggest the cold, calm dignity of a peaceful death.
10082-8{_}822{_}The ordeal of looking on the dead body of her father had nerved her to carry through resolutely the task of discovering the author of the crime.
10082-8{_}823{_}She awaited the coming of the inspector in a small sitting-room, and when he entered she pointed quickly to a chair, but remained standing herself.
10082-8{_}824{_}In appearance Miss Fewbanks was a charming girl of the typical English type.
10082-8{_}825{_}She was of medium height, slight, but well-built, with fair hair and dark blue eyes, an imperious short upper lip and a determined chin, and the clear healthy complexion of a girl who has lived much out of doors.
10082-8{_}826{_}The inspector noted all these details; noted, too, that although her breast heaved with agitation she had herself well under control; her pretty head was erect, and one of her small hands was tightly clenched by her side.
10082-8{_}827{_}"Have you found out--anything?"
10082-8{_}828{_}she asked the inspector as he entered.
10082-8{_}829{_}The girl had chosen a vague word because she felt that there were many things which must come to light in unravelling the crime, but, from the police point of view of Inspector Chippenfield, the question whether he had found out anything was a stinging reflection on his ability.
10082-8{_}830{_}"I consider it inadvisable to make any arrest at the present stage of my investigations," he said, with cold official dignity.
10082-8{_}831{_}"Do you think you know who did it?"
10082-8{_}832{_}asked the girl.
10082-8{_}833{_}"It is my business to find out," replied the inspector, in a voice that indicated confidence in his ability to perform the task.
10082-8{_}834{_}The girl was too unsophisticated to follow the subtle workings of official pride.
10082-8{_}835{_}"The papers call it a mysterious crime.
10082-8{_}836{_}Do you think it is mysterious?"
10082-8{_}837{_}"There are certainly some mysterious features about it," said the inspector.
10082-8{_}838{_}"But I do not regard them as insoluble.
10082-8{_}839{_}Nothing is insoluble," he added, in a sententious tone.
10082-8{_}840{_}"If there are mysteries to be solved you ought to have help," said the young lady.
10082-8{_}841{_}She glanced at Mrs. Hewson significantly, and then proceeded to explain to Inspector Chippenfield what she meant.
10082-8{_}842{_}"I have asked Mr. Crewe, the celebrated detective, to assist you.
10082-8{_}843{_}Of course you know Mr. Crewe--everybody does.
10082-8{_}844{_}I know you are a very clever man at your profession, but in a thing of this kind two clever men are better than one.
10082-8{_}845{_}I hope you will not mind--there is no reflection whatever on your ability.
10082-8{_}846{_}In fact, I have the utmost confidence in you.
10082-8{_}847{_}But it is due to my father's memory to do all that is possible to get to the bottom of this dreadful crime.
10082-8{_}848{_}If money is needed it will be forthcoming.
10082-8{_}849{_}That applies to you no less than to Mr. Crewe.
10082-8{_}850{_}But I hope you will be able to carry out your investigations amicably together, and that you will be willing to assist one another.
10082-8{_}851{_}You will lose nothing by doing so.
10082-8{_}852{_}I trust you will place at Mr. Crewe's disposal all the facilities that are available to you as an officer of the police."
10082-8{_}853{_}This statement was so clear that Inspector Chippenfield had no choice but to face the conclusion that Miss Fewbanks had more faith in the abilities of a private detective to unravel the mystery than she had in the resources of Scotland Yard.
10082-8{_}854{_}He would have liked to have told the young lady what he thought of her for interfering with his work, and he determined to avail himself of the right opportunity to do so if it came along.
10082-8{_}855{_}But the statement that money was not to be spared had a soothing influence on his feelings.
10082-8{_}856{_}Of course, officers of Scotland Yard were not allowed to take gratuities however substantial they might be, but there were material ways of expressing gratitude which were outside the regulations of the department.
10082-8{_}857{_}"I shall be very pleased to give Mr. Crewe any assistance he wants," said Inspector Chippenfield, bowing stiffly.
10082-8{_}858{_}It was seldom that he took a subordinate fully into his confidence, but after he left Miss Fewbanks he flung aside his official pride in order to discuss with Rolfe the enlistment of the services of Crewe.
10082-8{_}859{_}Rolfe was no less indignant than his chief at the intrusion of an outsider into their sphere.
10082-8{_}860{_}Crewe was an exponent of the deductive school of crime investigation, and had first achieved fame over the Abbindon case some years ago, when he had succeeded in restoring the kidnapped heir of the Abbindon estates after the police had failed to trace the missing child.
10082-8{_}861{_}In detective stories the attitude of members of Scotland Yard to the deductive expert is that of admiration based on conscious inferiority, but in real life the experts of Scotland Yard have the utmost contempt for the deductive experts and their methods.
10082-8{_}862{_}The disdainful pity of the deductive experts for the rule-of-thumb methods of the police is not to be compared with the vigorous scorn of the official detective for the rival who has not had the benefit of police training.
10082-8{_}863{_}"Look here, Rolfe," said Inspector Chippenfield, "we mustn't let Crewe get ahead of us in this affair, or we'll never hear the last of it.
10082-8{_}864{_}It's scandalous of a man like Crewe, who has money of his own and could live like a gentleman, coming along and taking the bread out of our mouths by accepting fees and rewards for hunting after criminals.
10082-8{_}865{_}Of course I know they say he is lavish with his money and gives away more than he earns, but that's all bosh--he sticks it in his own pocket, right enough.
10082-8{_}866{_}One thing is certain: he gets paid whether he wins or loses; that is to say, he gets his fee in any case, but of course if he wins something will be added to his fee.
10082-8{_}867{_}In the meantime all you and I get is our salaries, and, as you know, the pay of an inspector isn't what it ought to be."
10082-8{_}868{_}Rolfe assured his superior of his conviction that the pay at Scotland Yard ought to be higher for all ranks--especially the rank and file.
10082-8{_}869{_}He also declared that he was ready to do his best to thwart Crewe.
10082-8{_}870{_}"That is the right spirit," commented Inspector Chippenfield approvingly.
10082-8{_}871{_}"Of course we'll tell him we're willing to help him all we can, and of course hell tell us we can depend on his help.
10082-8{_}872{_}But we know what his help will amount to.
10082-8{_}873{_}He'll keep back from us anything he finds out, and we'll do the same for him.
10082-8{_}874{_}But the point is, Rolfe, that you and I have to put all our brains into this and help one another.
10082-8{_}875{_}I'm not the man to despise help from a subordinate.
10082-8{_}876{_}If you have any ideas about this case, Rolfe, do not be afraid to speak out, I'll give them sympathetic consideration."
10082-8{_}877{_}"I know you will," said Rolfe, who was by no means sure of the fact.
10082-8{_}878{_}"You can count on me."
10082-8{_}879{_}"As you know, Rolfe, there have been cases in which men from the Yard haven't worked together as amicably as they ought to have done.
10082-8{_}880{_}It used to be said when I was one of the plain-clothes men that the man in charge got all the credit and the men under him did all the work.
10082-8{_}881{_}But as an inspector I can tell you that is very rarely the case.
10082-8{_}882{_}In my reports I believe in giving my junior credit for all he has done, and generally a bit more.
10082-8{_}883{_}It may be foolish of me, but that is my way.
10082-8{_}884{_}I never miss a chance of putting in a good word for the man under me."
10082-8{_}885{_}"It would be better if they were all like that," said Rolfe.
10082-8{_}886{_}"Well, it's a bargain, Rolfe," said Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}887{_}"You do your best on this job and you won't lose by it.
10082-8{_}888{_}I'll see to that.
10082-8{_}889{_}But in the meantime we don't want to put Crewe on the scent.
10082-8{_}890{_}Let us see how much we'll tell him and how much we won't."
10082-8{_}891{_}"He'll want to see the letter sent to the Yard about the murder," said Rolfe.
10082-8{_}892{_}"The _Daily Recorder_ published a facsimile of it this morning."
10082-8{_}893{_}"Yes, I knew about that.
10082-8{_}894{_}Well, he can have it.
10082-8{_}895{_}But don't say anything to him about that lace you found in the dead man's hand--or at any rate not until you find out more about it.
10082-8{_}896{_}The glove he can have since it is pretty obvious that it belonged to Sir Horace.
10082-8{_}897{_}We'll spin Crewe a yarn that we are depending on it as a clue."
10082-8{_}898{_}Crewe arrived during the afternoon to inspect the house and the room in which the crime had been committed.
10082-8{_}899{_}There was every appearance of cordiality in the way in which he greeted the police officials.
10082-8{_}900{_}"Delighted to see you, Inspector," he said.
10082-8{_}901{_}"Who is working this case with you?
10082-8{_}902{_}Rolfe?
10082-8{_}903{_}Don't think we have met before, Rolfe, have we?"
10082-8{_}904{_}Rolfe politely murmured something about not having had the pleasure of meeting Mr. Crewe, but of always having wanted to meet him, because of his fame.
10082-8{_}905{_}"Very good of you," replied Crewe.
10082-8{_}906{_}"This is a very sad business.
10082-8{_}907{_}I understand there are some attractive points of mystery in the crime.
10082-8{_}908{_}I hope you haven't unravelled it yet before I have got a start.
10082-8{_}909{_}You fellows are so quick."
10082-8{_}910{_}"Slow and sure is our motto," said Inspector Chippenfield, feeling certain that a sneer and not a compliment had been intended.
10082-8{_}911{_}"There is nothing to be gained in arresting the wrong man."
10082-8{_}912{_}"That's a sound maxim for us all," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}913{_}"However, let's get to business.
10082-8{_}914{_}I rang up the Yard this morning and they told me you were in charge of the case and that I'd probably find you here.
10082-8{_}915{_}Can you let me have a look at the original of that letter which was sent to Scotland Yard informing you of the murder?
10082-8{_}916{_}There is a facsimile of it in the _Daily Recorder_ this morning, and from all appearances there are some interesting conclusions to be drawn from it.
10082-8{_}917{_}But the original is the thing."
10082-8{_}918{_}"Here you are," said the inspector, producing his pocket-book, taking out the paper, and handing it to Crewe.
10082-8{_}919{_}"What do you make of it?"
10082-8{_}920{_}Crewe sat down, and placing the paper before him took a magnifying glass from his pocket.
10082-8{_}921{_}As he sat there, in his grey tweed suit, his hat pushed carelessly back from his forehead, he might have been mistaken for a young man of wealth with no serious business in life, for his clothes were of fashionable cut, and he wore them with an air of distinction.
10082-8{_}922{_}But a glance at his face would have dispelled the impression.
10082-8{_}923{_}The clear-cut, clean-shaven features riveted attention by reason of their strength and intelligence, and though the dark eyes were rather too dreamy for the face, the heavy lines of the lower jaw indicated the man of action and force of character.
10082-8{_}924{_}The thick neck and heavily-lipped firm mouth suggested tireless energy and abounding vitality.
10082-8{_}925{_}"At least two people have had a hand in it," he said, after studying the paper for a few minutes.
10082-8{_}926{_}"In the murder?"
10082-8{_}927{_}asked the inspector, who was astonished at a deduction which harmonised with a theory which had begun to take shape in his mind.
10082-8{_}928{_}"In writing this," said Crewe, with his attention still fixed on the paper.
10082-8{_}929{_}"But of course you know that yourself."
10082-8{_}930{_}"Of course," assented the inspector, who was surprised at the information, but was too experienced an official to show his feelings.
10082-8{_}931{_}"And both hands disguised."
10082-8{_}932{_}"Disguised to the extent of being printed in written characters," continued Crewe.
10082-8{_}933{_}"It is so seldom that a person writes printed characters that any method in which they are written suggests disguise.
10082-8{_}934{_}The original intention of the two persona who wrote this extraordinary note was for each to write a single letter in turn.
10082-8{_}935{_}That system was carried as far as 'Sir Horace' or, perhaps, up to the 'B' in 'Fewbanks.'
10082-8{_}936{_}After that they became weary of changing places and one of them wrote alternate letters to the end, leaving blanks for the other to fill in.
10082-8{_}937{_}That much is to be gathered from the variations in the spaces between the letters--sometimes there was too much room for an intermediate letter, sometimes too little, so the letter had to be cramped.
10082-8{_}938{_}Here and there are dots made with the pen as the first of the two spelled out the words so as to know what letters to write and what to leave blank.
10082-8{_}939{_}Look at the differences in the letter 'U.'
10082-8{_}940{_}One of the writers makes it a firm downward and upward stroke; the other makes the letter fainter and adds another downward stroke, the letter being more like a small 'u' written larger than a capital letter.
10082-8{_}941{_}The differences in the two hands are so pronounced throughout the note that I am inclined to think that one of the writers was a woman."
10082-8{_}942{_}"Exactly what I thought," said Inspector Chippenfield, looking hard at Crewe so that the latter should not question his good faith.
10082-8{_}943{_}"Then there are sometimes slight differences in the alternate letters written by the same hand.
10082-8{_}944{_}Look at the 'T' in 'last' and the 'T' in 'night'--the marked variation in the length and angle of the cross stroke.
10082-8{_}945{_}It is evident that the writers were labouring under serious excitement when they wrote this."
10082-8{_}946{_}Rolfe was so interested in Crewe's revelations that he stood beside the deductive expert and studied the paper afresh.
10082-8{_}947{_}"And now, about finger-prints?"
10082-8{_}948{_}asked Crews.
10082-8{_}949{_}"None," was the reply of the inspector, "We had it under the microscope at Scotland Yard."
10082-8{_}950{_}"None?"
10082-8{_}951{_}exclaimed Crewe, in surprise.
10082-8{_}952{_}"Why adopt such precautions as wearing gloves to write a note giving away this startling secret?"
10082-8{_}953{_}"Easy enough," replied Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}954{_}"The people who wrote the note either had little or nothing to do with the murder, but were afraid suspicion might be directed to them, or else they are the murderers and want to direct suspicion from themselves."
10082-8{_}955{_}"And now for the bullets," said Crewe, "I understand two shots were fired."
10082-8{_}956{_}"From two revolvers," said the inspector.
10082-8{_}957{_}"Here are both bullets.
10082-8{_}958{_}This one I picked out of the wall over there.
10082-8{_}959{_}You can see where I've broken away the plaster.
10082-8{_}960{_}This one--much the bigger one of the two--was the one that killed Sir Horace.
10082-8{_}961{_}The doctor handed it to me after the post-mortem."
10082-8{_}962{_}"Did Sir Horace keep a revolver?"
10082-8{_}963{_}"The butler says yes.
10082-8{_}964{_}But if he did it's gone."
10082-8{_}965{_}Crewe stood up and examined the hole in the wall where Inspector Chippenfield had dug out the smaller bullet.
10082-8{_}966{_}"Sir Horace made a bid for his life but missed.
10082-8{_}967{_}Of course, he had no time to take aim while there was a man on the other side of the room covering him, but in any case those fancy firearms cannot be depended upon to shoot straight."
10082-8{_}968{_}"You think Sir Horace fired at his murderer--fired first?"
10082-8{_}969{_}asked Rolfe.
10082-8{_}970{_}"This small bullet suggests one of those fancy silver-mounted weapons that are made to sell to wealthy people.
10082-8{_}971{_}Sir Horace was a bit of a sportsman, and knew something about game-shooting, but, I take it, he had no use for a revolver.
10082-8{_}972{_}I assume he kept one of those fancy weapons on hand thinking he would never have to use it, but that it would do to frighten a burglar if the occasion did arise."
10082-8{_}973{_}"And when he was held up in this room by a man with a revolver he made a dash for his own revolver and got in the first shot?"
10082-8{_}974{_}suggested Rolfe, with the idea of outlining Crewe's theory of how the crime was committed.
10082-8{_}975{_}"It is scarcely possible to reconstruct the crime to that extent," said Crewe with a smile.
10082-8{_}976{_}"But undoubtedly Sir Horace got in the first shot.
10082-8{_}977{_}If he fired after he was hit his bullet would have gone wild--would probably have struck the ceiling--whereas it landed there.
10082-8{_}978{_}Let us measure the height from the floor."
10082-8{_}979{_}He pulled a small spool out of a waistcoat pocket and drew out a tape measure.
10082-8{_}980{_}"A little high for the heart of an average man, and probably a foot wide of the mark."
10082-8{_}981{_}"And what do you make of the disappearance of Sir Horace's revolver?"
10082-8{_}982{_}asked Rolfe, who seemed to his superior officer to be in danger of displaying some admiration for deductive methods.
10082-8{_}983{_}"I'm no good at guess-work," replied Crewe, who felt that he had given enough information away.
10082-8{_}984{_}"Well," said Rolfe, "here is a glove which was found in the room.
10082-8{_}985{_}The other one is missing.
10082-8{_}986{_}It might be a clue."
10082-8{_}987{_}Crewe took the glove and examined it carefully.
10082-8{_}988{_}It was a left-hand glove made of reindeer-skin, and grey in colour.
10082-8{_}989{_}It bore evidence of having been in use, but it was still a smart-looking glove such as a man who took a pride in his appearance might wear.
10082-8{_}990{_}"Burglars wear gloves nowadays," said Crewe, "but not this kind.
10082-8{_}991{_}The india-rubber glove with only the thumb separate is best for their work.
10082-8{_}992{_}They give freedom of action for the fingers and leave no finger-prints.
10082-8{_}993{_}Have you made inquiries whether this is one of Sir Horace's gloves?"
10082-8{_}994{_}"Well, it is the same size as he wore--seven and a half," said Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}995{_}"The butler is the only servant here and he can't say for certain that it belonged to his master.
10082-8{_}996{_}I've been through Sir Horace's wardrobe and through the suit-case he brought from Scotland, but I can find no other pair exactly similar.
10082-8{_}997{_}Rolfe took it to Sir Horace's hosier, and he is practically certain that the glove is one of a pair he sold to Sir Horace."
10082-8{_}998{_}"That should be conclusive," said Crewe thoughtfully.
10082-8{_}999{_}"So I think," replied the inspector.
10082-8{_}1000{_}"Well, I'll take it with me, if you don't mind," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}1001{_}"You can have it back whenever you want it.
10082-8{_}1002{_}Let me have the address of Sir Horace's hosier--I'll give him a call."
10082-8{_}1003{_}"Take it by all means," said the inspector cordially, referring to the glove.
10082-8{_}1004{_}And with a wink at Rolfe he added, "And when you are ready to fit it on the guilty hand I hope you will let us know."
10082-8{_}1005{_}CHAPTER VII   Crewe made a careful inspection of the house and the grounds.
10082-8{_}1006{_}He took measurements of the impressions left on the sill of the window which had been forced and also of the foot-prints immediately beneath the window.
10082-8{_}1007{_}He had a long conversation with Hill and questioned him regarding his movements on the night of the murder.
10082-8{_}1008{_}He also asked about the other servants who were at Dellmere, and probed for information about Sir Horace's domestic life and his friends.
10082-8{_}1009{_}As he was talking to Hill, Police-Constable Flack came up to them with a card in his hand.
10082-8{_}1010{_}Hill looked at the card and exclaimed:  "Mr. Holymead?
10082-8{_}1011{_}What does he want?"
10082-8{_}1012{_}"He asked if Miss Fewbanks was at home."
10082-8{_}1013{_}Hill took the card in to Miss Fewbanks, and on coming out went to the front door and escorted Mr. Holymead to his young mistress.
10082-8{_}1014{_}Crewe, as was his habit, looked closely at Holymead.
10082-8{_}1015{_}The eminent K.C.
10082-8{_}1016{_}was a tall man, nearly six feet in height, with a large, resolute, strongly-marked face which, when framed in a wig, was suggestive of the dignity and severity of the law.
10082-8{_}1017{_}In years he was about fifty, and in his figure there was a suggestion of that rotundity which overtakes the man who has given up physical exercise.
10082-8{_}1018{_}He was correctly, if sombrely, dressed in dark clothes, and he wore a black tie--probably as a symbol of mourning for his friend.
10082-8{_}1019{_}His gloves were a delicate grey.
10082-8{_}1020{_}Crewe sought out Hill again and questioned him closely about the relations which had existed between Sir Horace Fewbanks and Mr. Holymead, whose enormous practice brought him in an income three times as large as the dead judge's, and kept him constantly before the public.
10082-8{_}1021{_}Hill was able to supply the detective with some interesting information regarding the visitor, and, in contrast to his manner when previously questioned at random by Crewe, concerning his young mistress's habits, seemed willing, if not actually anxious, to talk.
10082-8{_}1022{_}He had heard from Sir Horace's housekeeper that his late master and Mr. Holymead had been law students together, and after they were called to the Bar they used to spend their holidays together as long as they were single.
10082-8{_}1023{_}When they were married their wives became friends.
10082-8{_}1024{_}Mrs. Holymead had died fourteen years ago, but Mrs. Fewbanks--Sir Horace had not been a baronet while his wife was alive--had lived some years longer.
10082-8{_}1025{_}Mr. Holymead had married again.
10082-8{_}1026{_}His second wife was a very beautiful young lady, if he might make so bold as to say so, who had come from America.
10082-8{_}1027{_}The butler added deprecatingly that he had been told that both Sir Horace and Mr. Holymead had paid her some attention, and that she could have had either of them.
10082-8{_}1028{_}She was different to English ladies, he added.
10082-8{_}1029{_}She had more to say for herself, and laughed and talked with the gentlemen just as if she was one of themselves.
10082-8{_}1030{_}Hill mentioned that she had been out to see Miss Fewbanks the previous day, but that Miss Fewbanks had not come up from Dellmere then, so she had seen Inspector Chippenfield instead.
10082-8{_}1031{_}While Crewe and the butler were talking a boy of about fourteen, with the shrewd face of a London arab, approached them with an air of mystery.
10082-8{_}1032{_}He came down the hall with long cautious strides, and halted at each step as if he were stalking a band of Indians in a forest.
10082-8{_}1033{_}"Well, Joe, what is it?"
10082-8{_}1034{_}asked Crewe, as he came to a halt in front of them.
10082-8{_}1035{_}"If you don't want me for half an hour, sir, I'd like to take a run up the street.
10082-8{_}1036{_}There is a real good picture house just been opened."
10082-8{_}1037{_}The boy spoke eagerly, with his bright eyes fixed on Crewe.
10082-8{_}1038{_}"I may want you any minute, Joe," replied Crewe.
10082-8{_}1039{_}"Don't go away."
10082-8{_}1040{_}The boy nodded his head, and turned away.
10082-8{_}1041{_}As he went down the hall again to the front door he gave an imitation of a man walking with extended arms across a plank spanning a chasm.
10082-8{_}1042{_}"Picture mad," commented Crewe, as he watched him.
10082-8{_}1043{_}"I didn't quite understand you, sir," replied the butler.
10082-8{_}1044{_}"Spends all his spare time in cinemas," said Crewe, "and when he is not there he is acting picture dramas.
10082-8{_}1045{_}His ambition in life is to be a cinema actor."
10082-8{_}1046{_}Crewe engaged Police-Constable Flack in conversation while waiting for Mr. Holymead to take his departure.
10082-8{_}1047{_}Flack had so little professional pride that he was pleased at meeting a gentleman who usurped the functions of a detective without having had any police training, and who could beat the best of the Scotland Yard men like shelling peas, as he confided to his wife that night.
10082-8{_}1048{_}He was especially flattered at the interest Crewe seemed to display in his long connection with the police force, and also in his private affairs.
10082-8{_}1049{_}The constable was explaining with parental vanity the precocious cleverness of his youngest child, a girl of two, when Holymead made his appearance, and he became aware that Mr. Crewe's interest in children was at an end.
10082-8{_}1050{_}"Look at that man," said Crewe, in a sharp imperative tone to the police-constable, as the K.C.
10082-8{_}1051{_}was walking down the path of the Italian garden to the plantation.
10082-8{_}1052{_}"You saw him come in?"
10082-8{_}1053{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}1054{_}"Do you see any difference?"
10082-8{_}1055{_}"No, sir; he's the same man," said Flack, with stolid certainty.
10082-8{_}1056{_}"Anything about him that is different?"
10082-8{_}1057{_}continued Crewe.
10082-8{_}1058{_}Police-Constable Flack looked at Crewe in some bewilderment.
10082-8{_}1059{_}He was not a deductive expert, and, as he told his wife afterwards, he did not know what the detective was "driving at."
10082-8{_}1060{_}He took another long look at Holymead, who was then within a few yards of the plantation on his way to the gates, and remarked, in a hesitating tone, as though to justify his failure:  "Well, you see, sir, when he was coming in it was the front view I saw, now I can only see his back."
10082-8{_}1061{_}But before he had finished speaking Crewe had left him and was following the K.C.
10082-8{_}1062{_}Holymead had gone into the house without a walking-stick, and had reappeared carrying one on his arm.
10082-8{_}1063{_}Crewe admired the cool audacity which had prompted Holymead to go into a house where a murder had been committed to recover his stick under the very eyes of the police, and he immediately formed the conclusion that the K.C.
10082-8{_}1064{_}had come to the house to recover the stick for some urgent reason possibly not unconnected with the crime.
10082-8{_}1065{_}And it was apparent that Holymead was a shrewd judge of human nature, Crewe reflected, for he calculated that the rareness of the quality of observation, even in those who, like Flack, were supposed to keep their eyes open, would permit him to do so unnoticed.
10082-8{_}1066{_}As Crewe went down the path he beckoned to the boy Joe, who at the moment was acting the part of a comic dentist binding a recalcitrant patient to a chair, using an immense old-fashioned straight-backed chair which stood in the hall, for his stage setting.
10082-8{_}1067{_}Joe overtook his master as he entered the ornamental plantation in front of the house, and Crewe quickly whispered his instructions, as the retreating figure of the K.C.
10082-8{_}1068{_}threaded the wood towards the gates.
10082-8{_}1069{_}"When I catch up level with him, Joe, you are to run into him accidentally from behind, and knock his stick off his arm, so that it falls near me.
10082-8{_}1070{_}I will pick it up and return it to him.
10082-8{_}1071{_}I must handle the stick--you understand?
10082-8{_}1072{_}Do not wait to see how he takes it when you bump into him--get off round the corner at once and wait for me."
10082-8{_}1073{_}Crewe quickened his pace to overtake the man in front of him.
10082-8{_}1074{_}He gave no glance backward at the boy, for he knew his instructions would be carried out faithfully and intelligently.
10082-8{_}1075{_}He allowed Holymead to reach the big open gates, and turn from the gravelled carriage drive into the private street.
10082-8{_}1076{_}Then he hurried after him and drew level with Holymead.
10082-8{_}1077{_}As he did so there was a sound of running footsteps from behind, and then a shout.
10082-8{_}1078{_}Joe had cleverly tripped and fallen heavily between the two men, bringing down Holymead in his fall.
10082-8{_}1079{_}The K.C.
10082-8{_}1080{_}'s stick flew off his arm and bounded half a dozen yards away.
10082-8{_}1081{_}Crewe stepped forward quickly, secured the stick, glanced quickly at the monogram engraved on it, and held it out to Holymead, who was brushing the dust off his clothes with vexatious remarks about the clumsiness and impudence of street boys.
10082-8{_}1082{_}For a moment he seemed to hesitate about taking the stick.
10082-8{_}1083{_}"I believe this is yours," said Crewe politely.
10082-8{_}1084{_}"Ah--yes.
10082-8{_}1085{_}Thank you," said the K.C., giving him a keen suspicious glance.
10082-8{_}1086{_}CHAPTER VIII   Crewe had well-furnished offices in Holborn but lived in a luxurious flat in Jermyn Street.
10082-8{_}1087{_}Although he went to and fro between them daily, his personality was almost a dual one, though not consciously so; his passion for crime investigation was distinct--in outward seeming, at all events--from his polished West End life of wealthy ease.
10082-8{_}1088{_}Grave, self-contained, and inscrutable, he slipped from one to the other with an effortless regularity, and the fashionable folk with whom he mixed in his leisured bachelor existence in the West End, apart from knowing him as the famous Crewe, had even less knowledge of the real man behind his suave exterior than the clients who visited his inquiry rooms in Holborn to confide in him their stories of suffering, shame, or crimes committed against them.
10082-8{_}1089{_}His commissionaire and body-servant, Stork, had once, in a rare--almost unique--convivial moment, declared to the caretaker of the building that he knew no more about his master after ten years than he did the first day he entered his service.
10082-8{_}1090{_}He was deep beyond all belief, was Stork's opinion, delivered with reluctant admiration.
10082-8{_}1091{_}Although Crewe did not allow the externals of his two existences to become involved, his chief interest in life was in his work.
10082-8{_}1092{_}He had originally taken up detective work more as a relief from the boredom of his lot as a wealthy young man, leading an aimless, useless life with others of his class, than by deliberate choice of his vocation.
10082-8{_}1093{_}His initial successes surprised him; then the work absorbed him and became his life's career.
10082-8{_}1094{_}He had achieved some memorable successes and he had made a few failures, but the failures belonged to the earlier portion of his career, before he had learnt to trust thoroughly in his own great gifts of intuition and insight, and that uncanny imagination which sometimes carried him successfully through when all else failed.
10082-8{_}1095{_}Serious devotees of chess knew the name of Crewe in another capacity--as the name of a man who might have aspired to great deeds if he had but taken the game as his life's career.
10082-8{_}1096{_}He had flashed across the chess horizon some years previously as a player of surpassing brilliance by defeating Turgieff, when the great Russian master had visited London and had played twelve simultaneous boards at the London Chess Club.
10082-8{_}1097{_}Crewe was the only player of the twelve to win his game, and he did so by a masterly concealed ending in which he handled his pawns with consummate skill, proffering the sacrifice of a bishop with such art that Turgieff fell into the trap, and was mated in five subsequent moves.
10082-8{_}1098{_}Crewe proved this was not merely a lucky win by defeating the young South American champion, Caranda, shortly afterwards, when the latter visited England and played a series of exhibition games in London on his way to Moscow, where he was engaged in the championship tourney.
10082-8{_}1099{_}Once again it was masterly pawn play which brought Crewe a fine victory, and aged chess enthusiasts who followed every move of the game with trembling excitement, declared afterwards that Crewe's conception of this particular game had not been equalled since Morphy died.
10082-8{_}1100{_}They predicted a dazzling chess career for Crewe, but he disappointed their aged hearts by retiring suddenly from match chess, and they mourned him as one unworthy of his great chess gifts and the high hopes they had placed in him.
10082-8{_}1101{_}But, as a matter of fact, Crewe's intellect was too vigorous and active to be satisfied with the triumphs of chess, and his disappearance from the chess world was contemporary with his entrance into detective work, which appealed to his imagination and found scope for his restless mental activity.
10082-8{_}1102{_}But if detective work so absorbed him that he gave up match chess entirely, he still retained an interest in the science of chess, reserving problem play for his spare moments, and, when not immersed in the solution of a problem of human mystery, he would turn to the chessboard and seek solace and relaxation in the mysteries of an intricate "four-mover."
10082-8{_}1103{_}He had once said that there was a certain affinity between solving chess problems and the detection of crime mystery: once the key-move was found, the rest was comparatively easy.
10082-8{_}1104{_}But he added with a sigh that a really perfect crime mystery was as rare as a perfect chess problem: human ingenuity was not sufficiently skilful, as a rule, to commit a crime or construct a chess problem with completely artistic concealment of the key-move, and for that reason most problems and crimes were far too easy of detection to absorb one's intellectual interests and attention.
10082-8{_}1105{_}It was the morning after Crewe's visit to Riversbrook, and the detective sat in his private office glancing through a note-book which contained a summary of the Hampstead mystery.
10082-8{_}1106{_}Crewe was a painstaking detective as well as a brilliant one, and it was his custom to prepare several critical summaries of any important case on which he was engaged, writing and rewriting the facts and his comments, until he was satisfied that he had a perfect outline to work upon, with the details and clues of the crime in consecutive order and relation to one another.
10082-8{_}1107{_}Experience had taught him that the time and labour this task involved were well-spent.
10082-8{_}1108{_}If an unexpected development of the case altered the facts of the original summary Crewe prepared another one in the same painstaking way.
10082-8{_}1109{_}The summaries, when done with, were methodically filed and indexed and stored in a strong room at the office for future reference, where he also kept full records of all the cases upon which he had been engaged, together with the weapons and articles that had figured in them: huge volumes of newspaper reports and clippings; photographs of criminals with their careers appended; and a host of other odds and ends of his detective investigations--the whole forming an interesting museum of crime and mystery which would have furnished a store of rich material for a fresh Newgate Calendar.
10082-8{_}1110{_}It was an axiom of Crewe's that a detective never knew when some old scrap of information or some trifling article of some dead and forgotten crime might not afford a valuable clue.
10082-8{_}1111{_}Expert criminals frequently repeated themselves, like people in lesser walks of life, and Crewe's "library and museum," as he called it, had sometimes furnished him with a simple hint for the solution of a mystery which had defied more subtle methods of analysis.
10082-8{_}1112{_}Crewe, after carefully reading his summary of the murder of Sir Horace Fewbanks, and making a few alterations in the text, drew from his pocket the glove which Inspector Chippenfield had handed him as a clue, took it to the window, and carefully examined it through a large magnifying glass.
10082-8{_}1113{_}He was thus engrossed when the door was noiselessly opened, and Stork, the bodyguard, entered.
10082-8{_}1114{_}Stork belied his name.
10082-8{_}1115{_}He was short and fat, with a red mottled face; a model of discretion and imperturbability, who had served Crewe for ten years, and bade fair to serve him another ten, if he lived that long.
10082-8{_}1116{_}In his heart of hearts he often wondered why a gentleman like Crewe should so far forget what was due to his birth and position as to have offices in Holborn--Holborn, of all parts of London!
10082-8{_}1117{_}But the awe he felt for Crewe prevented his seeking information on the point from the only person who could give it to him, so he served him and puzzled over him in silence, his inward perturbation of spirits being made manifest occasionally by a puzzled glance at his master when the latter was not looking.
10082-8{_}1118{_}It was nothing to Stork that his master was a famous detective; the problem to him was _why_ he was a detective when he had no call to be one, having more money than any man--and let alone a single man--could spend in a lifetime.
10082-8{_}1119{_}Stork coughed slightly to attract Crewe's attention.
10082-8{_}1120{_}"If you please, sir," he said, "the boy has come."
10082-8{_}1121{_}While Crewe was busy with his magnifying glass Stork returned with the boy who had accompanied Crewe on his visit to Riversbrook on the previous day.
10082-8{_}1122{_}The boy, a thin white-faced, sharp-eyed London street urchin, seemed curiously out of place in the handsomely furnished office, with his legs tucked up under the carved rail of a fine old oak chair, and his big dark eyes fixed intently on Crewe's face.
10082-8{_}1123{_}The tie between him and the detective was an unusual one.
10082-8{_}1124{_}It dated back some twelve months, when Crewe, in the investigation of a peculiarly baffling crime, found it advisable to disguise himself and live temporarily in a crowded criminal quarter of Islington.
10082-8{_}1125{_}The rooms he took were above a secondhand clothing shop kept by a drunken female named Leaver; a supposed widow who lived at the back of the shop with her two children, Lizzie, a bold-eyed girl of 17, who worked at a Clerkenwell clothing factory, and Joe, a typical Cockney boy of fourteen, who sold papers in the streets during the day and was fast qualifying for a thief at night when Crewe went to the place to live.
10082-8{_}1126{_}Crewe soon discovered, through overhearing a loud quarrel between his landlady and her daughter, that Mrs. Leaver's husband was alive, though dead to his wife for all practical purposes, inasmuch as he was serving a life's imprisonment for manslaughter.
10082-8{_}1127{_}A fortnight after he had taken up his temporary quarters above the shop the woman was removed to the hospital suffering from the effects of a hard drinking bout, and died there.
10082-8{_}1128{_}The girl disappeared, and the boy would have been turned out on the streets but for Crewe, who had taken a liking to him.
10082-8{_}1129{_}Joe was self-reliant, alert, and precocious, like most London street boys, but in addition to these qualities he had a vein of imagination unusual in a lad of his upbringing and environment.
10082-8{_}1130{_}He devoured the exciting feuilleton stories in the evening papers he vended, and spent his spare pennies at the cinema theatres in the vicinity of his poor home.
10082-8{_}1131{_}His appreciation of the crude mysteries of the filmed detective drama amused the famous expert in the finer art of actual crime detection, until he discovered that the boy possessed natural gifts of intuition and observation, combined with penetration.
10082-8{_}1132{_}Crewe grew interested in developing the boy's talent for detective work.
10082-8{_}1133{_}When the lad's mother died Crewe decided to take him into his Holborn offices as messenger-boy.
10082-8{_}1134{_}Crewe soon discovered that Joe had a useful gift for "shadowing" work, and his street training as a newspaper runner enabled him not only to follow a person through the thickest of London traffic, but to escape observation where a man might have been noticed and suspected.
10082-8{_}1135{_}"Well, Joe," said Crewe, as the boy entered on the heels of Stork, "I have a job for you this morning.
10082-8{_}1136{_}I want you to find the glove corresponding to this one."
10082-8{_}1137{_}Crewe, having finished his examination of the glove, handed it to the boy, whose first act was to slip it on his left hand and move his fingers about to assure himself that they were in good working order in spite of being hidden.
10082-8{_}1138{_}It was the first occasion on which Joe had worn a glove.
10082-8{_}1139{_}"It was found in the room in which Sir Horace Fewbanks was murdered," continued Crewe.
10082-8{_}1140{_}"The other one was not there.
10082-8{_}1141{_}The question I want to solve is, did it belong to Sir Horace, or to some one who visited him on the night he was murdered?
10082-8{_}1142{_}The police think it belonged to Sir Horace because it is the same size as the gloves he wore, and because Sir Horace's hosier stocks the same kind--as does nearly every fashionable hosier in London.
10082-8{_}1143{_}They think he lost the right-hand glove on his way up from Scotland.
10082-8{_}1144{_}It will occur to you, Joe, though you don't wear gloves, that it is more common for men to lose the right-hand glove than the left-hand, because the right hand is used a great deal more than the left, and even men who would not be seen in the street without gloves find there are many things they cannot do with a gloved hand.
10082-8{_}1145{_}For instance, to dive one's hand into one's trouser pocket where most men keep their loose change the glove has to be removed."
10082-8{_}1146{_}"Then the gentleman would take off his right glove when he paid for his taxi-cab from St. Pancras," said Joe, who was familiar through the accounts in the newspapers with the main details of the Fewbanks mystery.
10082-8{_}1147{_}"Right, Joe," said his master approvingly.
10082-8{_}1148{_}"And in that case he dropped the glove between the taxi-cab outside his front gates and his room, and it would have been found.
10082-8{_}1149{_}I have made inquiries and I am satisfied it was not found."
10082-8{_}1150{_}"He might have lost it when he was getting into the train at Scotland," suggested the lad.
10082-8{_}1151{_}"He had to change trains at Glasgow--he might have lost it there."
10082-8{_}1152{_}"That is a rule-of-thumb deduction," said Crewe, with a kindly smile.
10082-8{_}1153{_}"It is good enough for the police, for they have apparently adopted it, but it is not good enough for me.
10082-8{_}1154{_}What you don't understand, Joe, is that an odd glove is of no value in the eyes of a man who wears gloves.
10082-8{_}1155{_}He doesn't take it home as a memento of his carelessness in losing the other.
10082-8{_}1156{_}He throws it away.
10082-8{_}1157{_}Therefore if this is Sir Horace's glove he took it home because he was unaware that he had lost the other.
10082-8{_}1158{_}He would put on his gloves before leaving the train at St. Pancras.
10082-8{_}1159{_}And he would pull off the right-hand one--he was not left-handed--when the taxi-cab was nearing his home so as to be able to pay the fare.
10082-8{_}1160{_}Therefore, if it is Sir Horace's glove the fellow to it was dropped in the taxi-cab, or dropped between the taxi-cab and the house.
10082-8{_}1161{_}If the glove had been lost at the other end of the journey in Scotland Sir Horace would have flung this one out of the carriage window when he became aware of the loss.
10082-8{_}1162{_}As I have told you no glove was found between the gate at Riversbrook and the room in which Sir Horace was murdered.
10082-8{_}1163{_}I got from the police the number of the taxi-cab in which Sir Horace was driven from St. Pancras, and the driver tells me that no glove was left in his cab.
10082-8{_}1164{_}So what have we to do next, Joe?"
10082-8{_}1165{_}"To find the missing glove?
10082-8{_}1166{_}It's a tough job, ain't it, sir?"
10082-8{_}1167{_}"Yes and no," replied Crewe.
10082-8{_}1168{_}"It is possible to make some reasonable safe deductions in regard to it.
10082-8{_}1169{_}These would indicate what had happened to it, and knowing where to look, or, rather, in what circumstances we might expect to find it, we might throw a little light on it.
10082-8{_}1170{_}In the first place, it might be assumed that if the glove did not belong to Sir Horace it belonged to some one who visited him on the night he returned unexpectedly from Scotland.
10082-8{_}1171{_}That indicates that his visitor knew Sir Horace was returning; a most important point, for if he knew Sir Horace was returning he knew why he was returning--which no one else knows up to the present as far as I have been able to gather--and in all probability was responsible for his return, say, sent him a letter or a telegram which brought him to London.
10082-8{_}1172{_}So we come to the possibility of an angry scene in the room in which Sir Horace's dead body was subsequently found.
10082-8{_}1173{_}We have the possibility of the visitor leaving the house in a high state of excitement, hastily snatching up the hat and gloves he had taken off when he arrived, and in his excitement dropping unnoticed the right-hand glove on the floor."
10082-8{_}1174{_}"And leaving his gold-mounted stick behind him," said Joe, who was following his master's line of reasoning with keen interest.
10082-8{_}1175{_}"Right, Joe," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}1176{_}"That was placed in the stand in the hall, and when the visitor left hurriedly was entirely forgotten.
10082-8{_}1177{_}But at what stage did the visitor become conscious of the loss of his glove?
10082-8{_}1178{_}Not until his excitement cooled down a little.
10082-8{_}1179{_}How long he took to cool down depends upon the cause of his excitement and his temperament, things which, at present, we can only guess at.
10082-8{_}1180{_}He would probably walk a long distance before he cooled down.
10082-8{_}1181{_}Then he would resume his normal habits and among other things would put on his gloves--if he had them.
10082-8{_}1182{_}He would find that he had lost one and that he had left his stick behind.
10082-8{_}1183{_}He would know that the stick had been left behind in the hall, but he would not know the glove had been dropped in the house.
10082-8{_}1184{_}The probabilities are that he would think he had dropped it while walking.
10082-8{_}1185{_}But if he felt that he had dropped it in the house, and he had the best of all reasons for not wishing anyone to know that he had visited Sir Horace that night, he would destroy the remaining glove and our chance of tracing it would be gone.
10082-8{_}1186{_}The fact that he had left his stick behind was a minor matter that he could easily account for if he had been a friend of Sir Horace who had been in the habit of visiting Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}1187{_}If anything cropped up subsequently about the stick he could say that he had left it there before Sir Horace closed up his house and went to Scotland.
10082-8{_}1188{_}"But the problem of the glove is a different matter, Joe.
10082-8{_}1189{_}There are three phases to it: first, if the visitor thought he had dropped it in the house and wanted to keep his visit there a profound secret from subsequent inquiry he would take home the remaining glove and destroy it--probably by burning it.
10082-8{_}1190{_}Secondly, if he thought he had dropped it after leaving the house he would not feel that safety necessitated the destruction of the remaining one, but he would probably throw it away where it would not be likely to be found.
10082-8{_}1191{_}In the third place, if he had no particular reason for wishing to hide the fact that he had visited Riversbrook he would throw it away anywhere when he became conscious that he had lost the other.
10082-8{_}1192{_}He would throw it away merely because an odd glove is of no use to a man who wears gloves.
10082-8{_}1193{_}The man who doesn't wear gloves would pick up an odd glove from the ground and think he had made a find.
10082-8{_}1194{_}He would take it home to his wife and she would probably keep it for finger-stalls for the children."
10082-8{_}1195{_}Crewe put down his notes and got up from his chair.
10082-8{_}1196{_}"Your job is this, Joe.
10082-8{_}1197{_}Go to Riversbrook and make a careful search on both sides of the road for the missing glove.
10082-8{_}1198{_}I do not think he threw it away--if he did throw it away--until he had walked some distance, but you mustn't act on that assumption.
10082-8{_}1199{_}Look over the fences of the houses and into the hedges.
10082-8{_}1200{_}Walk along in the direction of Hampstead Underground.
10082-8{_}1201{_}Search the gutters and all the trees and hedges along the road.
10082-8{_}1202{_}Take one side of the street to the Underground station and if you do not find the glove go back to Riversbrook along the other side.
10082-8{_}1203{_}Make a thorough job of it, as it is most important that the glove should be found--if it is to be found."
10082-8{_}1204{_}After Joe had departed Crewe put on his hat and left his office for the Strand.
10082-8{_}1205{_}His first call was at the shop of Bruden and Marshall, hosiers, in order to find out if any information was to be obtained there about the ownership of the glove.
10082-8{_}1206{_}He was aware that the police had been there on the same mission, but his experience had often shown that valuable information was to be gathered after the police had been over the ground.
10082-8{_}1207{_}On introducing himself to the manager of the shop that gentleman displayed as much humble civility as he would have done towards a valued customer.
10082-8{_}1208{_}He could not say anything about the ownership of the glove which Crewe had brought, and he could not even say if it had come from their shop.
10082-8{_}1209{_}It was an excellent glove, the line being known in the trade as "first-choice reindeer."
10082-8{_}1210{_}They stocked that particular kind of article at 10/6 the pair.
10082-8{_}1211{_}They had the pleasure of having had the late Sir Horace Fewbanks on their books.
10082-8{_}1212{_}He was quite an old account, if he might use the expression.
10082-8{_}1213{_}He was one of their best customers, being a gentleman who was particular about his appearance and who would have nothing but the best in any line that he fancied.
10082-8{_}1214{_}On the subject of Sir Horace's taste in hose the manager had much to say, and, in spite of Crewe's efforts to confine the conversation to gloves, the manager repeatedly dragged in socks.
10082-8{_}1215{_}He did it so frequently that he became conscious his visitor was showing signs of annoyance, so he apologised, adding, with an inspiration, "After all, hose is really gloves for the feet."
10082-8{_}1216{_}Crewe ascertained that a large number of legal gentlemen were customers of Bruden and Marshall.
10082-8{_}1217{_}He innocently suggested that the reason was because the shop was the nearest one of its kind to the Law Courts, but this explanation offended the shopman's pride.
10082-8{_}1218{_}It was because they stocked high-class goods and gave good value in every way, combined with attention and civility and a desire to please, that they did such an excellent business with legal gentlemen.
10082-8{_}1219{_}In refutation of the idea that proximity to the Courts was the direct reason of their having so many legal gentlemen among their customers the manager declared that they received orders from all parts of the world--India, Canada, Australia, and South Africa, to say nothing of American gentlemen who liked their hosiery to have the London hall-mark.
10082-8{_}1220{_}Their orders from the Colonies came from gentlemen who found that these things in the Colonies were not what they had been used to, and so they sent their orders to Bruden and Marshall.
10082-8{_}1221{_}Crewe's interest was in the legal customers and he asked for the names of some.
10082-8{_}1222{_}The manager ran through a list of names of judges, barristers and solicitors, but the name Crewe wanted to hear was not among them.
10082-8{_}1223{_}He was compelled to include the name among half a dozen others he mentioned to the manager.
10082-8{_}1224{_}He ascertained that Mr. Charles Holymead was a customer of the firm, but it was apparent from the manager's spiritless attitude towards Mr. Holymead that the famous K.C.
10082-8{_}1225{_}was not a man who ran up a big bill with his hosier, or was very particular about what he wore.
10082-8{_}1226{_}The world regarded some of the men of this type famous or distinguished, but in the hosier's mind they were all classed as commonplace.
10082-8{_}1227{_}But the manager would not go so far as to say that Mr. Holymead would not buy such a glove as that which Crewe had brought in.
10082-8{_}1228{_}He might and he might not, but, as a general rule, he did not pay more than 8/6 for his gloves.
10082-8{_}1229{_}Crewe took a taxi to Princes Gate in order to have a look at the house in which Holymead lived.
10082-8{_}1230{_}It occurred to him that if Holymead was not particular about what he spent on his clothes he was extravagant about the amount he spent in house rent.
10082-8{_}1231{_}Of course, a leading barrister earning a huge income could afford to live in a palatial residence in Princes Gate, but it was not the locality or residence that an economically-minded man would have chosen for his home.
10082-8{_}1232{_}But Crewe had little doubt that the beautiful wife Holymead possessed was responsible for the choice of house and locality.
10082-8{_}1233{_}After looking at the house Crewe walked back to the cab-stand at Hyde Park Corner.
10082-8{_}1234{_}He had arrived at the conclusion that it was necessary to settle beyond doubt whether the K.C.
10082-8{_}1235{_}had visited Riversbrook the night Sir Horace had returned from Scotland.
10082-8{_}1236{_}If the K.C.
10082-8{_}1237{_}had done so, he was anxious to keep the visit secret, for not only had he not informed the police of his visit but he had kept it from Miss Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}1238{_}Crewe had ascertained from Miss Fewbanks that Mr. Holymead when he had called at Riversbrook on a visit of condolence had not mentioned to her anything about having left his stick in the hall stand on a previous visit.
10082-8{_}1239{_}On leaving Miss Fewbanks Mr. Holymead had gone up to the hall stand and taken both his hat and stick as if he had left them both there a few minutes before.
10082-8{_}1240{_}Crewe reasoned that if Holymead had gone out to see Sir Horace Fewbanks at Riversbrook and had desired to keep his visit a secret he would not have taken a cab at Hyde Park Corner to Hampstead, but would have travelled by underground railway or omnibus.
10082-8{_}1241{_}In all probability the Tube had been used because of its speed being more in harmony with the feelings of a man impatient to get done with a subject so important that Sir Horace had been recalled from Scotland to deal with it.
10082-8{_}1242{_}He would leave the Tube at Hampstead and take a taxi-cab.
10082-8{_}1243{_}He would not be likely to go straight to Riversbrook in the taxi-cab, if he were anxious that his movements should not be traced subsequently.
10082-8{_}1244{_}He would dismiss the taxi-cab at one of the hotels bordering on Hampstead Heath, for they were the resort of hundreds of visitors on summer nights, and his actions would thus easily escape notice.
10082-8{_}1245{_}From the hotel he would walk across to Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}1246{_}But the return journey would be made in a somewhat different way.
10082-8{_}1247{_}If Holymead left Riversbrook in a state of excitement he would walk a long way without being conscious of the exertion.
10082-8{_}1248{_}He would want to be alone with his own thoughts.
10082-8{_}1249{_}Gradually he would cool down, and becoming conscious of his surroundings would make his way home.
10082-8{_}1250{_}Again he would use the Tube, for it would be more difficult for his movements to be traced if he mixed with a crowd of travellers than if he took a cab to his home.
10082-8{_}1251{_}It was impossible to say what station he got in at, for that would depend on how far he walked before he cooled down, but he would be sure to get out at Hyde Park Corner because that was the station nearest to his house.
10082-8{_}1252{_}Allowing for a temperamental reaction during a train journey of about twenty minutes, he would feel depressed and weary and would probably take a taxi-cab outside Hyde Park station to his home.
10082-8{_}1253{_}That was a thing he would often be in the habit of doing when returning late at night from the theatre or elsewhere, and therefore could be easily explained by him if the police happened to make inquiries as to his movements.
10082-8{_}1254{_}As Crewe anticipated, he had no difficulty in finding the driver of the taxi-cab in which Holymead had driven home on the night of Wednesday last.
10082-8{_}1255{_}The K.C.
10082-8{_}1256{_}frequently used cabs, and he was well-known to all the drivers on the rank.
10082-8{_}1257{_}Crewe got into the cab he had used and ordered the man to drive him to his office, and there invited him upstairs.
10082-8{_}1258{_}He adopted this course because he knew that the driver, who gave his name as Taylor, would be more likely to talk freely in an office where he could not be overheard than he would do on the cab-rank with his fellow-drivers crowding him, or in an hotel parlour where other people were present.
10082-8{_}1259{_}"Tell me exactly what happened when you drove Mr. Holymead home on Wednesday night," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}1260{_}"Did you notice anything strange about him, or was his manner much the same as on other occasions that he used your cab?"
10082-8{_}1261{_}"Well, I don't see whether I should tell you whether he was or whether he wasn't," replied the taxi-cab driver, who was as surly as most of his class.
10082-8{_}1262{_}"What's it to do with you, anyway?
10082-8{_}1263{_}He's a regular customer of mine on the rank, and he's not one of your tuppenny tipsters, either.
10082-8{_}1264{_}He's a gentleman.
10082-8{_}1265{_}And if he got to know that I had been telling tales about him it would not do me any good."
10082-8{_}1266{_}"It would not," replied Crewe, with cordial acquiescence.
10082-8{_}1267{_}"Therefore, Taylor, I give you my word of honour not to mention anything you tell me.
10082-8{_}1268{_}Furthermore, I'll see that you don't lose by it now or at any other time.
10082-8{_}1269{_}I cannot say more than that, but that's a great deal more than the police would say.
10082-8{_}1270{_}Now, would you sooner tell me or tell the police?
10082-8{_}1271{_}Here's a sovereign to start with, and if you have an interesting story to tell you'll have another one before you leave."
10082-8{_}1272{_}The appeal of money and the conviction that the police would use less considerate methods if Crewe passed him over to them abolished Taylor's scruples about discussing a fare, and it was in a much less surly tone that he responded:  "I didn't notice anything strange about him when he called me off the rank, but I did afterwards.
10082-8{_}1273{_}First of all, I didn't drive him home.
10082-8{_}1274{_}That is, I did drive him home, but he didn't go inside.
10082-8{_}1275{_}When I drew up outside his house in Princes Gate, I looked around expecting to see him get out.
10082-8{_}1276{_}As he didn't move I got down and opened the door.
10082-8{_}1277{_}'Aren't you getting out here, sir?'
10082-8{_}1278{_}I said, in a soft voice.
10082-8{_}1279{_}'No,' he said.
10082-8{_}1280{_}'Drive on.'
10082-8{_}1281{_}'This is your house, sir,' I ventured to say.
10082-8{_}1282{_}'I'm not going in,' he replied, 'drive on.'
10082-8{_}1283{_}I was surprised.
10082-8{_}1284{_}I thought he was the worse for drink, and I'd never seen him that way before.
10082-8{_}1285{_}But some gentlemen are so obstinate in liquor that you can't get them to do anything except the opposite of what you ask them.
10082-8{_}1286{_}I thought I'd try and coax him.
10082-8{_}1287{_}'Better go inside, sir,' I said.
10082-8{_}1288{_}'You'll be better off in bed.'
10082-8{_}1289{_}'Do you think I am drunk?'
10082-8{_}1290{_}he said sharply.
10082-8{_}1291{_}You could have knocked me down with a feather.
10082-8{_}1292{_}He was as sober as a judge, all in a moment.
10082-8{_}1293{_}'No, sir, I didn't,' I said.
10082-8{_}1294{_}'I wouldn't take the liberty,' I said.
10082-8{_}1295{_}'Then get back on your seat and drive me to the Hyde Park Hotel--no, I think I'll go to Verney's.
10082-8{_}1296{_}But don't go there direct.
10082-8{_}1297{_}Drive me round the Park first.
10082-8{_}1298{_}I feel I want a breath of cool air.'"
10082-8{_}1299{_}"Go on," said Crewe, in a tone which indicated approval of Taylor's method of telling his story.
10082-8{_}1300{_}"Well, I turned the cab round and drove through the Park.
10082-8{_}1301{_}But I was puzzled about him and looked back at him once or twice pretending that I was looking to see if a cab or car was coming up behind.
10082-8{_}1302{_}And as we passed over the Serpentine Bridge I saw him throw something out of the window."
10082-8{_}1303{_}"A glove?"
10082-8{_}1304{_}suggested Crewe quickly.
10082-8{_}1305{_}The driver looked at him in profound admiration.
10082-8{_}1306{_}"Well, if you don't beat all the detectives I've ever heard of."
10082-8{_}1307{_}"He tried to throw it in the water," continued Crewe, as if explaining the matter to himself rather than to his visitor.
10082-8{_}1308{_}"Did you get it?"
10082-8{_}1309{_}"Hold on a bit," said Taylor, who had his own ideas of how to give value for the extra sovereign he hoped to obtain.
10082-8{_}1310{_}"I couldn't see what it was he had thrown away, and, of course, I couldn't pull up to find out.
10082-8{_}1311{_}I drove on, but I kept my eye on him, though I had my back to him.
10082-8{_}1312{_}As we were driving back along the Broad Walk I had another look at him, and bless me if he wasn't crying--crying like a child.
10082-8{_}1313{_}He had his hands up to his face and his head was shaking as if he was sobbing.
10082-8{_}1314{_}I said to myself, 'He's barmy--he's gone off his rocker.'
10082-8{_}1315{_}I thought to myself I ought to drive him to the police station, but I reckoned it was none of my business, after all, so I'll take him to Verney's and be done with it.
10082-8{_}1316{_}So I drove to Verney's.
10082-8{_}1317{_}He got out, and paid me, but I couldn't see that he had been crying, and he looked much as usual, so far as I could see.
10082-8{_}1318{_}I thought to myself that perhaps, after all, he'd only had a queer turn; however, I said to myself I'd drive back to the bridge and see what he'd thrown out of the window.
10082-8{_}1319{_}It _was_ a glove, sure enough.
10082-8{_}1320{_}It had fallen just below the railing.
10082-8{_}1321{_}I looked about for the other one, but I couldn't find it, so I suppose it must have fallen into the water."
10082-8{_}1322{_}"No, it didn't," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}1323{_}"I have it here."
10082-8{_}1324{_}He opened a drawer in his desk and produced a glove.
10082-8{_}1325{_}"It was a right-hand glove you found.
10082-8{_}1326{_}Just look at this one and see if it corresponds to the one you picked up."
10082-8{_}1327{_}Taylor looked at the glove.
10082-8{_}1328{_}"They're as like as two peas," he said.
10082-8{_}1329{_}"What did you do with the one you found?"
10082-8{_}1330{_}inquired Crewe.
10082-8{_}1331{_}"I hope you didn't throw it away?"
10082-8{_}1332{_}"I'm not a fool," retorted Taylor.
10082-8{_}1333{_}"I've had odd gloves left in my cab before.
10082-8{_}1334{_}I kept this one thinking that sooner or later somebody might leave another like it, and then I'd have a pair for nothing."
10082-8{_}1335{_}"Well, I'll buy it from you," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}1336{_}"Have you anything more to tell me?"
10082-8{_}1337{_}"I went back to the rank and one of the chaps was curious that I'd been so long away, for he knew that Mr. Holymead's place isn't more than ten minutes' drive from the station.
10082-8{_}1338{_}But he got nothing out of me.
10082-8{_}1339{_}I know how to keep my mouth shut.
10082-8{_}1340{_}You're the first man I've told what happened, and I hope you won't give me away."
10082-8{_}1341{_}"I've already promised you that," said Crewe, flipping another sovereign from his sovereign case and handing it to Taylor, "and I'll give you five shillings for the glove."
10082-8{_}1342{_}Taylor looked at him darkly.
10082-8{_}1343{_}"Five shillings isn't much for a glove like that," he said insolently.
10082-8{_}1344{_}"What about my loss of time going home for it?
10082-8{_}1345{_}I suppose you'll pay the taxi-fare for the run down from Hyde Park?"
10082-8{_}1346{_}"No, I won't," said Crewe cheerfully.
10082-8{_}1347{_}"Then I don't see why I should bring it for a paltry five shillings," said Taylor.
10082-8{_}1348{_}"If you want the glove you'll have to pay for it."
10082-8{_}1349{_}"But I don't want the glove," said Crewe, who disliked being made the victim of extortion.
10082-8{_}1350{_}"What made you think so?
10082-8{_}1351{_}I'll sell you this one for five shillings.
10082-8{_}1352{_}We may as well do a deal of some kind; it is no use each of us having one glove.
10082-8{_}1353{_}What do you say, Taylor?
10082-8{_}1354{_}Will you buy mine for five shillings, or shall I buy yours?"
10082-8{_}1355{_}Taylor smiled sourly.
10082-8{_}1356{_}"You're a deep one," he said.
10082-8{_}1357{_}"Here's the other glove."
10082-8{_}1358{_}He dipped his hand into the deep pocket of his driving coat and produced a glove.
10082-8{_}1359{_}"I suppose you knew I'd have it on me.
10082-8{_}1360{_}Five shillings, and it's yours."
10082-8{_}1361{_}"The pair are worth about five shillings to me," said Crewe as he paid over the money.
10082-8{_}1362{_}"Do you remember what time it was when Mr. Holymead engaged you at Hyde Park?"
10082-8{_}1363{_}"Eleven o'clock."
10082-8{_}1364{_}"You are quite sure as to the time?"
10082-8{_}1365{_}"I heard one of the big clocks striking as he was getting into my cab."
10082-8{_}1366{_}Taylor took his departure, and Crewe, after wrapping up the left-hand glove which he had to return to Inspector Chippenfield, put the other one in his safe.
10082-8{_}1367{_}"We are getting on," he said in a pleased tone.
10082-8{_}1368{_}"This means a trip to Scotland, but I'll wait until the inquest is over."
10082-8{_}1369{_}CHAPTER IX   At the inquest on the body of Sir Horace Fewbanks, which was held at the Hampstead Police Court, there was an odd mixture of classes in the crowd that thronged that portion of the court in which the public were allowed to congregate.
10082-8{_}1370{_}The accounts of the crime which had been published in the press, and the atmosphere of mystery which enshrouded the violent death of one of the most prominent of His Majesty's judges, had stirred the public curiosity, and therefore, in spite of the fact that every one was supposed to be out of town in August, the attendance at the court included a sprinkling of ladies of the fashionable world, and their escorts.
10082-8{_}1371{_}Both branches of the legal profession were numerously represented.
10082-8{_}1372{_}All of the victim's judicial colleagues were out of town, and though some of them intended as a mark of respect for the dead man to come up for the funeral, which was to take place two days later, they were too familiar with legal procedure to feel curiosity as to the working of the machinery at a preliminary inquiry into the crime.
10082-8{_}1373{_}They were emphatic among their friends on the degeneracy of these days which rendered possible such an outrageous crime as the murder of a High Court judge.
10082-8{_}1374{_}The fact that it was without precedent in the history of British law added to its enormity in the eyes of gentlemen who had been trained to worship precedent as the only safe guide through the shifting quicksands of life.
10082-8{_}1375{_}They were insistent on the urgency of the murderer being arrested and handed over to Justice in the person of the hangman, for--as each asked himself--where was this sort of crime to end?
10082-8{_}1376{_}In spite of the degeneracy of the times they were reluctant to believe in such a far-fetched supposition as the existence of a band of criminals who, in revenge for the judicial sentences imposed on members of their class, had sworn to exterminate the whole of His Majesty's judges; but, until the murderer was apprehended and the reason for the crime was discovered, it was impossible to say that the English judicature would not soon be called upon to supply other victims to criminal violence.
10082-8{_}1377{_}The murder of a judge seemed to them a particularly atrocious crime, in the punishment of which the law might honourably sacrifice temporarily its well-earned reputation for delay.
10082-8{_}1378{_}The bar was represented chiefly by junior members.
10082-8{_}1379{_}The senior members were able to make full use of the long vacation, spending it at health resorts or in the country, but the incomes of the young shoots of the great parasitical profession did not permit them to enjoy more than a brief holiday out of town.
10082-8{_}1380{_}Of course it would never have done for them to admit even to each other that they could not afford to go away for an extended holiday, and therefore they told one another in bored tones that they had not been able to make up their minds where to go.
10082-8{_}1381{_}The junior bar included old men, who, through lack of influence, want of energy, want of advertisement, want of ability, or some other deficiency, had never earned more than a few guineas at their profession, though they had spent year after year in chambers.
10082-8{_}1382{_}They lived on scanty private means.
10082-8{_}1383{_}Broken in spirit they had even ceased to attend the courts in order to study the methods and learn the tricks of successful counsel.
10082-8{_}1384{_}But the murder of a High Court judge was a thing which stirred even their sluggish blood, and in the hope of some sensational development they had put on faded silk hats and shabby black suits and gone out to Hampstead to attend the inquest.
10082-8{_}1385{_}The interest of the junior bar in the crime was as personal as that of the members of the Judicial Bench, though it manifested itself in an entirely different direction.
10082-8{_}1386{_}They speculated among themselves as to who would be appointed to the vacancy on the High Court Bench.
10082-8{_}1387{_}A leading K.C.
10082-8{_}1388{_}with a political pull would of course be selected by the Attorney-General, but there were several K.C.
10082-8{_}1389{_}'s who possessed these qualifications, and therefore there was room for differences of opinion among the junior bar as to who would get the offer.
10082-8{_}1390{_}The point on which they were all united was that vacancies of the High Court Bench were a good thing for the bar as a whole, for they removed leading K.C.
10082-8{_}1391{_}'s, and the dispersion of their practice was like rain on parched ground.
10082-8{_}1392{_}Metaphorically speaking, every one--including even the junior bar--had the chance of getting a shove up when a leading K.C.
10082-8{_}1393{_}accepted a judicial appointment.
10082-8{_}1394{_}Some of the more irreverent spirits among the junior bar, in drawing attention to the fact that Sir Horace Fewbanks had been one of the youngest members of the High Court Bench, expressed the hope that the shock of his death would be felt by some of the extremely aged members of the bench who were too infirm in health to be able to stand many shocks.
10082-8{_}1395{_}The members of the junior bar chatted with the representatives of the lower branch of the profession who ranged from articled clerks whose young souls had not been entirely dried up by association with parchment, to hard old delvers in dusty documents who had lived so long in the legal atmosphere of quibbling, obstruction, and deceit, that they were as incapable of an honest impetuous act as of an illegal one.
10082-8{_}1396{_}The gossip concerning the murdered judge in which the two branches of the profession joined had reference to his moral character in legal circles.
10082-8{_}1397{_}There had always been gossip of the kind in his life-time.
10082-8{_}1398{_}Sir Horace's judicial reputation was beyond reproach and he had known his law a great deal better than most of his judicial colleagues.
10082-8{_}1399{_}Comparatively few of his decisions had been upset on appeal.
10082-8{_}1400{_}But every one about the courts knew that he was susceptible to a pretty feminine face and a good figure.
10082-8{_}1401{_}Many were the conflicts that arose in court between bench and bar as the result of Mr. Justice Fewbanks's habit of protecting pretty witnesses from cross-examining questions which he regarded as outside the case.
10082-8{_}1402{_}There was no suggestion that his judicial decisions were influenced by the good looks of ladies who were parties to the cases heard by him, but there were rumours that on occasions the relations between the judge and a pretty witness begun in court had ripened into something at which moral men might well shake their heads.
10082-8{_}1403{_}While the members of the legal profession struggled to obtain seats in the body of the court, an entirely different class of spectators struggled to get into the gallery.
10082-8{_}1404{_}For the most part they were badly dressed men who needed a shave, but there were a few well-dressed men among them, and also a few ladies.
10082-8{_}1405{_}Detective Rolfe took a professional interest in the occupants of the gallery.
10082-8{_}1406{_}"What a collection of crooks," he whispered to Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}1407{_}"A regular rogues' gallery.
10082-8{_}1408{_}Look--there is 'Nosey George'; it is time he was in again.
10082-8{_}1409{_}And behind him is that cunning old 'drop' Ikey Samuels--I wish we could get him.
10082-8{_}1410{_}Look at the other end of the first row.
10082-8{_}1411{_}Isn't that 'Sunny Jim'?
10082-8{_}1412{_}I hardly knew him.
10082-8{_}1413{_}He's grown a beard since he's been out.
10082-8{_}1414{_}We'll soon have it off again for him.
10082-8{_}1415{_}He's got the impudence to scowl at us.
10082-8{_}1416{_}He'll lay for you one of these nights, Inspector."
10082-8{_}1417{_}The judicial duties of the murdered man had been concerned chiefly with civil cases at the Royal Courts of Justice, but when the criminal calendar had been heavy he had often presided at Number One Court at the Old Bailey.
10082-8{_}1418{_}It was this fact which had given the criminal class a sort of personal interest in his murder and accounted for the presence of many well-known criminals who happened to be out of gaol at the time.
10082-8{_}1419{_}The spectators in the gallery included men whom the murdered man had sentenced and men who had been fortunate enough to escape being sentenced by him owing to the vagaries of juries.
