10082-8{_}1420{_}There were pickpockets, sneak thieves, confidence men, burglars, and receivers among the occupants of the gallery, and many of them had brought with them the ladies who assisted them professionally or presided over their homes when they were not in gaol.
10082-8{_}1421{_}"I wouldn't be surprised if the man we want is among that bunch," said Rolfe to Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}1422{_}"You've a lot to learn about them, my boy," said his superior.
10082-8{_}1423{_}"There is Crewe up among them," continued Rolfe.
10082-8{_}1424{_}"I wonder what he thinks he's after."
10082-8{_}1425{_}Inspector Chippenfield gave a glance in the direction of Crewe, but did not deign to give any sign of recognition.
10082-8{_}1426{_}The fact that Crewe by his presence in the gallery seemed to entertain the idea that the murderer might be found among the occupants of that part of the court could not be as lightly dismissed as Rolfe's vague suggestion.
10082-8{_}1427{_}It annoyed Inspector Chippenfield to think that Crewe might be nearer at the moment to the murderer than he himself was, even though that proximity was merely physical and unsupported by evidence or even by any theory.
10082-8{_}1428{_}It would have been a great relief to him if he had known that Crewe's object in going to the gallery was not to mix with the criminal classes, but in order to keep a careful survey of what took place in the body of the court without making himself too prominent.
10082-8{_}1429{_}Mr. Holymead, K.C., arrived, and members of the junior bar deferentially made room for him.
10082-8{_}1430{_}He shook hands with some of these gentlemen and also with Inspector Chippenfield, much to the gratification of that officer.
10082-8{_}1431{_}Miss Fewbanks arrived in a taxi-cab a few minutes before the appointed hour of eleven.
10082-8{_}1432{_}She was accompanied by Mrs. Holymead, and they were shown into a private room by Police-Constable Flack, who had received instructions from Inspector Chippenfield to be on the lookout for the murdered man's daughter.
10082-8{_}1433{_}Miss Fewbanks and Mrs. Holymead had been almost inseparable since the tragedy had been discovered.
10082-8{_}1434{_}Immediately on the arrival of Miss Fewbanks from Dellmere, Mrs. Holymead had gone out to Riversbrook to condole with her, and to support her in her great sorrow.
10082-8{_}1435{_}But the murdered man's daughter, who, on account of having lived apart from her father, had developed a self-reliant spirit, seemed to be less overcome by the horror of the tragedy than Mrs. Holymead was.
10082-8{_}1436{_}It was with a feeling that there was something lacking in her own nature, that the girl realised that Mrs. Holymead's grief for the violent death of a man who had been her husband's dearest friend was greater than her own grief at the loss of a father.
10082-8{_}1437{_}One of the directions in which Mrs. Holymead's grief found expression was in a feverish desire to know all that was being done to discover the murderer.
10082-8{_}1438{_}She displayed continuous interest in the investigations of the detectives engaged on the case, and she had implored Miss Fewbanks to let her know when any important discovery was made.
10082-8{_}1439{_}She applauded the action of her young friend in engaging such a famous detective as Crewe, and declared that if anyone could unravel the mystery, Crewe would do it.
10082-8{_}1440{_}She had been particularly anxious to hear through Miss Fewbanks what Crewe's impressions were, with regard to the tragedy.
10082-8{_}1441{_}The court was opened punctually, the coroner being Mr. Bodyman, a stout, clean-shaven, white-haired gentleman who had spent thirty years of his life in the stuffy atmosphere of police courts hearing police-court cases.
10082-8{_}1442{_}Police-Inspector Seldon nodded in reply to the inquiring glance of the coroner, and the inquest was opened.
10082-8{_}1443{_}The first witness was Miss Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}1444{_}She was dressed in deep black and was obviously a little unnerved.
10082-8{_}1445{_}In a low tone she said she had identified the body as that of her father.
10082-8{_}1446{_}She was staying at her father's country house in Dellmere, Sussex, when the crime was committed.
10082-8{_}1447{_}She had no knowledge of anyone who was evilly disposed towards her father.
10082-8{_}1448{_}He had never spoken to her of anyone who cherished a grudge against him.
10082-8{_}1449{_}Evidence relating to the circumstances in which the body was found was given by Police-Constable Flack.
10082-8{_}1450{_}He described the position of the room in which the body was found, and the attitude in which the body was stretched.
10082-8{_}1451{_}He was on duty in the neighbourhood of Tanton Gardens on the night of the murder, but saw no suspicious characters and heard no sounds.
10082-8{_}1452{_}The evidence of Hill was chiefly a repetition of what he had told Inspector Chippenfield as to his movements on the day of the crime, and his methods of inspecting the premises three times a week in accordance with his master's orders.
10082-8{_}1453{_}He knew nothing about Sir Horace's sudden return from Scotland.
10082-8{_}1454{_}His first knowledge of this was the account of the murder, which he read in the papers.
10082-8{_}1455{_}Inspector Chippenfield gave evidence for the purpose of producing the letter received at Scotland Yard announcing that Sir Horace Fewbanks had been murdered.
10082-8{_}1456{_}The letter was passed up to the coroner for his inspection, and when he had examined it he sent it to the foreman of the jury.
10082-8{_}1457{_}Then followed medical evidence, which showed that death was due to a bullet wound and could not have been self-inflicted.
10082-8{_}1458{_}The coroner, in his summing-up, dwelt upon the loss sustained by the Judiciary by the violent death of one of its most distinguished members, and the jury, after a retirement of a few minutes, brought in a verdict of wilful murder by some person or persons unknown.
10082-8{_}1459{_}As the occupants of the court filed out into the street, Crewe, who was watching Holymead, noticed the K.C.
10082-8{_}1460{_}give a slight start when he saw Miss Fewbanks and his wife.
10082-8{_}1461{_}Mr. Holymead went up to the ladies and shook hands with Miss Fewbanks, and to Crewe it seemed as if he was on the point of shaking hands with his wife, but he stopped himself awkwardly.
10082-8{_}1462{_}He saw the ladies into their cab, and, raising his hat, went off.
10082-8{_}1463{_}As Mr. Holymead had seen Miss Fewbanks in court when she gave evidence, it was obvious to Crewe that he could not have been surprised at meeting her outside.
10082-8{_}1464{_}It was therefore the presence of his wife which had surprised him.
10082-8{_}1465{_}That fact--if it were a fact--opened a limitless field of speculation to Crewe, but in spite of the possibility of error--a possibility which he frankly recognised--he was pleased with himself for having noticed the incident.
10082-8{_}1466{_}To him it seemed to provide another link in the chain he was constructing.
10082-8{_}1467{_}It harmonised with Taylor's story of Mr. Holymead's decision to stay at Verney's instead of entering his own home the night Taylor drove him from Hyde Park Corner.
10082-8{_}1468{_}Rolfe also possessed the professional faculty of observation, but in a different degree.
10082-8{_}1469{_}He had seen Mr. Holymead talking to his wife and Miss Fewbanks, but he had noticed nothing but gentlemanly ease in the barrister's manner.
10082-8{_}1470{_}What did astonish him in connection with Mr. Holymead was that after he had left the ladies and was walking in the direction of the cab-rank he spoke to one of the former occupants of the gallery.
10082-8{_}1471{_}This was a man known to the police and his associates as "Kincher."
10082-8{_}1472{_}His name was Kemp, and how he had obtained his nick-name was not known.
10082-8{_}1473{_}He was a criminal by profession and had undergone several heavy sentences for burglary.
10082-8{_}1474{_}He was a thick-set man of medium height, about fifty years of age.
10082-8{_}1475{_}Apart from a rather heavy lower jaw, he gave no external indication of his professional pursuits, but looked, with his brown and weather-beaten face and rough blue reefer suit, not unlike a seafaring man.
10082-8{_}1476{_}The likeness was heightened by a tattooed device which covered the back of his right hand, and a slight roll in his gait when he walked.
10082-8{_}1477{_}But appearances are deceptive, for Mr. Kemp, at liberty or in gaol, had never been out of London in his life.
10082-8{_}1478{_}He was born and bred a London thief, and had served all his sentences at Wormwood Scrubbs.
10082-8{_}1479{_}For over a minute he and Mr. Holymead remained in conversation.
10082-8{_}1480{_}Rolfe would have described it officially as familiar conversation, but that description would have overlooked the deference, the sense of inferiority, in "Kincher's" manner.
10082-8{_}1481{_}For a time Rolfe was puzzled by the incident, but he eventually lighted on an explanation which satisfied himself.
10082-8{_}1482{_}It was that in the earlier days before Mr. Holymead had reached such a prominent position at the bar, he had been engaged in practice in the criminal courts, and "Kincher" had been one of his clients.
10082-8{_}1483{_}With a cheerful smile Holymead brought the conversation to an end and went on his way.
10082-8{_}1484{_}Kemp walked on hurriedly in the opposite direction.
10082-8{_}1485{_}He had his eyes on a young man whom he had seen in the gallery, and who had seemed to avoid his eye.
10082-8{_}1486{_}It was obvious to him that this young man, for whom he had been on the watch when Mr. Holymead spoke to him, had seized the opportunity to slip past him while he was talking to the eminent K.C.
10082-8{_}1487{_}The young man, even from the back view, seemed to be well-dressed.
10082-8{_}1488{_}"Hallo, Fred," exclaimed Mr. Kemp, as he reached within a yard or two of his quarry.
10082-8{_}1489{_}"Hallo, Kincher," replied the young man, turning round.
10082-8{_}1490{_}"I didn't notice you.
10082-8{_}1491{_}Were you up at the court?"
10082-8{_}1492{_}"Yes, I looked in," said Mr. Kemp.
10082-8{_}1493{_}"There wasn't much doing, was there?"
10082-8{_}1494{_}"No," said Fred.
10082-8{_}1495{_}"He won't trouble us any more," pursued Mr. Kemp.
10082-8{_}1496{_}"No."
10082-8{_}1497{_}The young man seemed to have a dread of helping along the conversation, and therefore sought refuge in monosyllables.
10082-8{_}1498{_}Mr. Kemp coughed before he formed his question.
10082-8{_}1499{_}"Did you go up there that night?"
10082-8{_}1500{_}"No."
10082-8{_}1501{_}The reply came instantaneously, but the young man followed it up with a look of inquiry to ascertain if his denial was believed.
10082-8{_}1502{_}"A good thing as it happened," said Mr. Kemp.
10082-8{_}1503{_}"I had nothing to do with it," said Fred, earnestly.
10082-8{_}1504{_}"I never said you had," replied Mr. Kemp.
10082-8{_}1505{_}"Nothing whatever to do with it," continued the young man with emphasis.
10082-8{_}1506{_}"That's not my sort of game."
10082-8{_}1507{_}"I'm not saying anything, Fred," replied the elder man.
10082-8{_}1508{_}"But whoever done it might have done it by accident-like."
10082-8{_}1509{_}"Accident or no accident, I had nothing to do with it, thank God."
10082-8{_}1510{_}"That is all right, Fred.
10082-8{_}1511{_}I'm not saying you know anything about it.
10082-8{_}1512{_}But even if you did you'd find I could be trusted.
10082-8{_}1513{_}I don't go blabbing round to everybody."
10082-8{_}1514{_}"I know you don't.
10082-8{_}1515{_}But as I said before I had nothing to do with it.
10082-8{_}1516{_}I didn't go there that night--I changed my mind."
10082-8{_}1517{_}"A very lucky thing then, because if they do look you up you can prove an alibi."
10082-8{_}1518{_}"Yes," said Fred, "I can prove an alibi easy enough.
10082-8{_}1519{_}But what makes you talk about them looking me up?
10082-8{_}1520{_}Why should they get into me--why should they look me up?
10082-8{_}1521{_}I've told you I didn't go there."
10082-8{_}1522{_}"That is all right, Fred," said the other, in a soothing tone.
10082-8{_}1523{_}"If that pal of yours keeps his mouth shut there is nothing to put them on your tracks.
10082-8{_}1524{_}But I don't like the looks of him.
10082-8{_}1525{_}He seems to me a bit nervous, and if they put him through the third degree he'll squeak.
10082-8{_}1526{_}That's my impression."
10082-8{_}1527{_}"If he squeaks he'll have to settle with me," said Fred.
10082-8{_}1528{_}"And he'll find there is something to pay.
10082-8{_}1529{_}If he tries to put me away I'll--I'll--I'll do him in."
10082-8{_}1530{_}"Kincher" instead of being horrified at this sentiment seemed to approve of it as the right thing to be done.
10082-8{_}1531{_}"I'd let him know if I was you, Fred," he said.
10082-8{_}1532{_}"I didn't like the look of him.
10082-8{_}1533{_}The reason I came out here to-day was to have a look at him.
10082-8{_}1534{_}And when I saw him in the box I said to myself, 'Well, I'm glad I've staked nothing on you, for it seems to me that you'll crack up if the police shake their thumb-screws in your face.'
10082-8{_}1535{_}I felt glad I hadn't accepted your invitation to make it a two-handed job, Fred.
10082-8{_}1536{_}It was the fact that some one else I'd never seen had put up the job that kept me out of it when you asked me to go with you.
10082-8{_}1537{_}A man can't be too careful--especially after he's had a long spell in 'stir,' But of course you're all right if you changed your mind and didn't go up there.
10082-8{_}1538{_}But if I was you I'd have my alibi ready.
10082-8{_}1539{_}It is no good leaving things until the police are at the door and making one up on the spur of the moment."
10082-8{_}1540{_}"Yes, I'll see about it," said Fred.
10082-8{_}1541{_}"It's a good idea."
10082-8{_}1542{_}"Come in and have a drink, Fred," said "Kincher."
10082-8{_}1543{_}"It will do you good.
10082-8{_}1544{_}It was dry work listening to them talking up there about the murder."
10082-8{_}1545{_}Fred accompanied Mr. Kemp into the bar of the hotel they reached, and the elder man, after an inquiring glance at his companion, ordered two whiskies.
10082-8{_}1546{_}"Kincher" added water to the contents of each glass, and, lifting his glass in his right hand, waited until Fred had done the same and then said:  "Well, here's luck and long life to the man that did it--whoever he is."
10082-8{_}1547{_}Fred offered no objection to this sentiment and they drained glasses.
10082-8{_}1548{_}CHAPTER X   "And so you've had no luck, Rolfe?"
10082-8{_}1549{_}Inspector Chippenfield, glancing up from his official desk in Scotland Yard, put this question in a tone of voice which suggested that the speaker had expected nothing better.
10082-8{_}1550{_}"I've seen the heads of at least half a dozen likely West End shops," Rolfe replied, "and they tell me there is nothing to indicate where the handkerchief was bought.
10082-8{_}1551{_}The scrap of lace merely shows that it was torn off a good handkerchief, but there is nothing about it to show that the handkerchief was different in any marked way from the average filmy scrap of muslin and lace which every smart woman carries as a handkerchief.
10082-8{_}1552{_}I thought so myself, before I started to make inquiries."
10082-8{_}1553{_}"Well, Rolfe, we must come at it another way," said the inspector.
10082-8{_}1554{_}"Undoubtedly there is a woman in the case, and it ought not to be impossible to locate her.
10082-8{_}1555{_}Your theory, Rolfe, is that the murder was committed by some one who broke into the place while Sir Horace was entertaining a lady friend or waiting for the arrival of a lady he expected.
10082-8{_}1556{_}Either the lady had not arrived or had left the room temporarily when the burglar broke into the house.
10082-8{_}1557{_}He had spotted the place some days before and ascertained that it was empty, and when he found that Sir Horace had returned alone he decided to break in, and, covering Sir Horace with a revolver, try to extort money from him.
10082-8{_}1558{_}A riskier but more profitable game than burgling an empty house--if it came off.
10082-8{_}1559{_}With his revolver in his hand he made his way up to the library.
10082-8{_}1560{_}Sir Horace parleyed with him until he could reach his own revolver, and then got in the first shot but missed his man.
10082-8{_}1561{_}The burglar shot him and then bolted.
10082-8{_}1562{_}The lady heard the shots, and, rushing in, found Sir Horace in his death agony.
10082-8{_}1563{_}She was stooping over him with her handkerchief in her hand, and in his convulsive moments he caught hold of a corner of it and the handkerchief was torn.
10082-8{_}1564{_}The lady left the place and on arrival home concocted that letter which was sent here telling us that Sir Horace had been murdered.
10082-8{_}1565{_}Is that it?"
10082-8{_}1566{_}"Yes," assented Rolfe.
10082-8{_}1567{_}"Of course, I don't lay it down that everything happened just as you've said.
10082-8{_}1568{_}But that's my idea of the crime.
10082-8{_}1569{_}It accounts for all the clues we've picked up, and that is something."
10082-8{_}1570{_}"It is an ingenious theory and it does you credit," said the inspector, who had not forgotten that he had proposed to Rolfe that they should help one another to the extent of taking one another fully into each other's confidence, for the purpose of getting ahead of Crewe.
10082-8{_}1571{_}"But you have overlooked the fact that it is possible to account in another way for all the clues we have picked up.
10082-8{_}1572{_}Suppose Sir Horace's return from Scotland was due to a message from a lady friend; suppose the lady went to see him accompanied by a friend whom Sir Horace did not like--a friend of whom Sir Horace was jealous.
10082-8{_}1573{_}Suppose they asked for money--blackmail--and there was a quarrel in which Sir Horace was shot.
10082-8{_}1574{_}Then we have your idea as to how the lady's handkerchief was torn--I agree with that in the main.
10082-8{_}1575{_}The lady and her friend fled from the place.
10082-8{_}1576{_}Later in the night the place is burgled by some one who has had his eye on it for some time, and on entering the library he is astounded to find the dead body of the owner.
10082-8{_}1577{_}Suppose he went home, and on thinking things over sent the letter to Scotland Yard with the idea that if the police got on to his tracks about the burglary the fact that he had told us about the murder would show he had nothing to do with killing Sir Horace."
10082-8{_}1578{_}"That is a good theory, too," said Rolfe, in a meditative tone.
10082-8{_}1579{_}"And the only person who can tell us which is the right one is Sir Horace's lady friend.
10082-8{_}1580{_}The problem is to find her."
10082-8{_}1581{_}"Right," said the inspector approvingly.
10082-8{_}1582{_}"And while you have been making inquiries at the shops about the handkerchief I have been down to the Law Courts branch of the Equity Bank where Sir Horace kept his account.
10082-8{_}1583{_}It occurred to me that a look at Sir Horace's account might help us.
10082-8{_}1584{_}You know the sort of man he was--you know his weakness for the ladies.
10082-8{_}1585{_}But he was careful.
10082-8{_}1586{_}I looked through his private papers out at Riversbrook expecting to get on the track of something that would show some one had been trying to blackmail him over an entanglement with a woman, but I found nothing.
10082-8{_}1587{_}I couldn't even find any feminine correspondence.
10082-8{_}1588{_}If Sir Horace was in the habit of getting letters from ladies he was also in the habit of destroying them.
10082-8{_}1589{_}No doubt he adopted that precaution when his wife was alive, and found it such a wise one that he kept it up when there was less need for it.
10082-8{_}1590{_}But a weakness for the ladies costs money, Rolfe, as you know, and that is why I had a look at his banking account.
10082-8{_}1591{_}He made some payments that it would be worth while to trace--payments to West End drapers and that sort of thing.
10082-8{_}1592{_}Of course, Sir Horace, being a cautious man and occupying a public position, might not care to flaunt his weakness in the eyes of West End shopkeepers, and instead of paying the accounts of his lady friend of the moment, may have given her the money and trusted to her paying the bills--a thing that women of that kind are never in a hurry to do.
10082-8{_}1593{_}In that case the payments to West End shopkeepers are for goods supplied to his daughter.
10082-8{_}1594{_}However, I've taken a note of the names, dates, and amounts of a number of them, and I want you to see the managers of these shops."
10082-8{_}1595{_}"We are getting close to it now," said Rolfe, approvingly.
10082-8{_}1596{_}"I think so," was the modest reply of his superior.
10082-8{_}1597{_}"There is one thing about Sir Horace's account which struck me as peculiar.
10082-8{_}1598{_}Every four weeks for the past eight months Sir Horace drew a cheque for 24, and every cheque of the kind was made payable to Number 365.
10082-8{_}1599{_}Now, unless he wished to hide the nature of the transaction from his bankers, why not put in the cheque in the name of the person who received the money?
10082-8{_}1600{_}It couldn't have been for his personal use, for in that case he would have made the cheques payable to self.
10082-8{_}1601{_}Besides, a man with a banking account doesn't draw a regular 24 every four weeks for personal expenses.
10082-8{_}1602{_}He draws a cheque just when he wants a few pounds, instead of carrying five-pound notes about with him.
10082-8{_}1603{_}I asked the bank manager about these cheques and he looked up a couple of them and found they had been cashed over the counter.
10082-8{_}1604{_}So he called up the cashier and from him I learnt that Sir Horace came in and cashed them.
10082-8{_}1605{_}As far as he can remember Sir Horace cashed all these 24 cheques.
10082-8{_}1606{_}I assume he did so because he realised that there was less likely to be comment in the bank than if a well-dressed good-looking young lady arrived at the bank with them.
10082-8{_}1607{_}This 24 a month suggests that Sir Horace had something choice and not too expensive stowed away in a flat.
10082-8{_}1608{_}That is a matter on which Hill ought to be able to throw some light.
10082-8{_}1609{_}If he knows anything I'll get it out of him.
10082-8{_}1610{_}It struck me as extraordinary that Sir Horace should have taken Hill into his service knowing what he was.
10082-8{_}1611{_}But this, apparently, is the explanation.
10082-8{_}1612{_}He knew that Hill wouldn't gossip about him for fear of being exposed, for that would mean that Hill would lose his situation and would find it impossible to get another one without a reference from him.
10082-8{_}1613{_}We'll have Hill brought here--"  There was a knock at the door, and a boy in buttons entered and handed Inspector Chippenfield a card.
10082-8{_}1614{_}"Seldon from Hampstead," he explained to Rolfe.
10082-8{_}1615{_}"Don't go away yet.
10082-8{_}1616{_}It may be something about this case."
10082-8{_}1617{_}Police-Inspector Seldon entered the office, and held the door ajar for a man behind him.
10082-8{_}1618{_}He shook hands with Inspector Chippenfield and Rolfe, and then motioned his companion to a chair.
10082-8{_}1619{_}"This is Mr. Robert Evans, the landlord of the Flowerdew Hotel, Covent Garden," he explained.
10082-8{_}1620{_}He looked at Mr. Evans with the air of a police-court inspector waiting for a witness to corroborate his statement, but as that gentleman remained silent he sharply asked, "Isn't that so?"
10082-8{_}1621{_}"Quite right," said Mr. Evans, in a moist, husky voice.
10082-8{_}1622{_}He was a short fat man, with an extremely red face and bulging eyes, which watered very much and apparently required to be constantly mopped with a handkerchief which he carried in his hand.
10082-8{_}1623{_}This peculiarity gave Mr. Evans the appearance of a man perpetually in mourning, and this effect was heightened by a species of incipient palsy which had seized on his lower facial muscles, and caused his lips to tremble violently.
10082-8{_}1624{_}He was bald in the front of the head but not on the top.
10082-8{_}1625{_}The baldness over the temples had joined hands and left isolated over the centre of the forehead a small tuft of hair, which, with the playfulness of second childhood, showed a tendency to curl.
10082-8{_}1626{_}"Yes, you're quite right," he repeated huskily, as though some one had doubted the statement.
10082-8{_}1627{_}"Evans is my name and I'm not ashamed of it."
10082-8{_}1628{_}"He came to me this morning and told me that Hill gave false evidence at the inquest yesterday," Inspector Seldon explained.
10082-8{_}1629{_}"So I brought him along to see you."
10082-8{_}1630{_}"False evidence--Hill?"
10082-8{_}1631{_}exclaimed Inspector Chippenfield, with keen interest.
10082-8{_}1632{_}"Let us hear about it."
10082-8{_}1633{_}"Well, you will remember Hill said he was at home on the night of the murder," pursued Inspector Seldon.
10082-8{_}1634{_}"I looked up his depositions before I came away and what he said was this: 'I took my daughter to the Zoo in the afternoon.
10082-8{_}1635{_}We left the Zoo at half past five and went home and had tea.
10082-8{_}1636{_}My wife then took the child to the picture-palace and I remained at home.
10082-8{_}1637{_}I did not go out that night.
10082-8{_}1638{_}They returned about half-past ten, and after supper we all went to bed.'
10082-8{_}1639{_}But Evans tells me he saw Hill in his bar at three o'clock on the morning of the 19th of August.
10082-8{_}1640{_}He has an early license for the accommodation of the Covent Garden traffic.
10082-8{_}1641{_}He can swear to Hill.
10082-8{_}1642{_}A man who goes to bed at half-past ten has no right to be wandering about Covent Garden at 3 a. m. And besides, Hill told us nothing about this.
10082-8{_}1643{_}So I brought Evans along to see what you make of it."
10082-8{_}1644{_}Inspector Chippenfield had taken up a pencil and was making a few notes.
10082-8{_}1645{_}"Very interesting indeed," he said.
10082-8{_}1646{_}Then he turned to Evans and asked, "Are you sure you saw Hill in your bar at three a. m.?
10082-8{_}1647{_}There is no possibility of a mistake?"
10082-8{_}1648{_}"He is the man who was knocked down outside by a porter running into him," said Mr. Evans, mopping his eyes.
10082-8{_}1649{_}"I could bring half a dozen witnesses who will swear to him."
10082-8{_}1650{_}"You see, it's this way," interpolated Inspector Seldon, taking up the landlord's narrative.
10082-8{_}1651{_}His police-court training had taught him to bring out the salient points of a story, and he was naturally of the opinion that he could tell another man's story better than the man could tell it himself.
10082-8{_}1652{_}"Hill was staring about him--it was probably the first time he had been to Covent Garden in the early morning--and got knocked over.
10082-8{_}1653{_}He was stunned, and some porters took him in to the bar, sat him on a form, and poured some rum into him.
10082-8{_}1654{_}Some of the porters were for ringing up the ambulance; others were for carrying Hill off to the hospital, but he soon recovered.
10082-8{_}1655{_}However, he sat there for about twenty minutes, and after having several drinks at his own expense he went away.
10082-8{_}1656{_}Evans served him with the drinks."
10082-8{_}1657{_}"Good," said Inspector Chippenfield, who liked the circumstantial details of the story.
10082-8{_}1658{_}"And you can get half a dozen porters to identify him?"
10082-8{_}1659{_}"Bill Cribb, Harry Winch, Charlie Brown, a fellow they call 'Green Violets'--I don't know his real name--"  Mr. Evans was calling on his memory for further names but was stopped by Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}1660{_}"That will do very well.
10082-8{_}1661{_}And how did you happen to be at the inquest at Hampstead?
10082-8{_}1662{_}That is a bit out of your way."
10082-8{_}1663{_}Mr. Evans mopped his eyes, and Inspector Seldon took upon himself to reply for him.
10082-8{_}1664{_}"He has a brother-in-law in the trade at Hampstead--keeps the _Three Jugs_ in Coulter Street.
10082-8{_}1665{_}Evans had to go out to see his brother-in-law on business, and his brother-in-law took him along to the court out of curiosity."
10082-8{_}1666{_}Inspector Chippenfield nodded.
10082-8{_}1667{_}"Rolfe," he said, "take down Mr. Evans's statement outside and get him to sign it.
10082-8{_}1668{_}Don't go away when you've finished.
10082-8{_}1669{_}I want you."
10082-8{_}1670{_}Mr. Evans, even if he felt that full justice had been done to his story by Inspector Seldon, was disappointed at the police officer's failure to do justice to his manly scruples in coming forward to give evidence against a man who had never done him any harm.
10082-8{_}1671{_}Addressing Inspector Chippenfield he said:  "I don't altogether like mixing myself up in this business.
10082-8{_}1672{_}That isn't my way.
10082-8{_}1673{_}If I have a thing to say to a man I like to say it to his face.
10082-8{_}1674{_}I don't like a man to say things behind a man's back, that is, if he calls himself a man.
10082-8{_}1675{_}But I thought over this thing after leaving the court and hearing this chap Hill say he hadn't left home that night, and I talked it over with my wife--"  "You did the right thing," said Inspector Chippenfield, with the emphasis of a man who had profited by the triumph of right.
10082-8{_}1676{_}Mr. Evans was under the impression that the inspector's approval referred chiefly to the part he had played as a husband in talking over his perplexity with his wife, rather than the part he had played as a man in revealing that Hill had lied in his evidence.
10082-8{_}1677{_}"I always do," he said.
10082-8{_}1678{_}"My wife's one of the sensible sort, and when a man takes her advice he don't go far wrong.
10082-8{_}1679{_}She advised me to go straight to the police-station and tell them all I know.
10082-8{_}1680{_}'It is a cruel murder,' she said, 'and who knows but it might be our turn next?'"
10082-8{_}1681{_}This example of the imaginative element in feminine logic made no impression on the practical official who listened to the admiring husband.
10082-8{_}1682{_}"That is all right," said Inspector Chippenfield soothingly.
10082-8{_}1683{_}"I understand your scruples.
10082-8{_}1684{_}They do you credit.
10082-8{_}1685{_}But an honest man like you doesn't want to shield a criminal from justice--least of all a cold-blooded murderer."
10082-8{_}1686{_}When Rolfe returned to his superior with Evans's signed statement in his hand, he found the inspector preparing to leave the office.
10082-8{_}1687{_}"Put on your hat and come with me," said the inspector.
10082-8{_}1688{_}"We will go out and see Mrs. Hill.
10082-8{_}1689{_}I'll frighten the truth out of her and then tackle Hill.
10082-8{_}1690{_}He is sure to be up at Riversbrook, and we can go on there from Camden Town."
10082-8{_}1691{_}While on the way to Camden Town by Tube, Inspector Chippenfield arranged his plans with the object of saving time.
10082-8{_}1692{_}He would interview Mrs. Hill and while he was doing so Rolfe could make inquiries at the neighbouring hotels about Hill.
10082-8{_}1693{_}It was the inspector's conviction that a man who had anything to do with a murder would require a steady supply of stimulants next day.
10082-8{_}1694{_}Mrs. Hill kept a small confectionery shop adjoining a cinema theatre to supplement her husband's wages by a little earnings of her own in order to support her child.
10082-8{_}1695{_}Although the shop was an unpretentious one, and catered mainly for the ha'p'orths of the juvenile patrons of the picture house next door, it was called "The Camden Town Confectionery Emporium," and the title was printed over the little shop in large letters.
10082-8{_}1696{_}Inspector Chippenfield walked into the empty shop, and rapped sharply on the counter.
10082-8{_}1697{_}A little thin woman, with prematurely grey hair, and a depressed expression, appeared from the back in response to the summons.
10082-8{_}1698{_}She started nervously as her eye encountered the police uniform, but she waited to be spoken to.
10082-8{_}1699{_}"Is your name Hill?"
10082-8{_}1700{_}asked the inspector sternly.
10082-8{_}1701{_}"Mrs. Emily Hill?"
10082-8{_}1702{_}The woman nodded feebly, her frightened eyes fixed on the inspector's face.
10082-8{_}1703{_}"Then I want to have a word with you," continued the inspector, walking through the shop into the parlour.
10082-8{_}1704{_}"Come in here and answer my questions."
10082-8{_}1705{_}Mrs. Hill followed him timidly into the room he had entered.
10082-8{_}1706{_}It was a small, shabbily-furnished apartment, and the inspector's massive proportions made it look smaller still.
10082-8{_}1707{_}He took up a commanding position on the strip of drugget which did duty as a hearth-rug, and staring fiercely at her, suddenly commenced:  "Mrs. Hill, where was your husband on the night of the 18th of August, when his employer, Sir Horace Fewbanks, was murdered?"
10082-8{_}1708{_}Mrs. Hill shrank before that fierce gaze, and said, in a low tone:  "Please, sir, he was at home."
10082-8{_}1709{_}"At home, was he?
10082-8{_}1710{_}I'm not so sure of that.
10082-8{_}1711{_}Tell me all about your husband's movements on that day and night.
10082-8{_}1712{_}What time did he come home, to begin with?"
10082-8{_}1713{_}"He came home early in the afternoon to take our little girl to the Zoo--which was a treat she had been looking forward to for a long while.
10082-8{_}1714{_}I couldn't go myself, there being the shop to look after.
10082-8{_}1715{_}So Mr. Hill and Daphne went to the Zoo, and after they came home and had tea I took her to the pictures while Mr. Hill minded the shop.
10082-8{_}1716{_}It was not the picture-palace next door, but the big one in High Street, where they were showing 'East Lynne,' Then when we come home about ten o'clock we all had supper and went to bed."
10082-8{_}1717{_}"And your husband didn't go out again?"
10082-8{_}1718{_}"No, sir.
10082-8{_}1719{_}When I got up in the morning to bring him a cup of tea he was still sound asleep."
10082-8{_}1720{_}"But might he not have gone out in the night while you were asleep?"
10082-8{_}1721{_}"No, sir.
10082-8{_}1722{_}I'm a very light sleeper, and I wake at the least stir."
10082-8{_}1723{_}Mrs. Hill's story seemed to ring true enough, although she kept her eyes fixed on her interrogator with a kind of frightened brightness.
10082-8{_}1724{_}Inspector Chippenfield looked at her in silence for a few seconds.
10082-8{_}1725{_}"So that's the whole truth, is it?"
10082-8{_}1726{_}he said at length.
10082-8{_}1727{_}"Yes, sir," the woman earnestly assured him.
10082-8{_}1728{_}"You can ask Mr. Hill and he'll tell you the same thing."
10082-8{_}1729{_}Something reminiscent in Inspector Chippenfield's mind responded to this sentence.
10082-8{_}1730{_}He pondered over it for a moment, and then remembered that Hill had applied the same phrase to his wife.
10082-8{_}1731{_}Evidently there had been collusion, a comparing of tales beforehand.
10082-8{_}1732{_}The woman had been tutored by her cunning scoundrel of a husband, but undoubtedly her tale was false.
10082-8{_}1733{_}"The whole truth?"
10082-8{_}1734{_}said the inspector, again.
10082-8{_}1735{_}"Yes, sir," answered Mrs. Hill.
10082-8{_}1736{_}"Now, look here," said the police officer, in his sternest tones, as he shook a warning finger at the little woman, "I know you are lying.
10082-8{_}1737{_}I know Hill didn't sleep in the house, that night.
10082-8{_}1738{_}He was seen near Riversbrook in the early part of the night and he was seen wandering about Covent Garden after the murder had been committed.
10082-8{_}1739{_}It is no use lying to me, Mrs. Hill.
10082-8{_}1740{_}If you want to save your husband from being arrested for this murder you'll tell the truth.
10082-8{_}1741{_}What time did he leave here that night?"
10082-8{_}1742{_}"I've already told you the truth, sir," replied the little woman.
10082-8{_}1743{_}"He didn't leave the place after he came back from the Zoo."
10082-8{_}1744{_}Inspector Chippenfield was puzzled.
10082-8{_}1745{_}It seemed to him that Mrs. Hill was a woman of weak character, and yet she stuck firmly to her story.
10082-8{_}1746{_}Perhaps Evans had made a mistake in identifying Hill as the man who had been carried into his bar after being knocked down.
10082-8{_}1747{_}Nothing was more common than mistakes of identification.
10082-8{_}1748{_}His glance wandered round the room, as though in search of some inspiration for his next question.
10082-8{_}1749{_}His eye took mechanical note of the trumpery articles of rickety furniture; wandered over the cheap almanac prints which adorned the walls; but became riveted in the cheap overmantel which surmounted the fire-place.
10082-8{_}1750{_}For, in the slip of mirror which formed the centre of that ornament, Inspector Chippenfield caught sight of the features of Mrs. Hill frowning and shaking her head at somebody invisible.

10082-8{_}1752{_}Following the direction indicated by the mirror, Inspector Chippenfield saw Mrs. Hill had been signalling through a window which looked into the back yard.
10082-8{_}1753{_}He reached it in a step and threw open the window.
10082-8{_}1754{_}A small and not over-clean little girl was just leaving the yard by the gate.
10082-8{_}1755{_}Inspector Chippenfield called to her pleasantly, and she retraced her steps with a frightened face.
10082-8{_}1756{_}"Come in, my dear, I want you," said the inspector, wreathing his red face into a smile.
10082-8{_}1757{_}"I'm fond of little girls."
10082-8{_}1758{_}The little girl smiled, nodded her head, and presently appeared in response to the inspector's invitation.
10082-8{_}1759{_}He glanced at Mrs. Hill, noticed that her face was grey and drawn with sudden terror.
10082-8{_}1760{_}She opened her mouth as though to speak, but no words came.
10082-8{_}1761{_}The inspector lifted the child on to his knee.
10082-8{_}1762{_}She nestled to him confidingly enough, and looked up into his face with an artless glance.
10082-8{_}1763{_}"What is your name, my dear?"
10082-8{_}1764{_}"Daphne, sir--Daphne Hill."
10082-8{_}1765{_}"How old are you, Daphne?"
10082-8{_}1766{_}"Please, sir, I'm eight next birthday."
10082-8{_}1767{_}"Why, you're quite a big girl, Daphne!

10082-8{_}1769{_}"Oh, yes, sir.
10082-8{_}1770{_}I'm in the second form."
10082-8{_}1771{_}"Do you like going to school, Daphne?"
10082-8{_}1772{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}1773{_}"I suppose you like going to the Zoo better?
10082-8{_}1774{_}Did you like going with father the other day?"
10082-8{_}1775{_}The child's eyes sparkled with retrospective pleasure.

10082-8{_}1779{_}"That was splendid, Daphne!
10082-8{_}1780{_}Which did you like best--the Zoo or the pictures?"
10082-8{_}1781{_}"I liked them both," she replied.
10082-8{_}1782{_}"Was Father at home when you came home from the pictures?"
10082-8{_}1783{_}"No," said the little girl innocently.
10082-8{_}1784{_}"He was out."
10082-8{_}1785{_}Mrs. Hill, standing a little way off with fear on her face, uttered an inarticulate noise, and took a step towards the inspector and her daughter.
10082-8{_}1786{_}"Better not interfere, Mrs. Hill, unless you want to make matters worse," said the inspector meaningly.
10082-8{_}1787{_}"Now, tell me, Daphne, dear, when did your father come home?"
10082-8{_}1788{_}"Not till morning," replied the little girl, with a timid glance at her mother.
10082-8{_}1789{_}"How do you know that?"
10082-8{_}1790{_}"Because I slept in Mother's bed that night with Mother, like I always do when Father is away, but Father came home in the morning and lifted me into my own bed, because he said he wanted to go to bed."
10082-8{_}1791{_}"What time was that, Daphne?"
10082-8{_}1792{_}"I don't know, sir."
10082-8{_}1793{_}"It was light, Daphne?
10082-8{_}1794{_}You could see?"
10082-8{_}1795{_}"Oh, yes, sir."
10082-8{_}1796{_}Inspector Chippenfield told the child she was a good girl, and gave her sixpence.
10082-8{_}1797{_}The little one slipped off his knee and ran across to her mother with delight, to show the coin; all unconscious that she had betrayed her father.
10082-8{_}1798{_}The mother pushed the child from her with a heart-broken gesture.
10082-8{_}1799{_}A heavy step was heard in the shop, and the inspector, looking through the window, saw Rolfe.
10082-8{_}1800{_}He opened the door leading from the shop and beckoned his subordinate in.
10082-8{_}1801{_}Rolfe was excited, and looked like a man burdened with weighty news.
10082-8{_}1802{_}He whispered a word in Inspector Chippenfield's ear.
10082-8{_}1803{_}"Let's go into the shop," said Inspector Chippenfield promptly.
10082-8{_}1804{_}"But, first, I'll make things safe here."
10082-8{_}1805{_}He locked the door leading to the kitchen, put the key into his pocket, and followed his colleague into the shop.
10082-8{_}1806{_}"Now, Rolfe, what is it?"
10082-8{_}1807{_}"I've found out that Hill put in nearly the whole day after the murder drinking in a wine tavern.
10082-8{_}1808{_}He sat there like a man in a dream and spoke to nobody.
10082-8{_}1809{_}The only thing he took any interest in was the evening papers.
10082-8{_}1810{_}He bought about a dozen of them during the afternoon."
10082-8{_}1811{_}"Where was this?"
10082-8{_}1812{_}asked the inspector.

10082-8{_}1814{_}The man who keeps the place gave me a good description of him, though.
10082-8{_}1815{_}Hill went there about ten o'clock in the morning, and started drinking port wine, and as fast as the evening papers came out he sent the boy out for them, glanced through them, and then crumpled them up.
10082-8{_}1816{_}He stayed there till after five o'clock.
10082-8{_}1817{_}By that time the 6.30 editions would reach Camden Town, and if you remember it was the six-thirty editions which had the first news of the murder.
10082-8{_}1818{_}The tavern-keeper declares that Hill drank nearly two bottles of Tarragona port, in threepenny glasses, during the day."
10082-8{_}1819{_}"I should have credited Hill with a better taste in port, with his opportunities as Sir Horace Fewbanks's butler," said Inspector Chippenfield drily.
10082-8{_}1820{_}"What you have found out, Rolfe, only goes to bear out my own discovery that Hill is deeply implicated in this affair.
10082-8{_}1821{_}I have found out, for my part, that Hill did not spend the night of the murder at home here."
10082-8{_}1822{_}There was a ring of triumph in Inspector Chippenfield's voice as he announced this discovery, but before Rolfe could make any comment upon it there was a quick step behind them, and both men turned, to see Hill.
10082-8{_}1823{_}The butler was astonished at finding the two police officers in his wife's shop.
10082-8{_}1824{_}He hesitated, and apparently his first impulse was to turn into the street again; but, realising the futility of such a course, he came forward with an attempt to smooth his worried face into a conciliatory smile.
10082-8{_}1825{_}"Hill!"
10082-8{_}1826{_}said Inspector Chippenfield sternly.
10082-8{_}1827{_}"Once and for all, will you own up where you were on the night of the murder?"
10082-8{_}1828{_}Hill started slightly, then, with admirable self-command, he recovered himself and became as tight-lipped and reticent as ever.
10082-8{_}1829{_}"I've already told you, sir," he replied smoothly.
10082-8{_}1830{_}"I spent it in my own home.
10082-8{_}1831{_}If you ask my wife, sir, she'll tell you I never stirred out of the house after I came back from taking my little girl to the Zoo."
10082-8{_}1832{_}"I know she will, you scoundrel!"
10082-8{_}1833{_}burst out the choleric inspector.
10082-8{_}1834{_}"She's been well tutored by you, and she tells the tale very well.
10082-8{_}1835{_}But it's no good, Hill.
10082-8{_}1836{_}You forgot to tutor your little daughter, and she's innocently put you away.
10082-8{_}1837{_}What's more, you were seen in London before daybreak the night after the murder.
10082-8{_}1838{_}The game's up, my man."
10082-8{_}1839{_}Inspector Chippenfield produced a pair of handcuffs as he spoke.
10082-8{_}1840{_}Hill passed his tongue over his dry lips before he was able to speak.
10082-8{_}1841{_}"Don't put them on me," he said imploringly, as Inspector Chippenfield advanced towards him.
10082-8{_}1842{_}"I'll--I'll confess!"
10082-8{_}1843{_}CHAPTER XI   Inspector Chippenfield's first words were a warning.
10082-8{_}1844{_}"You know what you are saying, Hill?"
10082-8{_}1845{_}he asked.
10082-8{_}1846{_}"You know what this means?
10082-8{_}1847{_}Any statement you make may be used in evidence against you at your trial."
10082-8{_}1848{_}"I'll tell you everything," faltered Hill.
10082-8{_}1849{_}The impassive mask of the well-trained English servant had dropped from him, and he stood revealed as a trembling elderly man with furtive eyes, and a painfully shaken manner.
10082-8{_}1850{_}"I'll be glad to tell you everything," he declared, laying a twitching hand on the inspector's coat.
10082-8{_}1851{_}"I've not had a minute's peace or rest since--since it happened."
10082-8{_}1852{_}The dry official manner in which Inspector Chippenfield produced a note-book was in striking contrast to the trapped man's attitude.
10082-8{_}1853{_}"Go ahead," he commanded, wetting his pencil between his lips.
10082-8{_}1854{_}Before Hill could respond a small boy entered the shop--a ragged, shock-headed dirty urchin, bareheaded and barefooted.
10082-8{_}1855{_}He tapped loudly on the counter with a halfpenny.
10082-8{_}1856{_}"What do you want, boy?"
10082-8{_}1857{_}roughly asked the inspector.
10082-8{_}1858{_}"A 'a'porth of blackboys," responded the child, in the confident tone of a regular customer.
10082-8{_}1859{_}"If you'll permit me, sir, I'll serve him," said Hill and he glided behind the little counter, took some black sticky sweetmeats from one of the glass jars on the shelf and gave them to the boy, who popped one in his mouth and scurried off.
10082-8{_}1860{_}"I think we had better go inside and hear what Hill has to say, Inspector, while Mrs. Hill minds the shop," said Rolfe.
10082-8{_}1861{_}He had caught a glimpse of Mrs. Hill's white frightened face peering through the dirty little glass pane in the parlour door.
10082-8{_}1862{_}Inspector Chippenfield approved of the idea.
10082-8{_}1863{_}"We don't want to spoil your wife's business, Hill--she's likely to need it," he said, with cruel official banter.
10082-8{_}1864{_}"Come here, Mrs. Hill," he said, raising his voice.
10082-8{_}1865{_}The faded little woman appeared in response to the summons, bringing the child with her.
10082-8{_}1866{_}She shot a frightened glance at her husband, which Inspector Chippenfield intercepted.
10082-8{_}1867{_}"Never mind looking at your husband, Mrs. Hill," he said roughly.
10082-8{_}1868{_}"You've done your best for him, and the only thing to be told now is the truth.
10082-8{_}1869{_}Now you and your daughter can stay in the shop.
10082-8{_}1870{_}We want your husband inside."
10082-8{_}1871{_}Mrs. Hill clasped her hands quickly.
10082-8{_}1872{_}"Oh, what is it, Henry?"
10082-8{_}1873{_}she said.
10082-8{_}1874{_}"Tell me what has happened?
10082-8{_}1875{_}What have they found out?"
10082-8{_}1876{_}"Keep your mouth shut," commanded her husband harshly.
10082-8{_}1877{_}"This way, sir, if you please."
10082-8{_}1878{_}Inspector Chippenfield and Rolfe followed him into the parlour.
10082-8{_}1879{_}"Now, Hill," impatiently said Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}1880{_}The butler raised his head wearily.
10082-8{_}1881{_}"I suppose I may as well begin at the beginning and tell you everything," he said.
10082-8{_}1882{_}"Yes," replied the inspector, "it's not much use keeping anything back now."
10082-8{_}1883{_}"Oh, it's not a case of keeping anything back," replied Hill.
10082-8{_}1884{_}"You're too clever for me, and I've made up my mind to tell you everything, but I thought I might be able to cut the first part short, so as to save your time.
10082-8{_}1885{_}But so that you'll understand everything I've got to go a long way back--shortly after I entered Sir Horace Fewbanks's service.
10082-8{_}1886{_}In fact, I hadn't been long with him before I began to see he was leading a strange life--a double life, if I may say so.
10082-8{_}1887{_}A servant in a gentleman's house--particularly one in my position--sees a good deal he is not meant to see; in fact, he couldn't close his eyes to it if he wanted to, as no doubt you, from your experience, sir, know very well.
10082-8{_}1888{_}A confidential servant sees and hears a lot of things, sir."
10082-8{_}1889{_}Inspector Chippenfield nodded his head sharply, but he did not speak.
10082-8{_}1890{_}"I think Sir Horace trusted me, too," continued Hill humbly, "more than he would have trusted most servants, on account of my--my past.
10082-8{_}1891{_}I fancy, if I may say so, that he counted on my gratitude because he had given me a fresh start in life.
10082-8{_}1892{_}And he was quite right--at first."
10082-8{_}1893{_}Hill dropped his voice and looked down as he uttered the last two words.
10082-8{_}1894{_}"I'd have done anything for him.
10082-8{_}1895{_}But as I was saying, sir, I hadn't been long in his house before I found out that he had a--a weakness--" Hill timidly bowed his head as though apologising to the dead judge for assailing his character--"a weakness for--for the ladies.
10082-8{_}1896{_}Sometimes Sir Horace went off for the week-end without saying where he was going and sometimes he went out late at night and didn't return till after breakfast.
10082-8{_}1897{_}Then he had ladies visiting him at Riversbrook--not real ladies, if you understand, sir.
10082-8{_}1898{_}Sometimes there was a small party of them, and then they made a noise singing music-hall songs and drinking wine, but generally they came alone.
10082-8{_}1899{_}Towards the end there was one who came a lot oftener than the others.
10082-8{_}1900{_}I found out afterwards that her name was Fanning--Doris Fanning.
10082-8{_}1901{_}She was a very pretty young woman, and Sir Horace seemed very fond of her.
10082-8{_}1902{_}I knew that because I've heard him talking to her in the library.
10082-8{_}1903{_}Sir Horace had rather a loud voice, and I couldn't help overhearing him sometimes, when I took things to his rooms.
10082-8{_}1904{_}"One night,--it was before Sir Horace left for Scotland--a rainy gusty night, this young woman came.
10082-8{_}1905{_}I forgot to mention that when Sir Horace expected visitors he used to tell me to send the servants to bed early.
10082-8{_}1906{_}He told me to do so this night, saying as usual, 'You understand, Hill?'
10082-8{_}1907{_}and I replied, 'Yes, Sir Horace,' The young woman came about half-past ten o'clock, and I let her in the side door and showed her up to the library on the first floor, where he used to sit and work and read.
10082-8{_}1908{_}Half an hour afterwards I took up some refreshments--some sandwiches and a small bottle of champagne for the young lady--and then went back downstairs till Sir Horace rang for me to let the lady out, which was generally about midnight.
10082-8{_}1909{_}But this night, I'd hardly been downstairs more than a quarter of an hour, when I heard a loud crash, followed by a sort of scream.
10082-8{_}1910{_}Before I could get out of my chair to go upstairs I heard the study door open, and Sir Horace called out, 'Hill, come here!'
10082-8{_}1911{_}"I went upstairs as quick as I could, and the door of the study being wide open, I could see inside.
10082-8{_}1912{_}Sir Horace and the young lady had evidently been having a quarrel.
10082-8{_}1913{_}They were standing up facing each other, and the table at which they had been sitting was knocked over, and the refreshments I had taken up had been scattered all about.
10082-8{_}1914{_}The young woman had been crying--I could see that at a glance--but Sir Horace looked dignified and the perfect gentleman--like he always was.
10082-8{_}1915{_}He turned to me when he saw me, and said, 'Hill, kindly show this young lady out,' I bowed and waited for her to follow me, which she did, after giving Sir Horace an angry look.
10082-8{_}1916{_}I let her out the same way as I let her in, and took her through the plantation to the front gate, which I locked after her.
10082-8{_}1917{_}When I got inside the house again, and was beginning to bolt up things for the night Sir Horace called me again and I went upstairs.
10082-8{_}1918{_}'Hill,' he said, in the same calm and collected voice, 'if that young lady calls again you're to deny her admittance.
10082-8{_}1919{_}That is all, Hill,' And he turned back into his room again.
10082-8{_}1920{_}"I didn't see her again until the morning after Sir Horace left for Scotland.
10082-8{_}1921{_}I had arranged for the female servants to go to Sir Horace's estate in the country during his absence, as he instructed before his departure, and they and I were very busy on this morning getting the house in order to be closed up--putting covers on the furniture and locking up the valuables.
10082-8{_}1922{_}"It was Sir Horace's custom to have this done when he was away every year instead of keeping the servants idling about the house on board wages, and the house was then left in my charge, as I told you, sir, and after the servants went to the country it was my custom to live at home till Sir Horace returned, coming over two or three times a week to look over the place and make sure that everything was all right.
10082-8{_}1923{_}On this morning, sir, after superintending the servants clearing up things, I went outside the house to have a final look round, and to see that the locks of the front and back gates were in good working order.
10082-8{_}1924{_}I was going to the back first, sir, but happening to glance about me as I walked round the house, I saw the young woman that Sir Horace had ordered me to show out of the house the night before he went to Scotland, peering out from behind one of the fir trees of the plantation in front of the house.
10082-8{_}1925{_}As soon as she saw that I saw her she beckoned to me.
10082-8{_}1926{_}"I would not have taken any notice of her, only I didn't want the women servants to see her.
10082-8{_}1927{_}Sir Horace, I knew, would not have liked that.
10082-8{_}1928{_}So I went across to her.
10082-8{_}1929{_}I asked her what she wanted, and I told her it was no use her wanting to see Sir Horace, for he had gone to Scotland.
10082-8{_}1930{_}'I don't want to see him,' she said, as impudent as brass.
10082-8{_}1931{_}'It's you I want to see, Field or Hill or whatever you call yourself now.'
10082-8{_}1932{_}It gave me quite a turn, I assure you, to find that this young woman knew my secret, and I turned round apprehensive-like, to make sure that none of the servants had heard her.
10082-8{_}1933{_}She noticed me and she laughed.
10082-8{_}1934{_}'It's all right, Hill,' she said.
10082-8{_}1935{_}'I'm not going to tell on you.
10082-8{_}1936{_}I've just brought you a message from an old friend--Fred Birchill--he wants to see you to-night at this address.'
10082-8{_}1937{_}And with that she put a bit of paper into my hand.
10082-8{_}1938{_}I was so upset and excited that I said I'd be there, and she went away.
10082-8{_}1939{_}"This Fred Birchill was a man I'd met in prison, and he was in the cell next to me.
10082-8{_}1940{_}How he'd got on my tracks I had no idea, but I seemed to see all my new life falling to pieces now he knew.
10082-8{_}1941{_}I'd tried to run straight since I served my sentence, and I knew Sir Horace would stand to me, but he couldn't afford to have any scandal about it, and I knew that if there was any possibility of my past becoming known I should have to leave his employ.
10082-8{_}1942{_}And then there was my poor wife and child, and this little business, sir.
10082-8{_}1943{_}Nothing was known about my past here.
10082-8{_}1944{_}So I determined to go and see this Birchill, sir.
10082-8{_}1945{_}The address she had given me was in Westminster, and, as my time was practically my own when Sir Horace wasn't home, I went down that same evening, and when I got up the flight of stairs and knocked at the door it was a woman's voice that said 'Come in,' I thought I recognised the voice.
10082-8{_}1946{_}When I opened the door, you can imagine my surprise when I saw the young woman to be Doris Fanning, who had had the quarrel with Sir Horace that night and had brought me the note that morning.
10082-8{_}1947{_}Birchill was sitting in a corner of the room, with his feet on another chair, smoking a pipe.
10082-8{_}1948{_}'Come in, No.
10082-8{_}1949{_}21,' he says, with an unpleasant smile, 'come in and see an old friend.
10082-8{_}1950{_}Put a chair for him, Doris, and leave the room.'
10082-8{_}1951{_}"The girl did so, and as soon as the door was closed behind her Birchill turned round to me and burst out, 'Hill, that damned employer of yours has served me a nasty trick, but I'm going to get even with him, and you're going to help me!'
10082-8{_}1952{_}I was taken back at his words, but I wanted to hear more before I spoke.
10082-8{_}1953{_}Then he told me that the young woman I had seen had been brutally treated by Sir Horace.
10082-8{_}1954{_}She had been living in a little flat in Westminster on a monthly allowance which Sir Horace made her, but he'd suddenly cut off her allowance and she'd have to be turned out in the street to starve because she couldn't pay her rent.
10082-8{_}1955{_}'A nice thing,' said Birchill fiercely, 'for this high-placed loose liver to carry on like this with a poor innocent girl whose only fault was that she loved him too well.
10082-8{_}1956{_}If I could show him up and pull him down, I would.
10082-8{_}1957{_}But I've done time, like you, Hill.
10082-8{_}1958{_}He was the judge who sentenced me, and if I tried to injure him that way my word would carry no weight; but I'll put up a job on him that'll make him sorry the longest day he lives, and you'll help me.
10082-8{_}1959{_}Sir Horace is in Scotland, Hill, and you're in charge of his place.
10082-8{_}1960{_}Get rid of the servants, Hill, and we'll burgle his house.
10082-8{_}1961{_}We can easily do it between us.'"
10082-8{_}1962{_}At this stage of his narrative, Hill stopped and looked anxiously at his audience as though to gather some idea of their feelings before he proceeded further.
10082-8{_}1963{_}But Inspector Chippenfield, with a fierce stare, merely remarked:  "And you consented?"
10082-8{_}1964{_}"I didn't at first," Hill retorted earnestly, "but when I refused he threatened me--threatened that he'd expose me and drag me and my wife and child down to poverty.
10082-8{_}1965{_}I pleaded with him, but it was of no use, and at last I had to consent.
10082-8{_}1966{_}I had some hope that in doing so I might find an opportunity to warn Sir Horace, but Birchill did not give me a chance.
10082-8{_}1967{_}He insisted that the burglary should take place without delay.
10082-8{_}1968{_}All I was to do was to give him a plan of the house, explain where to find the most valuable articles that had been left there, and wait for him at the flat while he committed the burglary.
10082-8{_}1969{_}His idea in making me wait for him at the flat was to make sure that I didn't play him false--put the double on him, as he called it--and he told the girl not to let me out of her sight till he came back, if anything went wrong I should have to pay for it when he came back.
10082-8{_}1970{_}"In accordance with Sir Horace's instructions, I sent the servants off to his country estate.
10082-8{_}1971{_}It had been arranged that Birchill was to wait for me to come over to the flat on the 18th of August, the night fixed for the burglary.
10082-8{_}1972{_}But about 7 o'clock, while I was at Riversbrook, I heard the noise of wheels outside, and looking out, I saw to my dismay Sir Horace getting out of a taxi-cab with a suit-case in his hand.
10082-8{_}1973{_}My first impulse was to tell him everything--indeed, I think that if I had had a chance I would have--but he came in looking very severe, and without saying a word about why he had returned from Scotland, said very sharply, 'Hill, have the servants been sent down to the country, as I directed?'
10082-8{_}1974{_}I told him that they had.
10082-8{_}1975{_}'Very good,' he said, 'then you go away at once, I won't want you any more.
10082-8{_}1976{_}I want the house to myself to-night.'
10082-8{_}1977{_}'Sir Horace,' I began, trembling a little, but he stopped me.
10082-8{_}1978{_}'Go immediately,' he said; 'don't stand there,' And he said it in such a tone that I was glad to go.
10082-8{_}1979{_}There was something in his look that frightened me that night.
10082-8{_}1980{_}I got across to Birchill's place and found him and the girl waiting for me.
10082-8{_}1981{_}I told him what had happened, and begged him to give up the idea of the burglary.
10082-8{_}1982{_}But he'd been drinking heavily, and was in a nasty mood.
10082-8{_}1983{_}First he said I'd been playing him false and had warned Sir Horace, but when I assured him that I hadn't he insisted on going to commit the burglary just the same.
10082-8{_}1984{_}With that he pulled out a revolver from his pocket, and swore with an oath that he'd put a bullet through me when he came back if I'd played him false and put Sir Horace on his guard, and that he'd put a bullet in the old scoundrel--meaning Sir Horace--if he interrupted him while he was robbing the house.
10082-8{_}1985{_}"He sat there, cursing and drinking, till he fell asleep with his head on the table, snoring.
10082-8{_}1986{_}I sat there not daring to breathe, hoping he'd sleep till morning, but Miss Fanning woke him up about nine, and he staggered to his feet to get out, with his revolver stuck in his coat pocket.
10082-8{_}1987{_}He was away over three hours and the girl and I sat there without saying a word, just looking at each other and waiting for a clock on the mantelpiece to chime the quarters.
10082-8{_}1988{_}It was a cuckoo clock, and it had just chimed twelve when we heard a quick step coming upstairs to the flat.
10082-8{_}1989{_}The girl fixed her big dark eyes inquiringly on me, and then we heard a hoarse whisper through the keyhole telling us to open the door.
10082-8{_}1990{_}"The girl ran to the door and let him in, but she shrieked at the sight of him when she saw him in the light.
10082-8{_}1991{_}For he looked ghastly, and there was a spot of blood on his face, and his hands were smeared with it.
10082-8{_}1992{_}He was shaking all over, and he went to the whisky bottle and drained the drop of spirit he'd left in it.
10082-8{_}1993{_}Then he turned to us and said, 'Sir Horace Fewbanks is dead--murdered!'
10082-8{_}1994{_}I suppose he read what he saw in our eyes, for he burst out angrily, 'Don't stand staring at me like a pair of damned fools.
10082-8{_}1995{_}You don't think I did it?
10082-8{_}1996{_}As God's my judge, I never did it.
10082-8{_}1997{_}He was dead and stiff when I got there.'
10082-8{_}1998{_}"Then he told us his story of what had happened.
10082-8{_}1999{_}He said that when he got to Riversbrook there was a light in the library and he got over the fence and hid himself in the garden.
10082-8{_}2000{_}Then he noticed that there was a light in the hall and that the hall door was open.
10082-8{_}2001{_}He thought Sir Horace had left it open by mistake, and he was going to creep into the house and hide himself there till after Sir Horace went to bed.
10082-8{_}2002{_}But suddenly the light in the library went out and Birchill again hid behind a tree, for he thought Sir Horace was retiring for the night.
10082-8{_}2003{_}Then the light in the hall went out and immediately after Birchill heard the hall door being closed.
10082-8{_}2004{_}Then he heard a step on the gravel path and saw a woman walking quickly down the path to the gate.
10082-8{_}2005{_}She was a well-dressed woman, and Birchill naturally thought that she was one of Sir Horace's lady friends.
10082-8{_}2006{_}But he thought it odd that Sir Horace, who was always a very polite gentleman to the ladies, should not have shown her off the premises.
10082-8{_}2007{_}He waited in the garden about half an hour, and as everything in the house seemed quite still, he made his way to a side window and forced it open.
10082-8{_}2008{_}He had an electric torch with him, and he used this to find his way about the house.
10082-8{_}2009{_}First of all, he wanted to find out in which room Sir Horace was sleeping, and he knew from the plan he'd made me draw for him which was Sir Horace's bedroom, so he went there and opened the door quietly and listened.
10082-8{_}2010{_}But he could not hear anyone breathing.
10082-8{_}2011{_}Then he tried some of the other rooms and turned on his torch, but could see no one.
10082-8{_}2012{_}He thought that perhaps Sir Horace had fallen asleep in a chair in the library, and he went there.
10082-8{_}2013{_}He listened at the door but could hear no sound.
10082-8{_}2014{_}Then he turned on his torch and by its light he saw a dreadful sight.
10082-8{_}2015{_}Sir Horace was lying huddled up near the desk--dead--just dead, he thought, because there were little bubbles of blood on his lips as if they had been blown there when breathing his last.
10082-8{_}2016{_}He didn't wait to see any more, but he turned and ran out of the house.
10082-8{_}2017{_}"I didn't believe his story, though Miss Fanning did, but he stuck to it and seemed so frightened that I thought there might be something in it till he brought out that he'd lost his revolver somewhere.
10082-8{_}2018{_}Then I remembered the horrid threats he'd used against Sir Horace, and I was convinced that he had committed the murder.
10082-8{_}2019{_}But of course I dared not let him think I suspected him, and I pretended to console him.
10082-8{_}2020{_}But the feeling that kept running through my head was that both of us would be suspected of the murder.
10082-8{_}2021{_}"I told this to Birchill, and that frightened him still more.
10082-8{_}2022{_}'What are we to do?'
10082-8{_}2023{_}he kept saying.
10082-8{_}2024{_}'We shall both be hanged.'
10082-8{_}2025{_}Then, after a while, we recovered ourselves a bit and began to look at it from a more common-sense point of view.
10082-8{_}2026{_}Nobody knew about Birchill's visit to the house except our two selves and the girl, and there was no reason why anybody should suspect us as long as we kept that knowledge to ourselves.
10082-8{_}2027{_}Birchill's idea, after we'd talked this over, was that I should go quietly home to bed, and pay a visit to Riversbrook on Friday as usual, discover Sir Horace Fewbanks's body, and then tell the police.
10082-8{_}2028{_}But I didn't like to do that for two reasons.
10082-8{_}2029{_}I didn't think that my nerves would be in a fit state to tell the police how I found the body without betraying to them that I knew something about it; and I couldn't bear to think of Sir Horace's body lying neglected all alone in that empty house till the following day--though I kept that reason to myself.
10082-8{_}2030{_}"It was the girl who hit on the idea of sending a letter to the police.
10082-8{_}2031{_}She said that it would be the best thing to do, because if they were informed and went to the house and discovered the body it wouldn't be so difficult for me to face them afterwards.
10082-8{_}2032{_}I agreed to that, and so did Birchill, who was very frightened in case I might give anything away, and consented on that account.
10082-8{_}2033{_}The girl showed us how to write the letter, too--she said she'd often heard of anonymous letters being written that way--and she brought out three different pens and a bottle of ink and a writing pad.
10082-8{_}2034{_}After we'd agreed what to write, she showed us how to do it, each one printing a letter on the paper in turn, and using a different pen each time."
10082-8{_}2035{_}"You took care to leave no finger-prints," said Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}2036{_}"We used a handkerchief to wrap our hands in," said Hill.
10082-8{_}2037{_}"Birchill got tired of passing the paper from one to another and wrote all his letters, leaving spaces for the girl and me to write in ours.
10082-8{_}2038{_}When the letter was written we wrote the address on the envelope the same way, and stamped it.
10082-8{_}2039{_}Then I went out and posted the letter in a pillar-box."
10082-8{_}2040{_}"At Covent Garden?"
10082-8{_}2041{_}suggested Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}2042{_}"Yes, at Covent Garden," said Hill.
10082-8{_}2043{_}"When I got home my wife was awake and in a terrible fright.
10082-8{_}2044{_}She wanted to know where I'd been, but I didn't tell her.
10082-8{_}2045{_}I told her, though, that my very life depended on nobody knowing I'd been out of my own home that night, and I made her swear that no matter who questioned her she'd stick to the story that I'd been at home all night, and in bed.
10082-8{_}2046{_}She begged me to tell her why, and as I knew that she'd have to be told the next day, I told her that Sir Horace Fewbanks had been murdered.
10082-8{_}2047{_}She buried her face in her pillow with a moan, but when I took an oath that I had had no hand in it she recovered, and promised not to tell a living soul that I had been out of the house and I knew I could depend on her.
10082-8{_}2048{_}"Next morning, as soon as I got up, I hurried off to a little wine tavern and asked to see the morning papers.
10082-8{_}2049{_}It was a foolish thing to do, because I might have known that nothing could have been discovered in time to get into the morning papers, for I hadn't posted the letter until nearly four o'clock.
10082-8{_}2050{_}But I was all nervous and upset, and as I couldn't face my wife or settle to anything until I knew the police had got the letter and found the body, I--though a strictly temperate man in the ordinary course of life, sir--sat down in one of the little compartments of the place and ordered a glass of wine to pass the time till the first editions of the evening papers came out--they are usually out here about noon.
10082-8{_}2051{_}But there was no news in the first editions, and so I stayed there, drinking port wine and buying the papers as fast as they came out.
10082-8{_}2052{_}But it was not till the 6.30 editions came out, late in the afternoon, that the papers had the news.
10082-8{_}2053{_}I hurried home and then went up to Riversbrook and reported myself to you, sir."
10082-8{_}2054{_}As Hill finished his story he buried his face in his hands, and bowed his head on the table in an attitude of utter dejection.
10082-8{_}2055{_}Rolfe, looking at him, wondered if he were acting a part, or if he had really told the truth.
10082-8{_}2056{_}He looked at Inspector Chippenfield to see how he regarded the confession, but his superior officer was busily writing in his note-book.

10082-8{_}2060{_}Hill raised a haggard face.
10082-8{_}2061{_}"Yes, sir," he said, with what seemed to be a painful effort.
10082-8{_}2062{_}"What is this girl Fanning like?"
10082-8{_}2063{_}"Rather a showy piece of goods, if I may say so, sir.
10082-8{_}2064{_}She has big black eyes, and black hair and small, regular teeth."
10082-8{_}2065{_}"And Sir Horace had been keeping her?"
10082-8{_}2066{_}"I think so, sir."
10082-8{_}2067{_}"And a fortnight before Sir Horace left for Scotland there was a quarrel--Sir Horace cast her off?"
10082-8{_}2068{_}"That is what it looked like to me," said the butler.

10082-8{_}2070{_}"That I don't know, sir."

10082-8{_}2077{_}Of course they had been talking of moving after Sir Horace stopped the allowance."

10082-8{_}2084{_}CHAPTER XII   "Do you think Hill's story is true?"
10082-8{_}2085{_}Rolfe asked Inspector Chippenfield, as they left the Camden Town Police Station and turned in the direction of the Tube station.

10082-8{_}2089{_}If he were arrested he would probably shut up and say no more."

10082-8{_}2091{_}"Yes."

10082-8{_}2110{_}he asked.

10082-8{_}2122{_}"Yes."

10082-8{_}2130{_}Scotland!

10082-8{_}2132{_}With a warrant in his pocket Inspector Chippenfield, accompanied by Rolfe, set out for Macauley Mansions, Westminster.

10082-8{_}2137{_}The door was closed.

10082-8{_}2147{_}he asked.

10082-8{_}2158{_}"It is so terrible about him."

10082-8{_}2161{_}She had come to the conclusion they were police officers and that they were aware of the position she occupied.

10082-8{_}2164{_}How long before he--before he died?"

10082-8{_}2303{_}said the captive, regarding them sullenly.

10082-8{_}2339{_}CHAPTER XIII   Doris Fanning got off a Holborn tram at King's Cross, and with a hasty glance round her as if to make sure she was not followed, walked at a rapid pace across the street in the direction of Caledonian Road.
10082-8{_}2340{_}She walked up that busy thoroughfare at the same quick gait for some minutes, then turned into a narrow street and, with another suspicious look around her, stopped at the doorway of a small shop a short distance down.
10082-8{_}2341{_}The shop sold those nondescript goods which seem to afford a living to a not inconsiderable class of London's small shopkeepers.
10082-8{_}2342{_}The windows and the shelves were full of dusty old books and magazines, trumpery curios and cheap china, second-hand furniture and a collection of miscellaneous odds and ends.
10082-8{_}2343{_}A thick dust lay over the whole collection, and the shop and its contents presented a deserted and dirty appearance.
10082-8{_}2344{_}Moreover, the door was closed as though customers were not expected.
10082-8{_}2345{_}The girl tried the door and found it locked--a fact which seemed to indicate that customers were not even desired.
10082-8{_}2346{_}After another hasty look up and down the street she tapped sharply on the door in a peculiar way.
10082-8{_}2347{_}The door was opened after the lapse of a few minutes by a short thickset man of over fifty, whose heavy face displayed none of the suavity and desire to please which is part of the stock-in-trade of the small shopkeeper of London.
10082-8{_}2348{_}A look of annoyance crossed his face at the sight of the girl, and his first remark to her was one which no well-regulated shopkeeper would have addressed to a prospective customer.

10082-8{_}2350{_}he exclaimed.
10082-8{_}2351{_}"What in God's name has brought you here?

10082-8{_}2357{_}"You might have known that I would read all about the case in the papers, and that I'd let you hear from me."

10082-8{_}2359{_}Oh, if you only knew what I've suffered since he was arrested.
10082-8{_}2360{_}Yesterday he was committed for trial.

10082-8{_}2362{_}"So you attended the police-court proceedings?"
10082-8{_}2363{_}said Kemp.

10082-8{_}2560{_}I told Fred what I thought, but he only laughed at me and said I was talking nonsense.
10082-8{_}2561{_}But I was right, for a week afterwards the police came and arrested Fred at the flat."
10082-8{_}2562{_}"How did they get him?"
10082-8{_}2563{_}asked Kemp.
10082-8{_}2564{_}"I saw them coming along the street from the window, and I pointed them out to Fred.
10082-8{_}2565{_}He tried to get away through the kitchen window along the ledge and down the spouting.
10082-8{_}2566{_}He almost got away, but one of the detectives saw him before he reached the ground, and they dashed down stairs and got him in the street.
10082-8{_}2567{_}Next day I saw in the papers that Hill had made an important statement to the police, and this had led to Fred's arrest.
10082-8{_}2568{_}Hill is the murderer, Kincher.
10082-8{_}2569{_}The cunning, wicked, treacherous villain told the police about Fred being up there.
10082-8{_}2570{_}He wants to see Fred hang in order to save his own neck."
10082-8{_}2571{_}The girl's voice rose to a shriek, and she sprang to her feet with blazing eyes.
10082-8{_}2572{_}"Kincher," she cried, "you've got to help me put the rope round this wretch's neck.
10082-8{_}2573{_}Do you hear me?"
10082-8{_}2574{_}Kemp's impassivity was in marked contrast to the girl's hysterical excitement.
10082-8{_}2575{_}"What do you want me to do?"
10082-8{_}2576{_}he asked.

10082-8{_}2578{_}He sent me over to ask you to arrange it without delay.
10082-8{_}2579{_}He wants you and two or three others to swear that he was over here on the night of the murder.
10082-8{_}2580{_}That will be sufficient to get him off."
10082-8{_}2581{_}"Not me," said Kemp, shaking his head decidedly.

10082-8{_}2583{_}The police have too many things against me for my word to be any good as a witness.

10082-8{_}2588{_}I don't know anybody I could get to come forward and swear Fred was in their company that night--there is a difference between fixing up a tale for the police before a man's arrested, and going into the witness box and committing perjury on oath."

10082-8{_}2590{_}"Suppose I went to the police and told them that Hill is the murderer?"
10082-8{_}2591{_}she suggested.
10082-8{_}2592{_}Kemp shook his head slowly.
10082-8{_}2593{_}"There is only your word for it that Hill killed him," he said.
10082-8{_}2594{_}"It doesn't look to me as if he did, when he went over to your flat and told Fred that Sir Horace had come back from Scotland.
10082-8{_}2595{_}If he had killed him he would have let Fred go over without saying a word about it."
10082-8{_}2596{_}"That was part of his cunning," said the girl.

10082-8{_}2598{_}I'm as certain that Hill committed the murder as if I had seen him do it with my own eyes."
10082-8{_}2599{_}Kemp shrugged his shoulders as though realising the uselessness of attempting to combat such a feminine form of reasoning.
10082-8{_}2600{_}"Didn't Fred say that the body was warm when he touched it?"
10082-8{_}2601{_}he asked.
10082-8{_}2602{_}She meditated a moment over this evidence of Hill's innocence.
10082-8{_}2603{_}"Well, if Hill didn't kill him, the woman Fred saw leaving the house must have done so," she declared.
10082-8{_}2604{_}"There is something in that," said Kemp.
10082-8{_}2605{_}"Look here, we've got to get Fred a good lawyer to defend him, and we must be guided by his advice as to what is the best thing to do.
10082-8{_}2606{_}He knows more about what will go down with a jury than you do."
10082-8{_}2607{_}"I paid a solicitor to defend him at the police court," said the girl, "but the money I gave him was thrown away.
10082-8{_}2608{_}He said nothing and did nothing."
10082-8{_}2609{_}"That shows he is a man who knows his business," replied Kemp.

10082-8{_}2611{_}It's a waste of breath.
10082-8{_}2612{_}The thing is to see that Fred is properly defended when the case comes on at the Old Bailey.
10082-8{_}2613{_}We want somebody who can manage the jury.
10082-8{_}2614{_}I should say Holymead is the man if you can get him.

10082-8{_}2618{_}It would be bound to make a good impression on the jury."

10082-8{_}2620{_}asked the girl.

10082-8{_}2622{_}"You take my advice and have him if you can get him.
10082-8{_}2623{_}Never mind what he costs, if you can raise the money.

10082-8{_}2626{_}Sir Horace put 100 in the Savings Bank for me on my last birthday.
10082-8{_}2627{_}And the furniture at the flat is mine.
10082-8{_}2628{_}I'd sell that and everything I've got, for Fred's sake."
10082-8{_}2629{_}"That is the way to talk," said Kemp.
