10082-8{_}3269{_}On the other hand he told the judge and jury everything that his professional experience prompted him as necessary and proper for them to know in order to bring about a conviction.
10082-8{_}3270{_}In the course of his evidence he made several attempts to introduce damaging facts as to Birchill's past, but Mr. Holymead protested to the judge.
10082-8{_}3271{_}Counsel for the defence protested that he had allowed his learned friend in opening the case a great deal of latitude as to the relations which had previously existed between the witness Hill and the prisoner, because the defence did not intend to attempt to hide the fact that the prisoner had a criminal record, but he had no intention of allowing a police witness to introduce irrelevant matter in order to prejudice the jury against the prisoner.
10082-8{_}3272{_}His Honour told the witness to confine himself to answering the questions put to him, and not to volunteer information.
10082-8{_}3273{_}After this rebuke Inspector Chippenfield resumed giving evidence.
10082-8{_}3274{_}He related what Birchill had said when arrested, and declared that he was positive that the footprints found outside the kitchen window were made by the boots produced in court which Birchill had been wearing at the time he was arrested.
10082-8{_}3275{_}He produced a jemmy which he had found at Fanning's flat, and said that it fitted the marks on the window at Riversbrook which had been forced on the night of the 18th of August.
10082-8{_}3276{_}Inspector Chippenfield's evidence was followed by that of the two tramway employees, who declared that to the best of their belief Birchill was the man who boarded their tram at half-past nine on the night of the 18th of August, and rode to the terminus at Hampstead, which they reached at 10.4 p. m. Both the witnesses showed a very proper respect for the law, and were obviously relieved when the brief cross-examination was over and they were free to go back to their tram-car.
10082-8{_}3277{_}CHAPTER XVII   "James Hill!"
10082-8{_}3278{_}called the court crier.
10082-8{_}3279{_}The butler stepped forward, mounted the witness-stand, and bowed his head deferentially towards the judge.
10082-8{_}3280{_}He was neatly dressed in black, and his sandy-grey hair was carefully brushed.
10082-8{_}3281{_}His face was as expressionless as ever, but a slight oscillation of the Court Bible in his right hand as he was sworn indicated that his nerves were not so calm as he strove to appear.
10082-8{_}3282{_}He looked neither to the right nor left, but kept his glance downcast.
10082-8{_}3283{_}Only once, as he stood there waiting to be questioned, did he cast a furtive look towards the man whose life hung on his evidence, but the malevolent vindictive gaze Birchill shot back at him caused him to lower his eyelids instantly.
10082-8{_}3284{_}Hill commenced his evidence in a voice so low that Mr. Walters stopped him at the outset and asked him to speak in a louder tone.
10082-8{_}3285{_}It soon became apparent that his evidence was making a deep impression on the court.
10082-8{_}3286{_}Sir Henry Hodson listened to him intently, and watched him keenly, as Hill, with impassive countenance and smooth even tones, told his strange story of the night of the murder.
10082-8{_}3287{_}When he had drawn to a conclusion he gave another furtive glance at the dock, but Birchill was seated with his head bowed down, as though tired, and with one hand supporting his face.
10082-8{_}3288{_}Mr. Walters methodically folded up his brief and sat down, with a sidelong glance in the direction of Mr. Holymead as he did so.
10082-8{_}3289{_}Every eye in court was turned on Holymead as the great K.C.
10082-8{_}3290{_}settled his gown on his shoulders and got up to cross-examine the principal Crown witness.
10082-8{_}3291{_}His cross-examination was the admiration of those spectators whose sympathies were on the side of the man in the dock as one of themselves.
10082-8{_}3292{_}Hill was cross-examined as to the lapse from honesty which had sent him to gaol, and he was reluctantly forced to admit, that so far from the theft being the result of an impulse to save his wife and child from starvation, as the Counsel for the prosecution had indicated, it was the result of the impulse of cupidity.
10082-8{_}3293{_}He had robbed a master who had trusted him and had treated him with kindness.
10082-8{_}3294{_}Having extracted this fact, in spite of Hill's evasions and twistings, Holymead straightened himself to his full height, and, shaking a warning finger at the witness, said:  "I put it to you, witness, that the reason Sir Horace Fewbanks engaged you as butler in his household at Riversbrook was because he knew you to be a man of few scruples, who would be willing to do things that a more upright honest man would have objected to?"
10082-8{_}3295{_}"That is not true," replied Hill.
10082-8{_}3296{_}"Is it not true that your late master frequently entertained women of doubtful character at Riversbrook?"
10082-8{_}3297{_}thundered the K.C.
10082-8{_}3298{_}Hill gasped at the question.
10082-8{_}3299{_}When he had first heard that his late master's old friend, Mr. Holymead, was to appear for Birchill, he had immediately come to the conclusion that Mr. Holymead was taking up the case in order to save Sir Horace's name from exposure by dealing carefully with his private life at Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3300{_}But here he was ruthlessly tearing aside the veil of secrecy.
10082-8{_}3301{_}Hill hesitated.
10082-8{_}3302{_}He glanced round the curious crowded court and saw the eager glances of the women as they impatiently awaited his reply.
10082-8{_}3303{_}He hesitated so long that Holymead repeated the question.
10082-8{_}3304{_}"Women of doubtful character?"
10082-8{_}3305{_}faltered the witness.
10082-8{_}3306{_}"I do not understand you."
10082-8{_}3307{_}"You understand me perfectly well, Hill.
10082-8{_}3308{_}I do not mean women off the streets, but women who have no moral reputation to maintain--women who do not mind letting confidential servants see that they have no regard for the conventional standards of life.
10082-8{_}3309{_}I mean, witness, that your late master frequently entertained at Riversbrook, women--I will not call them ladies--who were not particular at what hour they went home.
10082-8{_}3310{_}Sometimes one or more of them stayed all night, and you were entrusted with the confidential task of smuggling them out of the house without other servants knowing of their presence.
10082-8{_}3311{_}Is not that so?"
10082-8{_}3312{_}"I--I--"  "Answer the question without equivocation, witness."
10082-8{_}3313{_}"Y-es, sir."
10082-8{_}3314{_}There was a slight stir in the body of the court due to the fact that Miss Fewbanks and Mrs. Holymead had risen and were making their way to the door.
10082-8{_}3315{_}The fashionably-dressed women in the court stared with much interest at the daughter of the murdered man, whom most of them knew, in order to see how she was taking the disclosures about her dead father's private life.
10082-8{_}3316{_}"And sometimes there were quarrels between your late master and these visitors, were there not?"
10082-8{_}3317{_}continued Holymead.
10082-8{_}3318{_}"Quarrels, sir?"
10082-8{_}3319{_}"Surely you know that under the influence of wine some people become quarrelsome?"
10082-8{_}3320{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}3321{_}"Well, did your late master's nocturnal visitors ever become quarrelsome?"
10082-8{_}3322{_}"Sometimes, sir."
10082-8{_}3323{_}"In the exercise of your confidential duties did you sometimes see quarrelsome ladies off the premises?"
10082-8{_}3324{_}"Sometimes, sir."
10082-8{_}3325{_}"And it was no uncommon thing for them to say things to you about your master, eh?"
10082-8{_}3326{_}"Sometimes they didn't care what they said."
10082-8{_}3327{_}"Quite so," commented Counsel drily.
10082-8{_}3328{_}"They indulged in threats?"
10082-8{_}3329{_}"Not all of them," replied Hill, who at length saw where the cross-examination was tending.
10082-8{_}3330{_}"I do not suggest that all of them did--only that the more violent of them did so."
10082-8{_}3331{_}"Quite so, sir."
10082-8{_}3332{_}"So we may take it that the quarrel between your late master and Miss Fanning was not the only quarrel of the kind which came under your notice?"
10082-8{_}3333{_}"There were not many others," said Hill.
10082-8{_}3334{_}"It was not the only one?"
10082-8{_}3335{_}persisted Counsel.
10082-8{_}3336{_}"No, sir."
10082-8{_}3337{_}"In your evidence-in-chief you said nothing about Miss Fanning using threats against your master when you were showing her out?"
10082-8{_}3338{_}"No, sir."
10082-8{_}3339{_}"She did not use any?"
10082-8{_}3340{_}"Not in my hearing, sir."
10082-8{_}3341{_}There was a pause at this stage while Mr. Holymead consulted the notes he had made of Mr. Walters's cross-examination of the witness.
10082-8{_}3342{_}"What o'clock was it when you left Riversbrook on the 18th of August after your master's return from Scotland?"
10082-8{_}3343{_}"About half-past seven, sir."
10082-8{_}3344{_}"And what time did Sir Horace arrive home?"
10082-8{_}3345{_}"About seven o'clock, sir."
10082-8{_}3346{_}"What were you doing between seven and seven-thirty?"
10082-8{_}3347{_}"I unpacked his bags and got his bedroom ready.
10082-8{_}3348{_}I took him some refreshment up to the library."
10082-8{_}3349{_}"And he told you he wouldn't want you again until the following night about eight o'clock?"
10082-8{_}3350{_}"Yes, sir.
10082-8{_}3351{_}He said he thought he would be going back to Scotland by the night express, and I was to get his bag packed and lock up the house."
10082-8{_}3352{_}"You told Counsel for the prosecution in the course of your evidence that you were afraid of Birchill," continued Holymead.
10082-8{_}3353{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}3354{_}"Were you afraid of physical violence from him, or only that he would expose your past to the other servants?"
10082-8{_}3355{_}"I was afraid of him both ways," said Hill.
10082-8{_}3356{_}"Was it because of this fear that you made out for him a plan of Riversbrook to assist him in the burglary?"
10082-8{_}3357{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}3358{_}"When did you make out this plan?"
10082-8{_}3359{_}"The day after Sir Horace left for Scotland."
10082-8{_}3360{_}"Was that on your first visit to Miss Fanning's flat in Westminster after the prisoner had sent her to Riversbrook to tell you he wanted to see you?"
10082-8{_}3361{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}3362{_}"Did Birchill stand over you while you made out this plan?"
10082-8{_}3363{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}3364{_}"Would you know the plan again if you saw it?"
10082-8{_}3365{_}"Yes, sir."
10082-8{_}3366{_}Mr. Finnis, who had been hiding the plan under the papers before him, handed a document up to his chief.
10082-8{_}3367{_}Mr. Holymead unfolded it, and with a brief glance at it handed it up to the witness.
10082-8{_}3368{_}"Is that the plan?"
10082-8{_}3369{_}he asked.
10082-8{_}3370{_}Hill was somewhat taken aback at the production of the plan.
10082-8{_}3371{_}It was drawn in ink on a white sheet of paper of foolscap size, with a slightly bluish tint.
10082-8{_}3372{_}The paper was by no means clean, for Birchill had carried it about in his pocket.
10082-8{_}3373{_}The witness reluctantly admitted that the plan was the one he had given to Birchill.
10082-8{_}3374{_}To his manifest relief Counsel asked no further questions about it.
10082-8{_}3375{_}In a low tone Mr. Holymead formally expressed his intention to put the plan in as evidence.
10082-8{_}3376{_}He handed it to Mr. Walters, who, after a close inspection of it, passed it along to the judge's Associate for His Honour's inspection.
10082-8{_}3377{_}The rest of Hill's cross-examination concerned what happened at the flat on the night of the burglary.
10082-8{_}3378{_}He adhered to the story he had told, and could not be shaken in the main points of it.
10082-8{_}3379{_}But Mr. Holymead made some effective use of the discrepancy between the witness's evidence at the inquest as to his movements on the night of the murder and his evidence in court.
10082-8{_}3380{_}He elicited the fact that the police had discovered his evidence at the inquest was false and had forced him to make a confession by threatening to arrest him for the murder.
10082-8{_}3381{_}Mr. Holymead signified that he had nothing further to ask the witness, and Mr. Walters called his last witness, a young man named Charles Ryder, a resident of Liverpool, who had spent a week's holiday in London from the 14th to the 21st of August.
10082-8{_}3382{_}Ryder had stayed with some friends at Hampstead, and when making his way home on the night of the 18th of August had walked down Tanton Gardens in the belief that he was taking a short cut.
10082-8{_}3383{_}The time was about 11.20.
10082-8{_}3384{_}He saw a man running towards him along the footpath from the direction of Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3385{_}He caught a good glimpse of the man, who seemed to be very excited.
10082-8{_}3386{_}He was sure the prisoner was the man he had seen.
10082-8{_}3387{_}In cross-examination by Mr. Holymead he was far less positive in his identification of the prisoner, and finally admitted that the man he saw that night might be somebody else who resembled the prisoner in build.
10082-8{_}3388{_}CHAPTER XVIII   The second day of the trial began promptly when Mr. Justice Hodson took his seat.
10082-8{_}3389{_}Mr. Holymead's opening statement to the jury was brief.
10082-8{_}3390{_}He reminded them that the life of a fellow creature rested on their verdict.
10082-8{_}3391{_}If there was any doubt in their minds whether the prisoner had fired the shot which killed Sir Horace Fewbanks the prisoner was entitled to a verdict of "not guilty."
10082-8{_}3392{_}It was obligatory on the prosecution to prove guilt beyond all reasonable doubt.
10082-8{_}3393{_}He submitted that the prosecution had not established their case.
10082-8{_}3394{_}After hearing the case for the prosecution the jury must have grave doubts as to the guilt of the prisoner, and it was his duty as Counsel for the prisoner to put before the jury facts which would not only increase their doubts but bring them to the positive conclusion that the prisoner was not guilty.
10082-8{_}3395{_}He was not going to attempt to deny that the prisoner went to Riversbrook on the night of the murder.
10082-8{_}3396{_}He went there to commit a burglary.
10082-8{_}3397{_}But so far from Hill being terrorised into complicity in that crime it was he who had first suggested it to Birchill and had arranged it.
10082-8{_}3398{_}Material evidence on that point would be submitted to the jury.
10082-8{_}3399{_}Hill was a man who was incapable of gratitude.
10082-8{_}3400{_}His disposition was to bite the hand that fed him.
10082-8{_}3401{_}After being well treated by Sir Horace Fewbanks he had made up his mind to rob him as he had robbed his former master Lord Melhurst.
10082-8{_}3402{_}He knew that Sir Horace had quarrelled with this girl Fanning because of her association with Birchill, and he went to Birchill and put before him a proposal to rob Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3403{_}Birchill consented to the plan, and when on the night of the 18th August he broke into the house he found the murdered body of Sir Horace in the library.
10082-8{_}3404{_}That was the full extent of the prisoner's connection with the crime.
10082-8{_}3405{_}To the superficial and suspicious mind it might seem an improbable story, but to an earnest mind it was a story that carried conviction because of its simple straightforwardness--its crudity, if the jury liked to call it that.
10082-8{_}3406{_}It lacked the subtlety and the finish of a concocted story.
10082-8{_}3407{_}The murder took place before Birchill reached Riversbrook on his burglarious errand.
10082-8{_}3408{_}"It is my place," added Mr. Holymead, in concluding his address, "to convince you that my client is not guilty, or, in other words, to convince you that the murder was committed before he reached the house.
10082-8{_}3409{_}It is only with the greatest reluctance that I take upon myself the responsibility of pointing an accusing finger at another man.
10082-8{_}3410{_}In crimes of this kind you cannot expect to get anything but circumstantial evidence.
10082-8{_}3411{_}But there are degrees of circumstantial evidence, and my duty to my client lays upon me the obligation of pointing out to you that there is one person against whom the existing circumstantial evidence is stronger than it is against my client."
10082-8{_}3412{_}Crewe, who had secured his former place in the gallery of the court, looked down on the speaker.
10082-8{_}3413{_}He had carefully followed every word of Holymead's address, but the concluding portion almost electrified him.
10082-8{_}3414{_}He flattered himself that he was the only person in court who understood the full significance of the sonorous sentences with which the famous K.C.
10082-8{_}3415{_}concluded his address to the jury.
10082-8{_}3416{_}As his eyes wandered over the body of the court below, Crewe saw that Mrs. Holymead and Mademoiselle Chiron were sitting in one of the back seats, but that they were not accompanied by Miss Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}3417{_}It was evident to him by the way in which Mrs. Holymead followed the proceedings that her interest in the case was something far deeper than wifely interest in her husband's connection with it as counsel for the defence.
10082-8{_}3418{_}Leaning forward in her seat, with her hands clasped in her lap, she listened eagerly to every word.
10082-8{_}3419{_}During the day his gaze went back to her at intervals, and on several occasions he became aware that she had been watching him while he watched her husband.
10082-8{_}3420{_}The first witness for the defence was Doris Fanning.
10082-8{_}3421{_}The drift of her evidence was to exonerate the prisoner at the expense of Hill.
10082-8{_}3422{_}She declared that she had not gone to Riversbrook to see Hill after the final quarrel with Sir Horace.
10082-8{_}3423{_}Hill had come to her flat in Westminster of his own accord and had asked for Birchill.
10082-8{_}3424{_}She went out of the room while they discussed their business, but after Hill had gone Birchill told her that Hill had put up a job for him at Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3425{_}Birchill showed her the plan of Riversbrook that Hill had made, and asked her if it was correct as far as she knew.
10082-8{_}3426{_}Yes, she was sure she would know the plan again if she saw it.
10082-8{_}3427{_}The judge's Associate handed it to Mr. Holymead, who passed it to the witness.
10082-8{_}3428{_}"Is this it?"
10082-8{_}3429{_}he asked.
10082-8{_}3430{_}"Yes," she replied emphatically, almost without inspecting it.
10082-8{_}3431{_}"I want you to look at it closely," said Counsel.
10082-8{_}3432{_}"When Birchill showed you the plan immediately after Hill's departure, what impression did you get regarding it?"
10082-8{_}3433{_}She looked at him blankly.
10082-8{_}3434{_}"I don't understand you," she said.
10082-8{_}3435{_}"You can tell the difference between ink that has been newly used and ink that has been on the paper some days.
10082-8{_}3436{_}Was the ink fresh?"
10082-8{_}3437{_}"No, it was old ink," she said.
10082-8{_}3438{_}"How do you know that?"
10082-8{_}3439{_}"Because ink doesn't go black till a long while after it is written.
10082-8{_}3440{_}At least, the letters _I_ write don't."
10082-8{_}3441{_}She shot a veiled coquettish glance at the big K.C.
10082-8{_}3442{_}from under her long eyelashes.
10082-8{_}3443{_}The K.C.
10082-8{_}3444{_}returned the glance with a genial smile.
10082-8{_}3445{_}"What do you write your letters on, Miss Fanning?"
10082-8{_}3446{_}She almost giggled at the question.
10082-8{_}3447{_}"I use a writing tablet," she replied.
10082-8{_}3448{_}"Ruled or unruled?"
10082-8{_}3449{_}"Ruled.
10082-8{_}3450{_}I couldn't write straight if there weren't lines."
10082-8{_}3451{_}She smiled again.
10082-8{_}3452{_}"And what colour do you affect--grey, rose-pink or white paper?"
10082-8{_}3453{_}"Always white."
10082-8{_}3454{_}"Is that all the paper you have at your flat for writing purposes?"
10082-8{_}3455{_}"Yes."
10082-8{_}3456{_}"Then what did Birchill write on when he wanted to write a letter?"
10082-8{_}3457{_}"He used mine."
10082-8{_}3458{_}"Are you sure of that?"
10082-8{_}3459{_}"Yes.
10082-8{_}3460{_}When he wanted to write a letter he used to ask me for my tablet and an envelope.
10082-8{_}3461{_}And generally he used to borrow a stamp as well."
10082-8{_}3462{_}She pouted slightly, with another coquettish glance.
10082-8{_}3463{_}"Look at that plan again," said the K.C.
10082-8{_}3464{_}"Have you ever had paper like it at your flat?"
10082-8{_}3465{_}She shook her head.
10082-8{_}3466{_}"Never."
10082-8{_}3467{_}"Have you ever seen paper of that kind in Birchill's possession before he showed you the plan?"
10082-8{_}3468{_}"Never."
10082-8{_}3469{_}"When he showed you the plan had the paper been folded?"
10082-8{_}3470{_}"Yes."
10082-8{_}3471{_}The K.C.
10082-8{_}3472{_}took the witness, now very much at her ease, to the night of the murder.
10082-8{_}3473{_}She denied strenuously that Hill tried to dissuade Birchill from carrying out the burglary because Sir Horace Fewbanks had returned unexpectedly from Scotland.
10082-8{_}3474{_}It was Birchill who suggested postponing the burglary until Sir Horace left, but Hill urged that the original plan should be adhered to.
10082-8{_}3475{_}He declared that Sir Horace would remain at home at least a fortnight, and perhaps longer.
10082-8{_}3476{_}His master was a sound sleeper, he said, and if Birchill waited until he went to bed there would be no danger of awakening him.
10082-8{_}3477{_}She contradicted many details of Hill's evidence as to what took place when the prisoner returned from breaking into Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3478{_}It was untrue, she said, that there was a spot of blood on Birchill's face or that his hands were smeared with blood.
10082-8{_}3479{_}He was a little bit excited when he returned, but after one glass of whisky he spoke quite calmly of what had happened.
10082-8{_}3480{_}The next witness was a representative of the firm of Holmes and Jackson, papermakers, who was handed the plan of Riversbrook which Hill had drawn.
10082-8{_}3481{_}He stated that the paper on which the plan was drawn was manufactured by his firm, and supplied to His Majesty's Stationery Office.
10082-8{_}3482{_}He identified it by the quality of the paper and the watermark.
10082-8{_}3483{_}In reply to Mr. Walters the witness was sure that the paper he held in his hand had been manufactured by his firm for the Government.
10082-8{_}3484{_}It was impossible for him to be mistaken.
10082-8{_}3485{_}Other firms might manufacture paper of a somewhat similar quality and tint, but it would not be exactly similar.
10082-8{_}3486{_}Besides, he identified it by his firm's watermark, and he held the plan up to the light and pointed it out to the court.
10082-8{_}3487{_}Counsel for the defence called two more witnesses on this point--one to prove that supplies of the paper on which the plan was drawn were issued to legal departments of the Government, and an elderly man named Cobb, Sir Horace Fewbanks's former tipstaff, who stated that he took some of the paper in question to Riversbrook on Sir Horace's instructions.
10082-8{_}3488{_}And then, to the astonishment of junior members of the bar who were in court watching his conduct of the case in order to see if they could pick up a few hints, he intimated that his case was closed.
10082-8{_}3489{_}It seemed to them that the great K.C.
10082-8{_}3490{_}had put up a very flimsy case for the defence, and that in spite of the fact that the prosecutor's case rested mainly on the evidence of a tainted witness Holymead would be very hard put to it to get his man off.
10082-8{_}3491{_}"Isn't my learned friend going to call the prisoner?"
10082-8{_}3492{_}suggested Mr. Walters, with the cunning design of giving the jury something to think of when they were listening to his learned friend's address.
10082-8{_}3493{_}"It's scarcely necessary," said Mr. Holymead, who saw the trap, and replied in a tone which indicated that the matter was not worth a moment's consideration.
10082-8{_}3494{_}He began his address to the jury by emphasising the fact that a fellow creature's life depended on the result of their deliberations.
10082-8{_}3495{_}The duty that rested upon them of saying whether the prosecution had established beyond all reasonable doubt that the prisoner shot Sir Horace Fewbanks was a solemn and impressive one.
10082-8{_}3496{_}He asked them to consider the case carefully in all its bearings.
10082-8{_}3497{_}He could not claim for his client that he was a man of spotless reputation.
10082-8{_}3498{_}The prisoner belonged to a class who earned their living by warring against society.
10082-8{_}3499{_}But that fact did not make him a murderer.
10082-8{_}3500{_}On what did the case for the prosecution rest?
10082-8{_}3501{_}On the evidence of Hill and three other witnesses who, on the night of the murder, had seen a man somewhat resembling the prisoner in the vicinity of Riversbrook, or making towards the vicinity of that house.
10082-8{_}3502{_}But so far from wishing to emphasise the weakness of identification he admitted that the prisoner went to Riversbrook with the intention of committing a burglary.
10082-8{_}3503{_}"We admit that he went there the night Sir Horace Fewbanks returned from Scotland," he continued.
10082-8{_}3504{_}"Counsel for the prosecution will make the most of those admissions in the course of his address to you, but the point to which I wish to direct your attention is that we make this damaging admission so that you may decide between the prisoner and the man who led him into a trap by instigating the burglary.
10082-8{_}3505{_}Now we come to the evidence of Hill.
10082-8{_}3506{_}I know you will not convict a man of murder on the unsupported evidence of a fellow criminal.
10082-8{_}3507{_}But I want to point out to you that even if Hill's evidence were true in every detail, even if Hill had not swerved one iota from the truth, there is nothing in his evidence to lead to the positive conclusion that the prisoner murdered Hill's master, Sir Horace Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}3508{_}What does Hill's evidence against the prisoner amount to?
10082-8{_}3509{_}Let us accept it for the moment as absolutely true.
10082-8{_}3510{_}Later on I will show you plainly that the man is a liar, that he is a cunning scoundrel, and that his evidence is utterly unreliable.
10082-8{_}3511{_}But accepting for the moment his evidence as true the case against the prisoner amounts to this: by threats of exposure Birchill compelled Hill to consent to Riversbrook being robbed while the owner was in Scotland.
10082-8{_}3512{_}"Hill's complicity, according to his own story, extended only to supplying a plan of the house and giving Birchill some information as to where various articles of value would be found.
10082-8{_}3513{_}On the 18th of August Hill went to Riversbrook to see that everything was in order for the burglary that night.
10082-8{_}3514{_}While he was there his master returned unexpectedly.
10082-8{_}3515{_}Hill then went to the flat in Westminster and told Birchill that Sir Horace had returned.
10082-8{_}3516{_}His own story is that he tried to get Birchill to abandon the idea of the burglary, but that Birchill, who had been drinking, swore that he would carry out the plan, and that if he came across Sir Horace he would shoot him.
10082-8{_}3517{_}What grudge had Birchill against Sir Horace Fewbanks?
10082-8{_}3518{_}The fact that Sir Horace had discarded the woman Fanning because of her association with Birchill.
10082-8{_}3519{_}Gentlemen, does a man commit a murder for a thing of that kind?
10082-8{_}3520{_}"Let us test the credibility of the man who has tried to swear away the life of the prisoner.
10082-8{_}3521{_}You saw him in the witness-box, and I have no doubt formed your own conclusions as to the type of man he is.
10082-8{_}3522{_}Did he strike you as a man who would stand by the truth above all things, or a man who would lie persistently in order to save his own skin?
10082-8{_}3523{_}That the man cannot be believed even when on his oath has been publicly demonstrated in the courts of the land.
10082-8{_}3524{_}The story he told the court yesterday in the witness-box of his movements on the day of the murder is quite different to the story he told on his oath at the inquest on the body of Sir Horace Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}3525{_}Let me read to you the evidence he gave at the inquest."
10082-8{_}3526{_}Mr. Finnis handed to his leader a copy of Hill's evidence at the inquest, and Mr. Holymead read it out to the jury.
10082-8{_}3527{_}He then read out a shorthand writer's account of Hill's evidence on the previous day.
10082-8{_}3528{_}"Which of these accounts are we to believe?"
10082-8{_}3529{_}he said, turning to the jury.
10082-8{_}3530{_}"The latter one, the prosecution says.
10082-8{_}3531{_}But why, I ask?
10082-8{_}3532{_}Because it tallies with the statement extorted from Hill by the police under the threat of charging him with the murder.
10082-8{_}3533{_}Does that make it more credible?
10082-8{_}3534{_}Is a man like Hill, who is placed in that position, likely to tell the truth, the whole truth, and nothing but the truth?
10082-8{_}3535{_}It is an insult to the jury as men of intelligence to ask you to believe Hill's evidence.
10082-8{_}3536{_}I do not ask you to believe the story he told at the inquest in preference to the story he told here in the witness-box yesterday.
10082-8{_}3537{_}I ask you to regard both stories as the evidence of a man who is too deeply implicated in this crime to be able to speak the truth.
10082-8{_}3538{_}"I will prove to you, gentlemen of the jury, that the man is a criminal by instinct and a liar by necessity--the necessity of saving his own skin.
10082-8{_}3539{_}He robbed his former master, Lord Melhurst, and he planned to rob his late master, Sir Horace Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}3540{_}But knowing that his former crime would be brought against him when the police came to investigate a robbery at Riversbrook he was too cunning to rob Riversbrook himself.
10082-8{_}3541{_}He looked about him for an accomplice and he selected Birchill.
10082-8{_}3542{_}You heard him say in the witness-box that he drew Birchill a plan of Riversbrook--the plan I now hold in my hand.
10082-8{_}3543{_}I will ask you to inspect the plan closely.
10082-8{_}3544{_}Hill told us that Birchill terrorised him into drawing this plan by threats of exposure.
10082-8{_}3545{_}Exposure of what?
10082-8{_}3546{_}His master, Sir Horace Fewbanks, knew he had been in gaol, so what had he to fear from exposure?
10082-8{_}3547{_}His proper course, if he were an honest man, would have been to tell his master that Birchill was planning to rob the house and had endeavoured to draw him into the crime.
10082-8{_}3548{_}But he did nothing of the kind, for the simple reason that the plan to rob Riversbrook was his own, and not Birchill's.
10082-8{_}3549{_}"Now, gentlemen, you have all seen the plan which this tainted witness declares was drawn by him because Birchill terrorised him and stood over him while he drew it.
10082-8{_}3550{_}Is there anything in that plan to suggest that it was drawn by a man in a state of nervous terror?
10082-8{_}3551{_}Why, the lines are as firmly drawn as if they had been made by an architect working at his leisure in his office.
10082-8{_}3552{_}Was this plan drawn by a man in a state of nervous terror with his tormentor standing threateningly over him, or was it drawn up by a man working at leisure, free not only from terror but from interruption?
10082-8{_}3553{_}The answer to that question is supplied in the evidence given by three witnesses as to the paper used.
10082-8{_}3554{_}Hill says the plan was drawn at the flat.
10082-8{_}3555{_}Two other witnesses swore that it was paper supplied exclusively for Government Departments, and another witness swore that he had taken such paper to Riversbrook for the use of Sir Horace Fewbanks, who, like every one of His Majesty's judges, found it necessary to do some of his judicial work at home.
10082-8{_}3556{_}What is the inevitable inference?
10082-8{_}3557{_}I ask you if you can have any doubt, after looking at that plan and after hearing the evidence given to-day about the paper, that the proposal to rob Riversbrook was Hill's own proposal, that Hill drew a plan of the house on paper he abstracted from his master's desk--paper which this confidential servant was apparently in the habit of using for private purposes--and that he gave it to Birchill when he asked Birchill to join him in the crime?
10082-8{_}3558{_}"When one of the main features of Hill's story is proved to be false, how can you believe any of the rest?
10082-8{_}3559{_}In the light in which we now see him, with his cunning exposed, what significance is to be attached to his statement that Birchill in his presence threatened to shoot Sir Horace Fewbanks if the master of Riversbrook interfered with him?
10082-8{_}3560{_}Such a threat was not made, but why should Hill say it was made?
10082-8{_}3561{_}For the same reason that he lied about the plan--to save his own skin.
10082-8{_}3562{_}I submit to you, gentlemen, that when Hill went to see Birchill at the Westminster flat on the night arranged for the burglary Sir Horace Fewbanks was dead--murdered--and that Hill knew he was murdered.
10082-8{_}3563{_}His own story is that he tried to persuade Birchill to abandon the proposed burglary, but, according to the witness Fanning, he did all in his power to induce Birchill to carry out the original plan when he saw that Birchill was disposed to postpone the burglary in view of the return of the master of Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3564{_}Why did he want Birchill to carry out the burglary?
10082-8{_}3565{_}Because he knew that his master's murdered body was lying in the house, and he wanted to be in the position to produce evidence against Birchill as the murderer if he found himself in a tight corner as the result of the subsequent investigations of the police.
10082-8{_}3566{_}Remember that the body of the victim was fully dressed when it was discovered by the police, and that none of the electric lights were burning.
10082-8{_}3567{_}Does not that prove conclusively that the murder was not committed by Birchill, that Sir Horace Fewbanks was dead when Birchill broke into the house?
10082-8{_}3568{_}"Birchill, an experienced criminal, would not break into the house while there was anybody moving about.
10082-8{_}3569{_}He would wait until the house was in darkness and the inmates asleep.
10082-8{_}3570{_}To do otherwise would increase enormously the risks of capture.
10082-8{_}3571{_}But the fact that the police found the body of the murdered man fully dressed shows that Sir Horace was murdered before he went to bed--before Birchill broke into the house.
10082-8{_}3572{_}It shows conclusively that the murder was committed before dusk.
10082-8{_}3573{_}Your only alternatives to that conclusion are that the murdered man went to bed with his clothes on, or that the murderer broke into the house before Sir Horace had gone to bed and after killing Sir Horace went coolly round the house turning out the lights instead of fleeing in terror at his deed without even waiting to collect any booty.
10082-8{_}3574{_}I am sure that as reasonable men you will reject both these alternatives as absurd.
10082-8{_}3575{_}No evidence has been produced to show that anything has been stolen from the place.
10082-8{_}3576{_}It was evidently the theory of the prosecution that the prisoner, after shooting Sir Horace, had fled.
10082-8{_}3577{_}The evidence of Hill was that he arrived at Fanning's flat in a state of great excitement.
10082-8{_}3578{_}His excitement would be consistent with his story of having discovered the body of a murdered man, but not consistent with the conduct of a cold-blooded calculating murderer who had broken into the house before Sir Horace had undressed for bed, had shot him, and had then gone round the house turning out the lights without having any apparent object in doing so.
10082-8{_}3579{_}"Gentlemen, I think you will admit that the crime must have been committed before dusk; before any lights were turned on.
10082-8{_}3580{_}I do not ask you to say that Hill is guilty.
10082-8{_}3581{_}The responsibility of saying what man other than the prisoner shot Sir Horace Fewbanks does not rest with you.
10082-8{_}3582{_}But I do urge you to ask yourselves whether, as between Hill and the prisoner, the probability of guilt is not on the side of this witness who lied to the coroner's court about his movements on the night of the murder, and who lied to this court about the plan for the robbery of Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3583{_}I have shown you that Hill was the master mind in planning the burglary, and, that being so, would not Birchill have consented to the postponement of the burglary if Hill had urged him to do so when he visited the flat after the unexpected return of the master of Riversbrook?
10082-8{_}3584{_}Is not the evidence of the witness Fanning, that Hill urged Birchill to carry out the burglary after Sir Horace had gone to sleep, more credible than Hill's statement that he endeavoured to induce Birchill to abandon the proposed crime?
10082-8{_}3585{_}Knowing what you know of Hill's past as a man who will rob his master, knowing that he attempted to deceive you with regard to this plan of Riversbrook in order that you might play your part in his cunning scheme, I urge you to ask yourselves whether it is not more probable that Hill fired the shot which killed Sir Horace Fewbanks than that the prisoner did so.
10082-8{_}3586{_}Is it not extremely probable that the unexpected return of Sir Horace upset Hill, who was giving a final look round the house before the burglary took place?
10082-8{_}3587{_}That, instead of answering his master with the suave obsequious humility of the well-trained servant, he revealed the baffled ferocity of a criminal whose carefully arranged plan seemed to have miscarried; that his master angrily rebuked him, and Hill, losing control of himself, sprang at Sir Horace, and the struggle ended with Hill drawing a revolver and shooting his master?
10082-8{_}3588{_}"The rest of the story from that point can be constructed without difficulty.
10082-8{_}3589{_}The murderer's first thought was to divert suspicion from himself, and the best way to do that was to divert suspicion elsewhere.
10082-8{_}3590{_}He locked up the house and went to see Birchill.
10082-8{_}3591{_}He urged Birchill to break into Riversbrook, in which the dead body of the murdered man lay.
10082-8{_}3592{_}It is true that he need not have told Birchill that Sir Horace had returned unexpectedly; but his object in doing so was to make Birchill search about the house until he inadvertently stumbled across the dead body.
10082-8{_}3593{_}Had Birchill been under the impression that he had broken into an entirely empty house he would have collected the valuables and might not have entered the library in which the dead body lay.
10082-8{_}3594{_}It was necessary for Hill's purpose that Birchill should come across the corpse; then he would be vitally interested in diverting suspicion from himself (Birchill) and that is why he cunningly revealed to Birchill that Sir Horace had returned.
10082-8{_}3595{_}I put it to the jury that such is a more probable explanation of how Sir Horace met his death than that he was shot down by Birchill.
10082-8{_}3596{_}I ask you again to remember that the body was fully dressed when it was found by the police.
10082-8{_}3597{_}I put it to you that in this matter the prisoner walked into a trap prepared by his more cunning fellow criminal.
10082-8{_}3598{_}And I urge you, with all the earnestness it is possible for a man to use when the life of a fellow creature is at stake, not to be led into a trap--not to play the part this cunning criminal Hill has designed for you--in the sacrifice of the life of an innocent man for the purpose of saving himself from his just deserts.
10082-8{_}3599{_}Looking at the whole case--as you will not fail to do--with the breadth of view of experienced men of the world, with some knowledge of the workings of human nature, with a natural horror of the depths of cunning of which some natures are capable, with a deep sense of the solemn responsibility for a human life upon you, I confidently appeal to you to say that the prisoner was not the man who shot Sir Horace Fewbanks, and to bring in a verdict of 'not guilty.'"
10082-8{_}3600{_}A short discussion arose between the bench and bar on the question of adjourning the court or continuing the case in the hope of finishing it in a few hours.
10082-8{_}3601{_}Sir Henry Hodson wanted to finish the case that night, but Counsel for the prosecution intimated that his address to the jury would take nearly two hours.
10082-8{_}3602{_}As it was then nearly five o'clock, and His Honour had to sum up before the jury could retire, it was hardly to be hoped that the case could be finished that night, as the jury might be some time in arriving at a verdict.
10082-8{_}3603{_}His Honour decided to adjourn the court and finish the case next day.
10082-8{_}3604{_}CHAPTER XIX   Mr. Walters began his address to the jury on orthodox lines.
10082-8{_}3605{_}He referred to the fact that his learned friend had warned them that the life of a fellow creature rested on their verdict.
10082-8{_}3606{_}It was right that they should keep that in mind; it was right that they should fully realise the responsible nature of the duty they were called upon to perform, but it would be wrong for them to over-estimate their responsibility, or to feel weighed down by it.
10082-8{_}3607{_}It would be wrong for them to be influenced by sentimental considerations of the fact that a fellow creature's life was at stake.
10082-8{_}3608{_}Strictly speaking, that had nothing whatever to do with them.
10082-8{_}3609{_}Their responsibility ended with their verdict.
10082-8{_}3610{_}If their verdict was "guilty" the responsibility of taking the prisoner's life would rest upon the law--not on the jury, not on His Honour who passed the sentence of death, not on the prison officials who carried out the execution.
10082-8{_}3611{_}The jury would do well to keep in mind the fact that their responsibility in this trial, impressive and important as every one must acknowledge it to be, was nevertheless strictly limited as far as the taking of the life of the prisoner was concerned.
10082-8{_}3612{_}He then went over the evidence in detail, building up again the case for the prosecution where Mr. Holymead had made breaches in it, and attempting to demolish the case for the defence.
10082-8{_}3613{_}Hill, he declared, was an honest witness.
10082-8{_}3614{_}The man had made one false step but he had done his best to retrieve it, and with the help he had received from his late master, Sir Horace Fewbanks, he would have buried the past effectively if it had not been for the fact that the prisoner, who was a confirmed criminal, had determined to drag him down.
10082-8{_}3615{_}There was no doubt that Hill's association with Birchill had been unfortunate for him.
10082-8{_}3616{_}It had dragged his past into the light of day, and he stood before them a ruined man.
10082-8{_}3617{_}He had tried to live down the past, and but for Birchill he would have succeeded in doing so.
10082-8{_}3618{_}But now no one would employ him as a house servant after the revelations that had been made in this court.
10082-8{_}3619{_}They had seen Hill in the witness-box, and he would ask the jury whether he looked like the masterful cunning scoundrel which the defence had described, or a weak creature who would be easily led by a man of strong will, such as the prisoner was.
10082-8{_}3620{_}As to what took place at the flat, they had a choice between the evidence of Hill and the evidence of the girl Fanning.
10082-8{_}3621{_}Hill had told them that he had tried to dissuade the prisoner from going to Riversbrook to burgle the premises, because his master had returned unexpectedly; Fanning had told them that the prisoner was in favour of postponing the crime, but that Hill had urged him to carry it out.
10082-8{_}3622{_}Which story was the more probable?
10082-8{_}3623{_}What reliance could they place on the evidence of Fanning?
10082-8{_}3624{_}He did not wish to say that the witness was utterly vicious and incapable of telling the truth--a description that the defence had applied to Hill--but they must take into consideration the fact that Fanning was the prisoner's mistress.
10082-8{_}3625{_}Was it likely that a woman, knowing her lover's life was at stake, would come here and speak the truth, if she knew the truth would hang him?
10082-8{_}3626{_}He was sure that the jury, as men who knew the world thoroughly, would not hesitate between the evidence of Hill and that of Fanning.
10082-8{_}3627{_}The case for the defence depended to a great extent on the plan of Riversbrook which Hill candidly admitted he had drawn.
10082-8{_}3628{_}His learned friend had called evidence to show that the paper on which the plan was drawn was of a quality which was not procurable by the general public.
10082-8{_}3629{_}That might be so, but what his learned friend had not succeeded in doing, and could not possibly have hoped to succeed in doing, was to show that Birchill could not have obtained possession in any other way of paper of that kind.
10082-8{_}3630{_}Yet it was necessary for the defence to prove that, in order to prove that the plan was not drawn at Fanning's flat by Hill under threats from Birchill, but that Hill had drawn it at Riversbrook, and that he gave it to Birchill in order to induce him to consent to the proposal to break into the house.
10082-8{_}3631{_}There were dozens of ways in which paper of this particular quality might have got to the flat.
10082-8{_}3632{_}Might not Birchill have a friend in His Majesty's Stationery Office?
10082-8{_}3633{_}Was it impossible that the witness Fanning had a friend in that Office, or in one of the Government Departments to which the paper was supplied?
10082-8{_}3634{_}Was it impossible in view of her relations with the victim of this crime for Fanning to have obtained some of the paper at Riversbrook and to have taken it home to her flat?
10082-8{_}3635{_}She had sworn in the witness-box that she had not had paper of that kind in her possession, but with her lover's life at stake was she likely to stick at a lie if it would help to get him off?
10082-8{_}3636{_}Counsel for the defence had endeavoured to make much of the fact that the dead body of Sir Horace Fewbanks was fully dressed when the police discovered it.
10082-8{_}3637{_}He endeavoured to persuade them that such a fact established the complete innocence of the prisoner and that because of it they must bring in a verdict of "not guilty."
10082-8{_}3638{_}He asked them to accept it as evidence not only that Sir Horace Fewbanks was dead when the prisoner broke into the house, but that he was dead when Hill left Riversbrook at 7.30 p. m. to meet Birchill at Fanning's flat.
10082-8{_}3639{_}With an ingenuity which did credit to his imagination, he put before them as his theory of the crime that a quarrel took place between Sir Horace Fewbanks and Hill at Riversbrook, that Hill shot his master and then went to Fanning's flat so as to see that Birchill carried out the burglary as arranged, and at the same time found Sir Horace's dead body, and thus directed suspicion to himself.
10082-8{_}3640{_}The only support for this, far-fetched theory was that the body when discovered by the police was fully dressed, and that none of the electric lights were burning.
10082-8{_}3641{_}Counsel for the defence contended that these two facts established his theory that the murder was committed before dusk.
10082-8{_}3642{_}They established nothing of the kind.
10082-8{_}3643{_}There were half a dozen more credible explanations of these things than the one he asked the jury to accept.
10082-8{_}3644{_}What mystery was there in a man being fully dressed in his own house at midnight?
10082-8{_}3645{_}The defence had been at great pains to show that Sir Horace Fewbanks was a man of somewhat irregular habits in his private life.
10082-8{_}3646{_}Did not that suggest that he might have turned off the lights and gone to sleep in an arm-chair in the library with the intention of going out in an hour or two to keep an appointment?
10082-8{_}3647{_}If he had an appointment--and his sudden and unexpected return from Scotland would suggest that he had a secret and important appointment--he would be more likely to take a short nap in his chair than to undress and go to bed.
10082-8{_}3648{_}Might not the prisoner, who was a bold and reckless man, have broken into the house when the lights were burning and his victim was awake and fully dressed?
10082-8{_}3649{_}In that case what was to prevent his turning off the lights before leaving the house instead of leaving them burning to attract attention?
10082-8{_}3650{_}What was to prevent the prisoner turning off the lights in order to convey the impression that the crime had been committed in daylight?
10082-8{_}3651{_}"I want you to keep in mind, when arriving at your verdict, that there are certain material facts which have been admitted by the defence," said Mr. Walters in concluding his address to the jury.
10082-8{_}3652{_}"It has been admitted that the prisoner was a party to a proposal to break into Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3653{_}As far as that goes, there is no suggestion that he walked into a trap.
10082-8{_}3654{_}Whether he arranged the burglary and compelled Hill to help him, or whether Hill arranged it and sought out the prisoner's assistance is, after all, not very material.
10082-8{_}3655{_}What is admitted is that the prisoner went to Riversbrook with the intention of committing a crime.
10082-8{_}3656{_}It is admitted that he knew Sir Horace Fewbanks had returned home.
10082-8{_}3657{_}In that case is it not reasonable to suppose that the prisoner would arm himself, I do not say with the definite intention of committing murder, but for the purpose of threatening Sir Horace if necessary in order to make good his escape?
10082-8{_}3658{_}What is more likely than that Sir Horace heard the burglar in the house, crept upon him, and then tried to capture him?
10082-8{_}3659{_}There was a struggle, and the prisoner, determined to free himself, drew his revolver and shot Sir Horace.
10082-8{_}3660{_}Is not such a theory of the crime--that Sir Horace was shot while trying to capture the prisoner--more probable than the theory of the defence that Hill, the weak-willed, frightened-looking man you saw in the witness-box, was a masterful, cunning criminal who for some inexplicable reason had turned ferociously on the master who had befriended him and given him a fresh start in life, had killed him and left the body in the house, and had then managed to direct suspicion to the prisoner?
10082-8{_}3661{_}The theory of the defence does great credit to my learned friend's imagination, but it is one which I am sure the jury will reject as too highly coloured.
10082-8{_}3662{_}Looking at the plain facts of the case and dismissing from your minds the attempt to make them fit into a purely imaginative theory, I am sure that you will come to the conclusion that Sir Horace Fewbanks met his death at the hands of the prisoner."
10082-8{_}3663{_}The junior bar agreed that the case was one which might go either way.
10082-8{_}3664{_}If they had possessed any money the betting market would have shown scarcely a shade of odds.
10082-8{_}3665{_}Everything depended on the way the jury looked at the case, on the particular bits of evidence to which they attached most weight, on the view the most argumentative positive-minded members of the jury adopted, for they would be able to carry the others with them.
10082-8{_}3666{_}In the opinion of the junior bar the summing up of Mr. Justice Hodson would not help the jury very much in arriving at a verdict.
10082-8{_}3667{_}There were some judges who summed up for or against a prisoner according to the view they had formed as to the prisoner's guilt or innocence.
10082-8{_}3668{_}There were other judges who summed up so impartially and gave such even-balanced weight to the points against the prisoner and to the points in his favour, as to make on the minds of the jurymen the impression that the only way to arrive at a well-considered verdict was to toss a coin.
10082-8{_}3669{_}Another type of judge conveyed to the jury that the prosecution had established an unanswerable case, but the defence had shown equal skill in shattering it, and therefore he did not know on which side to make up his mind, and fortunately English legal procedure did not render it necessary for him to do so.
10082-8{_}3670{_}The prisoner might be guilty and he might be innocent.
10082-8{_}3671{_}Some of the jury might think one thing and the rest of the jury might think another.
10082-8{_}3672{_}But it was the duty of the jury to come to an unanimous verdict.
10082-8{_}3673{_}It did not matter if they looked at some things in different ways, but their final decision must be the same.
10082-8{_}3674{_}Mr. Justice Hodson belonged to the impartial, impersonal type of judge.
10082-8{_}3675{_}He had no personal feelings or conviction as to the guilt or innocence of the prisoner.
10082-8{_}3676{_}It was for the jury to settle that point and it was his duty to assist them to the best of his ability.
10082-8{_}3677{_}He went over his notes carefully and dealt with the evidence of each of the witnesses.
10082-8{_}3678{_}It was for the jury to say what evidence they believed and what they disbelieved.
10082-8{_}3679{_}There was a pronounced conflict of evidence between Hill and Fanning.
10082-8{_}3680{_}They were the chief witnesses in the case, but the guilt or innocence of the prisoner did not rest entirely upon the evidence of either of these witnesses.
10082-8{_}3681{_}Hill might be speaking the truth and the prisoner might be innocent though the presumption would be, if Hill's evidence were truthful in every detail, that the prisoner was guilty.
10082-8{_}3682{_}Fanning's evidence might be true as far as it went, but it would not in itself prove that the prisoner was innocent.
10082-8{_}3683{_}Hill had admitted that he had drawn the plan of Riversbrook to assist Birchill to commit burglary.
10082-8{_}3684{_}It was for the jury to determine for themselves whether he had been terrorised into drawing the plan for Birchill or whether he was the instigator of the burglary.
10082-8{_}3685{_}The defence had contended that Hill had drawn the plan at his leisure at a time when he had access to a special quality of paper supplied to his master.
10082-8{_}3686{_}If that were so, Hill's version of how he came to draw the plan was deliberately false and had been concocted for the purpose of exculpating himself.
10082-8{_}3687{_}But they would not be justified in dismissing Hill's evidence entirely from their minds because they were satisfied he had perjured himself with regard to the plan.
10082-8{_}3688{_}They would be justified, however, in viewing the rest of his evidence with some degree of distrust.
10082-8{_}3689{_}Counsel for the defence had made an ingenious use of the facts that the body of the victim was fully dressed when discovered and that none of the electric lights in the house were burning.
10082-8{_}3690{_}These facts lent support to the idea that the murder was committed in daylight, but they by no means established the theory as unassailable.
10082-8{_}3691{_}They did not establish the innocence of the prisoner, although to some extent they told in his favour.
10082-8{_}3692{_}Counsel for the prosecution had put before them several theories to account for these two facts consistent with his contention that the murder had been committed by the prisoner.
10082-8{_}3693{_}The jury must give full consideration to these theories as well as to the theory of the defence.
10082-8{_}3694{_}They were not called upon to say which theory was true except in so far as their opinions might be implied in the verdict they gave.
10082-8{_}3695{_}The defence, continued His Honour, was that Hill had committed the murder and had then decided to direct suspicion to the prisoner.
10082-8{_}3696{_}If the jury acquitted the prisoner, their verdict would not necessarily mean that they endorsed the theory of the defence.
10082-8{_}3697{_}It might mean that, but it might mean only that they were not satisfied that the prisoner had committed the murder.
10082-8{_}3698{_}If the jury were convinced beyond all reasonable doubt that the prisoner had committed the murder, they must bring in a verdict of "guilty," and if they were not satisfied they must bring in a verdict of acquittal.
10082-8{_}3699{_}The jury filed out of their apartment, and as they retired to consider their verdict the judge retired to his own room.
10082-8{_}3700{_}The prisoner was removed from the dock and taken down the stairs out of sight.
10082-8{_}3701{_}There was an immediate hum of voices in the court.
10082-8{_}3702{_}Inspector Chippenfield approached the table and whispered to Mr. Walters.
10082-8{_}3703{_}The latter nodded affirmatively and left the court room in company with Mr. Holymead.
10082-8{_}3704{_}The sibilant sound of whispering voices died down after a few minutes and then began the long tedious wait for the return of the jury.
10082-8{_}3705{_}The occupants of the gallery, who had no difficulty in coming to an immediate decision on the guilt or innocence of the prisoner, could not understand what was keeping the jury away so long.
10082-8{_}3706{_}They failed to understand the jury's point of view.
10082-8{_}3707{_}These gentlemen had sat in court for three days listening intently to proceedings concerning a matter in which their degree of personal interest was only a form of curiosity.
10082-8{_}3708{_}And now the end of the case had been reached, except for the climax, which was in their control.
10082-8{_}3709{_}To arrive at an immediate decision in a case that had occupied the court for three days would indicate they had no proper realisation of the responsibilities of their position.
10082-8{_}3710{_}A verdict was a thing that had to be nicely balanced in relation to the evidence.
10082-8{_}3711{_}Where the case against the prisoner was weak or overwhelmingly strong, the jury might arrive at a verdict with great speed as an indication that too much of their valuable time had already been wasted on the case.
10082-8{_}3712{_}But where the evidence for and against the prisoner was fairly equal it behoved the jury to indicate by the time they took in arriving at their verdict that they had given the case the most careful consideration.
10082-8{_}3713{_}Two hours and twenty minutes after the jury had retired, the prisoner was brought back into the dock.
10082-8{_}3714{_}This was an indication that the jury had arrived at their verdict and were ready to deliver it.
10082-8{_}3715{_}The prisoner looked worn and anxious, but he received encouraging smiles from his friends in the gallery.
10082-8{_}3716{_}A minute later the judge entered the court and resumed his seat.
10082-8{_}3717{_}The jury filed into court and entered the jury-box.
10082-8{_}3718{_}Amid the noise of barristers resuming their seats and court officials gliding about, the judge's Associate called over the names of the jurymen.
10082-8{_}3719{_}The suspense reached its climax as the Associate put the formal questions to the foreman whether the jury had agreed on their verdict.
10082-8{_}3720{_}"What say you: guilty or not guilty?"
10082-8{_}3721{_}asked the Associate in a hard metallic voice in which there was no trace of interest in the answer.
10082-8{_}3722{_}"Not guilty," replied the foreman.
10082-8{_}3723{_}There was a muffled cheer from the gallery, which was suppressed by the stentorian cry of the ushers, "Silence in the court!"
10082-8{_}3724{_}"A pack of damned fools," said the exasperated Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}3725{_}Rolfe understood that his chief referred to the jury, and he nodded the assent of a subordinate.
10082-8{_}3726{_}CHAPTER XX   "Hill has bolted!"
10082-8{_}3727{_}Rolfe flung the words at Inspector Chippenfield in a tone which he was unable to divest entirely of satisfaction.
10082-8{_}3728{_}"Fancy his being the guilty party after all," he added, with the tone of satisfaction still more evident in his voice.
10082-8{_}3729{_}"I often thought that he was our man, and that he was playing with you--I mean with us."
10082-8{_}3730{_}Inspector Chippenfield had betrayed surprise at the news by dropping his pen on the official report he was preparing.
10082-8{_}3731{_}But it was in his usual tone of cold official superiority that he replied:  "Do you mean that Hill, the principal witness in the Riversbrook murder trial, has disappeared from London?"
10082-8{_}3732{_}"Disappeared from London?
10082-8{_}3733{_}He's bolted clean out of the country by this time, I tell you!
10082-8{_}3734{_}Cleared out for good and left his unfortunate wife and child to starve."
10082-8{_}3735{_}"How have you learnt this, Rolfe?"
10082-8{_}3736{_}"His wife told me herself.
10082-8{_}3737{_}I went to the shop this afternoon to have a few words with Hill and see how he felt after the way Holymead had gone for him at the trial.
10082-8{_}3738{_}His wife burst out crying when she saw me, and she told me that her husband had cleared out last night after he came home from court.
10082-8{_}3739{_}The hardened scoundrel took with him the few pounds of her savings which she kept in her bedroom, and had even emptied the contents of the till of the few shillings and coppers it contained.
10082-8{_}3740{_}All he left were the half-pennies in the child's money-box.
10082-8{_}3741{_}He cleared out in the middle of the night after his wife had gone to bed.
10082-8{_}3742{_}He left her a note telling her she must get along without him.
10082-8{_}3743{_}I have the note here--his wife gave it to me."
10082-8{_}3744{_}Rolfe took a dirty scrap of paper out of his pocket-book and laid it before Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}3745{_}The paper was a half sheet torn from an exercise-book, and its contents were written in faint lead pencil.
10082-8{_}3746{_}They read:  "Dear Mary:  "I have got to leave you.
10082-8{_}3747{_}I have thought it out and this is the only thing to do.
10082-8{_}3748{_}I am too frightened to stay after what took place in the court to-day.
10082-8{_}3749{_}I'll make a fresh start in some place where I am not known, and as soon as I can send a little money I will send for you and Daphne.
10082-8{_}3750{_}Keep your heart up and it will be all right.
10082-8{_}3751{_}"Keep on the shop.
10082-8{_}3752{_}"YOUR LOVING HUSBAND."
10082-8{_}3753{_}"The poor little woman is heartbroken," continued Rolfe, when his superior officer had finished reading the note.
10082-8{_}3754{_}"She wants to know if we cannot get her husband back for her.
10082-8{_}3755{_}She says the shop won't keep her and the child.
10082-8{_}3756{_}Unless she can find her husband she'll be turned into the streets, because she's behind with the rent, and Hill's taken every penny she'd put by."
10082-8{_}3757{_}"Then she'd better go to the workhouse," retorted Inspector Chippenfield brutally.
10082-8{_}3758{_}"We'd have something to do if Scotland Yard undertook to trace all the absconding husbands in London.
10082-8{_}3759{_}We can do nothing in the matter, and you'd better tell her so."
10082-8{_}3760{_}Inspector Chippenfield handed back Hill's note as he spoke.
10082-8{_}3761{_}Rolfe eyed him in some surprise.
10082-8{_}3762{_}"But surely you're going to take out a warrant for Hill's arrest?"
10082-8{_}3763{_}he said.
10082-8{_}3764{_}"Certainly not," responded Inspector Chippenfield impatiently.
10082-8{_}3765{_}"I've already said that Scotland Yard has something more to do than trace absconding husbands.
10082-8{_}3766{_}There's nothing to prevent your giving a little of your private time to looking for him, Rolfe, if you feel so tender-hearted about the matter.
10082-8{_}3767{_}But officially--no.
10082-8{_}3768{_}I'm astonished at your suggesting such a thing."
10082-8{_}3769{_}"It isn't that," replied Rolfe, flushing a little, and speaking with slight embarrassment.
10082-8{_}3770{_}"But surely after Hill's flight you'll apply for a warrant for his arrest on--the other ground."
10082-8{_}3771{_}"On what other ground?"
10082-8{_}3772{_}asked his chief coldly.
10082-8{_}3773{_}"Why, on a charge of murdering Sir Horace Fewbanks," Rolfe burst out indignantly.
10082-8{_}3774{_}"Doesn't this flight point to his guilt?"
10082-8{_}3775{_}"Not in my opinion."
10082-8{_}3776{_}Inspector Chippenfield's voice was purely official.
10082-8{_}3777{_}"Why, surely it does!"
10082-8{_}3778{_}Rolfe's glance at his chief indicated that there was such a thing as carrying official obstinacy too far.
10082-8{_}3779{_}"This letter he left behind suggests his guilt, clearly enough."
10082-8{_}3780{_}"I didn't notice that," replied Inspector Chippenfield impassively.
10082-8{_}3781{_}"Perhaps you'll point out the passage to me, Rolfe."
10082-8{_}3782{_}Rolfe hastily produced the note again.
10082-8{_}3783{_}"Look here!"
10082-8{_}3784{_}--his finger indicated the place--"'I'm frightened to stay after what took place in the court to-day,' Doesn't that mean, clearly enough, that Hill realised the acquittal pointed to him as the murderer, and he determined to abscond before he could be arrested?"
10082-8{_}3785{_}"So that's your way of looking at it, eh, Rolfe?"
10082-8{_}3786{_}said Inspector Chippenfield quizzically.
10082-8{_}3787{_}"Certainly it is," responded Rolfe, not a little nettled by his chief's contemptuous tone.
10082-8{_}3788{_}"It's as plain as a pikestaff that the jury acquitted Birchill because they believed Hill was guilty.
10082-8{_}3789{_}Holymead made out too strong a case for them to get away from--Hill's lies about the plan and the fact that the body was fully dressed when discovered."
10082-8{_}3790{_}"You're a young man, Rolfe," responded Inspector Chippenfield in a tolerant tone, "but you'll have to shed this habit of jumping impulsively to conclusions--and generally wrong conclusions--if you want to succeed in Scotland Yard.
10082-8{_}3791{_}This letter of Hill's only strengthens my previous opinion that a damned muddle-headed jury let a cold-blooded murderer loose on the world when they acquitted Fred Birchill of the charge of shooting Sir Horace Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}3792{_}Why, man alive, Holymead no more believes Hill is guilty than I do.
10082-8{_}3793{_}He set himself to bamboozle the jury and he succeeded.
10082-8{_}3794{_}If he had to defend Hill to-morrow he would show the jury that Hill couldn't have committed the murder and that it must have been committed by Birchill and no one else.
10082-8{_}3795{_}He's a clever man, far cleverer than Walters, and that is why I lost the case."
10082-8{_}3796{_}"He led Hill into a trap about the plan of Riversbrook," said Rolfe.
10082-8{_}3797{_}"When I saw that Hill had been trapped on that point I felt we had lost the jury."
10082-8{_}3798{_}"Only because the jury were a pack of fools who knew nothing about evidence.
10082-8{_}3799{_}Granted that Hill lied about the plan--that he drew it up voluntarily in his spare time to assist Birchill--it proves nothing.
10082-8{_}3800{_}It doesn't prove that Hill committed the murder.
10082-8{_}3801{_}It only proves that Hill was going to share in the proceeds of the burglary; that he was a willing party to it.
10082-8{_}3802{_}The one big outstanding fact in all the evidence, the fact that towered over all the others, is that Birchill broke into the house on the night Sir Horace Fewbanks was murdered.
10082-8{_}3803{_}The defence made no attempt to get away from that fact because they could not do so.
10082-8{_}3804{_}But Holymead vamped up all sorts of surmises and suppositions for the purpose of befogging the jury and getting their minds away from the outstanding feature of the case for the prosecution.
10082-8{_}3805{_}We proved that Birchill was in the house on a criminal errand.
10082-8{_}3806{_}What more could they expect us to prove?
10082-8{_}3807{_}They couldn't expect us to have a man looking through the window or hiding behind the door when the murder was committed.
10082-8{_}3808{_}If we could get evidence of that kind we could do without juries.
10082-8{_}3809{_}We could hang our man first and try him afterwards.
10082-8{_}3810{_}I don't think a verdict of acquittal from a befogged jury would do so much harm in such a case."
10082-8{_}3811{_}"You are still convinced that Birchill did it?"
10082-8{_}3812{_}said Rolfe questioningly.
10082-8{_}3813{_}"I have never wavered from that opinion," said his superior.
10082-8{_}3814{_}"If I had, this note of Hill's would restore my conviction in Birchill's guilt."
10082-8{_}3815{_}"Why, how do you make out that?"
10082-8{_}3816{_}replied Rolfe blankly.
10082-8{_}3817{_}"Hill says he's clearing out of the country because he's frightened.
10082-8{_}3818{_}What's he frightened of?
10082-8{_}3819{_}His own guilty conscience and the long arm of the law?
10082-8{_}3820{_}Not a bit of it!
10082-8{_}3821{_}Hill's an innocent man.
10082-8{_}3822{_}If he had been guilty he'd never have stood the ordeal of the witness-box and the cross-examination.
10082-8{_}3823{_}Hill's cleared out because he was frightened of Birchill."
10082-8{_}3824{_}"Of Birchill?"
10082-8{_}3825{_}"Yes.
10082-8{_}3826{_}Didn't Birchill tell Hill, just before he set out for Riversbrook on the night of the murder, that if Hill played him false he'd murder him?
10082-8{_}3827{_}Hill _did_ play him false, not then, but afterwards, when he made his confession and Birchill was arrested for the murder in consequence.
10082-8{_}3828{_}When Birchill was acquitted at the trial his first thought would be to wreak vengeance on Hill.
10082-8{_}3829{_}A man with one murder on his soul would not be likely to hesitate about committing another.
10082-8{_}3830{_}Hill knew this, and fled to save his life when Birchill was acquitted.
10082-8{_}3831{_}That's the explanation of his letter, Rolfe."
10082-8{_}3832{_}"So that's the way you look at it?"
10082-8{_}3833{_}said Rolfe.
10082-8{_}3834{_}"Of course I do!
10082-8{_}3835{_}It's the only way Hill's flight can be looked at in the light of all that's happened.
10082-8{_}3836{_}The theory dovetails in every part.
10082-8{_}3837{_}I'm more used than you to putting these things together, Rolfe.
10082-8{_}3838{_}Hill's as innocent of the murder as you are."
10082-8{_}3839{_}"And where do you think Hill's gone to?"
10082-8{_}3840{_}"Certainly not out of London.
10082-8{_}3841{_}He's too much of a Cockney for that.
10082-8{_}3842{_}Besides, he's a man who is fond of his wife and child.
10082-8{_}3843{_}He's hiding somewhere close at hand, and I shouldn't wonder if the whole thing's a plant between him and his wife.
10082-8{_}3844{_}Have you forgotten how she tried to hoodwink us before?
10082-8{_}3845{_}I'll go to the shop to-morrow and see if I can't frighten the truth out of her.
10082-8{_}3846{_}Meanwhile, you'd better put the Camden Town police on to watching the shop.
10082-8{_}3847{_}If he's hiding in London he's bound to visit his wife sooner or later, or she'll visit him, so we ought not to have much difficulty in getting on to his tracks again."
10082-8{_}3848{_}Rolfe departed, to do his chief's bidding, a little crestfallen.
10082-8{_}3849{_}He was at first inclined to think that he had made a bit of a fool of himself in his desire to prove to Inspector Chippenfield that he had been hoodwinked by Hill into arresting Birchill.
10082-8{_}3850{_}But that night, as he sat in his bedroom smoking a quiet pipe, and reviewing this latest phase of the puzzling case, the earlier doubts which had assailed him on first learning of Hill's flight recurred to him with increasing force.
10082-8{_}3851{_}If Hill were innocent he would have been more likely to seek police protection before flight.
10082-8{_}3852{_}Hill's flight was hardly the action of an innocent man.
10082-8{_}3853{_}It pointed more to a guilty fear of his own skin, now that the man he had accused of the murder was free to seek vengeance.
10082-8{_}3854{_}Chippenfield's theory seemed plausible enough at first sight, but Rolfe now recalled that he knew nothing of the missing letters and Hill's midnight visit to Riversbrook to recover them.
10082-8{_}3855{_}Rolfe had concealed that episode from his superior officer because he lacked the courage to reveal to him how he had been hoodwinked by Mrs. Holymead's fainting fit the morning he was conducting his official inquiry at Riversbrook into the murder.
10082-8{_}3856{_}"It's an infernally baffling case," muttered Rolfe, refilling his pipe from a tin of tobacco on the mantelpiece, and walking up and down the cheap lodging-house drugget with rapid strides.
10082-8{_}3857{_}"If Birchill is not the murderer who is?
10082-8{_}3858{_}Is it Hill?"
10082-8{_}3859{_}He lit his pipe, closed the window, opened his pocket-book and sat down to peruse the notes he had taken during his investigation of Sir Horace Fewbanks's murder.
10082-8{_}3860{_}He read and re-read them, earnestly searching for a fresh clue in the pencilled pages.
10082-8{_}3861{_}After spending some time in this occupation he took a clean sheet of paper and a pencil, and copied afresh the following entries from his notebook:  August 19.
10082-8{_}3862{_}Went Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}3863{_}Saw Sir H.F.'s body.
10082-8{_}3864{_}Discovered fragment of lady's handkerchief clenched in right hand.
10082-8{_}3865{_}August 22.
10082-8{_}3866{_}Made inquiries handkerchief.
10082-8{_}3867{_}Unable find where purchased.
10082-8{_}3868{_}September 8.
10082-8{_}3869{_}Found Hill at Riversbrook searching Sir H.F.'s papers.
10082-8{_}3870{_}Told me about bundle of lady's letters tied up with pink ribbon which had been taken from secret drawer.
10082-8{_}3871{_}Says they disappeared morning after murder when investigation was taking place.
10082-8{_}3872{_}C.'s visitors that day: Dr. Slingsby / Seldon to arrange inquest / newspaper men / undertaker's representatives / Crewe.
10082-8{_}3873{_}C. saw one visitor alone, Hill says.
10082-8{_}3874{_}Mrs. H----, who fainted.
10082-8{_}3875{_}C. fetched glass of water, leaving her alone in room.
10082-8{_}3876{_}Hill suggests her letters indicate friendly relations between her and Sir H.F. Sir H.F. expected visit, probably from lady, night of murder.
10082-8{_}3877{_}Hurried Hill off when he returned from Scotland.
10082-8{_}3878{_}Mem: Inadvisable disclose this to C.  Underneath his entries of the case Rolfe had written finally:  Points to be remembered:  (1) Crewe said before the trial that Birchill was not the murderer and     would be acquitted.
10082-8{_}3879{_}Birchill was acquitted.
10082-8{_}3880{_}(2) Crewe suggested we had not got the whole truth out of Hill.
10082-8{_}3881{_}Hill     disappears the night after the trial.
10082-8{_}3882{_}Is Hill the murderer?
10082-8{_}3883{_}(3) The handkerchief and the letters point to a woman in the case,     although this was not brought out at the trial.
10082-8{_}3884{_}Is it possible that     woman is Mrs. H.?
10082-8{_}3885{_}Rolfe realised that the chief pieces of the puzzle were before him, but the difficulty was to put them together.
10082-8{_}3886{_}He felt sure there was a connection between these facts, which, if brought to light, would solve the Riversbrook mystery.
10082-8{_}3887{_}Without knowing it, he had been so influenced by Crewe's analysis of the case that he had practically given up the idea that Birchill had anything to do with the murder.
10082-8{_}3888{_}His real reason for going to Hill's shop that morning was to try and extract something from Hill which might put him on the track of the actual murderer.
10082-8{_}3889{_}He believed Hill knew more than he had divulged.
10082-8{_}3890{_}Hill, before his disappearance, had placed in his hands an important clue, if he only knew how to follow it up.
10082-8{_}3891{_}That incident of the missing letters must have some bearing on the case, if he could only elucidate it.
10082-8{_}3892{_}Should he disclose to Chippenfield Hill's story of the missing letters?
10082-8{_}3893{_}Rolfe dismissed the idea as soon as it crossed his mind.
10082-8{_}3894{_}He knew his superior officer sufficiently well to understand that he would be very angry to learn that he had been deceived by Mrs. Holymead, and, as she was outside the range of his anger, he would bear a grudge against his junior officer for discovering the deception which had been practised on him, and do all he could to block his promotion in Scotland Yard in consequence.
10082-8{_}3895{_}Apart from that, he could offer Chippenfield no excuse for not having told him before.
10082-8{_}3896{_}Should he consult Crewe?
10082-8{_}3897{_}Rolfe dismissed that thought also, but more reluctantly.
10082-8{_}3898{_}Hang it all, it was too humiliating for an accredited officer of Scotland Yard to consult a private detective!
10082-8{_}3899{_}Rolfe had acquired an unwilling respect for Crewe's abilities during the course of the investigations into the Riversbrook case, but he retained all the intolerance which regular members of the detective force feel for the private detectives who poach on their preserves.
10082-8{_}3900{_}Rolfe's professional jealousy was intensified in Crewe's case because of the brilliant successes Crewe had achieved during his career at the expense of the reputation of Scotland Yard.
10082-8{_}3901{_}Rolfe had an instinctive feeling that Crewe's mind was of finer quality than his own, and would see light where he only groped in darkness.
10082-8{_}3902{_}If Crewe had been his superior officer in Scotland Yard, Rolfe would have gone to him unhesitatingly and profited by his keener vision, but he could not do so in their existing relative positions.
10082-8{_}3903{_}He ransacked his brain for some other course.
10082-8{_}3904{_}After long consideration, Rolfe decided to go and see Mrs. Holymead and question her about the packet of letters which Hill declared she had removed from Riversbrook after the murder.
10082-8{_}3905{_}He realised that this was rather a risky course to pursue, for Mrs. Holymead was highly placed and could do him much harm if she got her husband to use his influence at the Home Office, for then he would have to admit that he had gone to her without the knowledge of his superior officer, on the statement of a discredited servant who had arranged a burglary in his master's house the night he was murdered.
10082-8{_}3906{_}Nevertheless, Rolfe decided to take the risk.
10082-8{_}3907{_}The chance of getting somewhere nearer the solution of the Riversbrook mystery was worth it, and what a feather in his cap it would be if he solved the mystery!
