10082-8{_}3908{_}He was convinced that Chippenfield had shut out important light on the mystery by doggedly insisting, in order to buttress up his case against Birchill, that the piece of handkerchief which had been found in the dead man's hand was a portion of a handkerchief which had belonged to the girl Fanning, and had been brought by Birchill from the Westminster flat on the night of the murder.
10082-8{_}3909{_}It was more likely, in view of Hill's story of the letters, that the handkerchief belonged to Mrs. Holymead.
10082-8{_}3910{_}Rolfe had not made up his mind that Mrs. Holymead had committed the murder, but he was convinced that she and her letters had some connection with the baffling crime, and he determined to try and pierce the mystery by questioning her.
10082-8{_}3911{_}Having arrived at this decision, he replaced his notebook in his coat pocket, knocked the ashes out of his pipe, and went to bed.
10082-8{_}3912{_}CHAPTER XXI   Rolfe went to Hyde Park next day and walked from the Tube station to Holymead's house at Princes Gate.
10082-8{_}3913{_}The servant who answered his ring informed him, in reply to his question, that Mrs. Holymead was "Not at home."
10082-8{_}3914{_}"Do you know when she will be home?"
10082-8{_}3915{_}persisted Rolfe, forestalling an evident desire on the servant's part to shut the door in his face.
10082-8{_}3916{_}The man looked at Rolfe doubtfully.
10082-8{_}3917{_}Well-trained English servant though he was, and used to summing up strangers at a glance, he could not quite make out who Rolfe might be.
10082-8{_}3918{_}But before he could come to a decision on the point a feminine voice behind him said:  "What is it, Trappon?"
10082-8{_}3919{_}The servant turned quickly in the direction of the voice.

10082-8{_}3923{_}"A person--a gentleman, mademoiselle," replied Trappon, determined to be on the safe side.

10082-8{_}3925{_}Trappon somewhat reluctantly complied, and a young lady stepped forward.
10082-8{_}3926{_}She was tall and dark, with charming eyes which were also shrewd; she had a fine figure which a tight-fitting dress displayed rather too boldly for good taste, and she was sufficiently young to be able to appear quite girlish in the half light.

10082-8{_}3930{_}Rolfe felt it incumbent upon him to be gallant in the presence of the fair representative of a nation whom he vaguely understood placed gallantry in the forefront of the virtues.
10082-8{_}3931{_}He took off his hat with a courtly bow.
10082-8{_}3932{_}"I do, mademoiselle," he replied, "and my business is important."
10082-8{_}3933{_}"Then, monsieur, step inside if you will be so good, and I will see you."

10082-8{_}3936{_}But this was precisely what Rolfe was not anxious to do except to Mrs. Holymead herself.

10082-8{_}3938{_}"I wish to see her."

10082-8{_}3942{_}Rolfe was a little taken aback at this intelligence, and showed it.

10082-8{_}3944{_}he repeated.

10082-8{_}4006{_}The glass reflected the actions of Rolfe at the table.
10082-8{_}4007{_}Seated with his back towards her, he had taken advantage of her being called to the telephone to examine her handkerchief, which he had picked up from the floor.
10082-8{_}4008{_}He had produced from his pocketbook the scrap of lace and muslin which he had found in the murdered man's hand.

10082-8{_}4010{_}While she looked he restored the scrap to his pocket-book, and the pocket-book to his pocket.

10082-8{_}4012{_}Then she hung up the receiver and turned to Rolfe.
10082-8{_}4013{_}"But, monsieur, you were saying--"  Rolfe handed the handkerchief to its owner with a courtly bow which he flattered himself was equal to the best French school.
10082-8{_}4014{_}"I picked this up off the floor, mademoiselle.

10082-8{_}4016{_}"This?"

10082-8{_}4019{_}I dropped it."

10082-8{_}4021{_}"I suppose you bought that in Paris.
10082-8{_}4022{_}It does not look English,''  "But no, monsieur, it is quite Engleesh.

10082-8{_}4029{_}He felt that he had acquitted himself with an adroitness which Crewe himself might have envied.

10082-8{_}4031{_}Rolfe knew from his inquiries in West End shops that handkerchiefs of that pattern and quality were stocked by many of the good shops, but the fact that he had found a handkerchief of this kind in the house of a lady who had abstracted secret letters from the murdered man's desk, and had, moreover, discovered the name of the shop where she bought her handkerchiefs, convinced him that he had struck a path which must lead to an important discovery.
10082-8{_}4032{_}Mademoiselle Chiron followed Rolfe into the hall and watched his departure from a front window.

10082-8{_}4074{_}CHAPTER XXII   "A lady to see you, sir."

10082-8{_}4095{_}The visitor did not immediately reply.

10082-8{_}4099{_}"And the boy?

10082-8{_}4102{_}"You are quite safe here, madame--mademoiselle, I should say," he added, with a quick glance at her left hand, from which she slowly removed the glove as she spoke.
10082-8{_}4103{_}"Mademoiselle Chiron, monsieur," said Gabrielle, flashing another smile at him.
10082-8{_}4104{_}"I am Madame Holymead's relative--her cousin.
10082-8{_}4105{_}I come to see you about the dreadful murder of the judge, Madame's friend."
10082-8{_}4106{_}"You come from Mrs.
10082-8{_}4107{_}Holymead?"
10082-8{_}4108{_}said Crewe quickly.
10082-8{_}4109{_}"Then, Mademoiselle Chiron, before--"  "No, no, monsieur, no!"
10082-8{_}4110{_}Her agitation was unmistakably genuine.
10082-8{_}4111{_}"I do not come _from_ Madame Holymead.
10082-8{_}4112{_}I am her relative, it is true, but I come--how shall I say it?--from myself.
10082-8{_}4113{_}I mean she does not know of my visit to you, monsieur."
10082-8{_}4114{_}"I quite understand," replied Crewe.
10082-8{_}4115{_}"Monsieur Crewe," said Gabrielle hurriedly, "although I have not come from Madame Holymead, it is for her sake that I come to see you--to save her from the persecution of one of your police agents who wants to ask her questions about this so sordid--so terrible a crime!
10082-8{_}4116{_}He has come once, this agent--last night he came--and he told me he wanted to question Madame Holymead about the murder of her dear friend the judge.
10082-8{_}4117{_}I do not want Madame worried with these questions, so I told him Madame was away in the motor in the country; but he says he will come again and again till he sees her.
10082-8{_}4118{_}Madame is distracted when she learns of his visit; it opens up her bleeding heart afresh, for she and her husband were _intime_ with the dead judge, and deeply, terribly, they deplore his so dreadful end.
10082-8{_}4119{_}I see Madame cry, and I say to myself I will not let this little police agent spoil her beauty and give her the migraine: his visits must be, shall be, prevented.
10082-8{_}4120{_}I have heard of the so great and good Monsieur Crewe, and I will go and see him.
10082-8{_}4121{_}We will--as you say in your English way--put our heads together, this famous detective and I, and we will find some way of--how do you call it?--circumventing this police agent so that my dear Madame shall cry no more.
10082-8{_}4122{_}Monsieur Crewe, I am here, and I beg of you to help me."
10082-8{_}4123{_}Crewe listened to this outburst with inward surprise but impassive features.
10082-8{_}4124{_}Apparently the police had come to the conclusion that they had blundered in arresting Birchill for the murder of Sir Horace Fewbanks, and had recommenced inquiries with a view to bringing the crime home to somebody else.
10082-8{_}4125{_}He did not know whether their suspicions were now directed against Mrs. Holymead, but they had conducted their preliminary inquiries so clumsily as to arouse her fears that they did.
10082-8{_}4126{_}So much was apparent from Mademoiselle Chiron's remarks, despite the interpretation she sought to place on Mrs. Holymead's fears.
10082-8{_}4127{_}He wondered if the "police agent" was Rolfe or Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}4128{_}It was obvious that the cool proposal that he should help to shield Mrs. Holymead against unwelcome police attentions covered some deeper move, and he shaped his conversation in the endeavour to extract more from the Frenchwoman.
10082-8{_}4129{_}"I am very sorry to hear that Mrs. Holymead has been subjected to this annoyance," he said warily.
10082-8{_}4130{_}"This police agent, did he come by himself?"
10082-8{_}4131{_}"But yes, monsieur, I have already said it."
10082-8{_}4132{_}"I know, but I thought he might have had a companion waiting for him in a taxi-cab outside.
10082-8{_}4133{_}Scotland Yard men frequently travel in pairs."
10082-8{_}4134{_}"He had no taxi-cab," declared Mademoiselle Chiron, positively.
10082-8{_}4135{_}"He walked away on foot by himself.
10082-8{_}4136{_}I watched him from the window."
10082-8{_}4137{_}Crewe registered a mental note of this admission.
10082-8{_}4138{_}If she had watched the detective's departure from the window she evidently had some reason for wanting to see the last of him.
10082-8{_}4139{_}Aloud he said:  "I expect I know him.
10082-8{_}4140{_}What was he like?"
10082-8{_}4141{_}"Tall, as tall as you, only bigger--much bigger.
10082-8{_}4142{_}And he had the great moustache which he caressed again and again with his fingers."
10082-8{_}4143{_}Gabrielle daintily imitated the action on her own short upper lip.
10082-8{_}4144{_}"I know him," declared Crewe with a smile.
10082-8{_}4145{_}"His name is Rolfe.
10082-8{_}4146{_}There should be nothing about him to alarm you, mademoiselle.
10082-8{_}4147{_}Why, he is quite a ladies' man."
10082-8{_}4148{_}Gabrielle shrugged her shoulders disdainfully.
10082-8{_}4149{_}"That may be," she replied; "but I like him not, and I do not wish him to worry Madame Holymead."
10082-8{_}4150{_}"But why not let him see Mrs.
10082-8{_}4151{_}Holymead?"
10082-8{_}4152{_}suggested Crewe, after a short pause.
10082-8{_}4153{_}"As he only wants to ask her a few short questions, it seems to me that would be the quickest way out of the difficulty, and would save you all the trouble and worry you speak of."
10082-8{_}4154{_}"I tell you I will not," declared Gabrielle vehemently.
10082-8{_}4155{_}"I will not have Madame Holymead worried and made ill with the terrible ordeal.
10082-8{_}4156{_}Bah!
10082-8{_}4157{_}What do you men--so clumsy--know of the delicate feelings of a lady like Madame Holymead?
10082-8{_}4158{_}The least soupon of excitement and she is disturbed, distraite, for days.
10082-8{_}4159{_}After last night--after the visit of the police agent--she was quite hysterical."
10082-8{_}4160{_}"Why should she be when she had nothing to be afraid of?"
10082-8{_}4161{_}rejoined Crewe.
10082-8{_}4162{_}He spoke in a tone of simple wonder, but Gabrielle shot a quick glance at him from under her veiled lashes as she replied:  "Bah!
10082-8{_}4163{_}What has that to do with it?
10082-8{_}4164{_}I repeat: Monsieur Crewe, you men cannot understand the feelings of a lady like Madame Holymead in a matter like this.
10082-8{_}4165{_}She and her husband were, as I have said before, _intime_ with the great judge.
10082-8{_}4166{_}They visited his house, they dined with him, they met him in Society.
10082-8{_}4167{_}Behold, he is brutally, horribly killed.
10082-8{_}4168{_}Madame, when she hears the terrible news, is ill for days; she cannot eat, she cannot sleep; she can interest herself in nothing.
10082-8{_}4169{_}She is forgetting a little when the police agents they catch a man and say he is the murderer.
10082-8{_}4170{_}Then comes the trial of this man at the court with so queer a name--Old Bailee.
10082-8{_}4171{_}The papers are full of the terrible story again; of the dead man; how he looked killed; how he lay in a pool of blood; how they cut him open!
10082-8{_}4172{_}Madame Holymead cannot pick up a paper without seeing these things, and she falls ill again.
10082-8{_}4173{_}Then the jury say the man the police agents caught is not the murderer.
10082-8{_}4174{_}He goes free, and once more the talk dies away.
10082-8{_}4175{_}Madame Holymead once more begins to forget, when this police agent comes to her house to remind her once more all about it.
10082-8{_}4176{_}It is too cruel, monsieur, it is too cruel!"
10082-8{_}4177{_}Gabrielle's voice vibrated with indignation as she concluded, and Crewe regarded her closely.
10082-8{_}4178{_}He decided that her affection for Mrs. Holymead was not simulated, and that it would be best to handle her from that point of view.
10082-8{_}4179{_}"I am sorry," he said coldly, "but I do not see how I can help you."
10082-8{_}4180{_}"Monsieur," said the Frenchwoman, clasping her hands, "I entreat you not to say so.
10082-8{_}4181{_}It would be so easy for you to help--not me, but Madame."
10082-8{_}4182{_}"How?"
10082-8{_}4183{_}"You know this police agent.
10082-8{_}4184{_}You also are a police agent, though so much greater.
10082-8{_}4185{_}Therefore you whisper just one little word in the ear of your friend the police agent, and he will not bother Madame Holymead again.
10082-8{_}4186{_}I think you could do this.
10082-8{_}4187{_}And if you need money to give to the police agent, why, I have brought some."
10082-8{_}4188{_}She fumbled nervously at her hand-bag.
10082-8{_}4189{_}"Stay," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}4190{_}"What you ask is impossible.
10082-8{_}4191{_}I have nothing whatever to do with Scotland Yard.
10082-8{_}4192{_}I could not interfere in their inquiries, even if I wished to.
10082-8{_}4193{_}They would only laugh at me."
10082-8{_}4194{_}Gabrielle's dark eyes showed her disappointment, but she made one more effort to gain her end.
10082-8{_}4195{_}She leant nearer to Crewe, and laid a persuasive hand on his arm.
10082-8{_}4196{_}"If you would only make the effort," she said coaxingly, "my beautiful Madame Holymead would be for ever grateful."
10082-8{_}4197{_}"Mademoiselle, once more I repeat that what you ask is impossible," returned Crewe decisively.
10082-8{_}4198{_}"I repeat, I cannot see why Mrs. Holymead should object to answering a few questions the police wish to ask her.
10082-8{_}4199{_}She is too sensitive about such a trifle."
10082-8{_}4200{_}Gabrielle shrugged her shoulders slightly in tacit recognition of the fact that the man in front of her was too shrewd to be deceived by subterfuge.
10082-8{_}4201{_}"There is another reason, monsieur," she whispered.
10082-8{_}4202{_}"You had better tell it to me."
10082-8{_}4203{_}"If you had been a woman you would have guessed.
10082-8{_}4204{_}The great judge who was killed was in his spare moments what you call a gallant--he did love my sex.
10082-8{_}4205{_}In France this would not matter, but in England they think much of it--so very much.
10082-8{_}4206{_}Madame Holymead is frightened for fear the least breath of scandal should attach to her name, if the world knew that the police agent had visited her house on such an errand.
10082-8{_}4207{_}Madame is innocent--it is not necessary to assure you of that; but the prudish dames of England are censorious."
10082-8{_}4208{_}"The Scotland Yard people are not likely to disclose anything about it," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}4209{_}"That may be so, but these things come out," retorted Gabrielle.
10082-8{_}4210{_}"Monsieur," she added, after a pause, and speaking in a low tone, "I know that you can do much--very much--if you will, and can stop Madame Holymead from being worried.
10082-8{_}4211{_}Would you do so if you were told who the murderer was--I mean he who did really kill the great judge?"
10082-8{_}4212{_}Crewe was genuinely surprised, but his control over his features was so complete that he did not betray it.
10082-8{_}4213{_}"Do you know who Sir Horace Fewbanks's murderer is?"
10082-8{_}4214{_}he asked, in quiet even tones.
10082-8{_}4215{_}"Monsieur, I do.
10082-8{_}4216{_}I will tell you the whole story in secret--how do you say?--in confidence, if you promise me you will help Madame Holymead as I have asked you."
10082-8{_}4217{_}"I cannot enter into a bargain like that," rejoined Crewe.
10082-8{_}4218{_}"I do not know whether Mrs. Holymead may not be implicated--concerned--in what you say."
10082-8{_}4219{_}"Monsieur, she is not!"
10082-8{_}4220{_}flashed Gabrielle indignantly.
10082-8{_}4221{_}"She knows nothing about it.
10082-8{_}4222{_}What I have to tell you concerns myself alone."
10082-8{_}4223{_}"In that case," rejoined Crewe, "I think you had better speak to me frankly and freely, and if I can I will help you."
10082-8{_}4224{_}"You are perhaps right," she replied.
10082-8{_}4225{_}"I will tell you everything, provided you give me your word of honour that you will not inform the police of what I will tell you."
10082-8{_}4226{_}"If you bind me to that promise I do not see how I can help you in the direction you indicate," said Crewe, after a moment's thought.
10082-8{_}4227{_}"If the police are asked to abandon their inquiries about Mrs. Holymead, they will naturally wish to know the reason."
10082-8{_}4228{_}"You are quite right," said Gabrielle.
10082-8{_}4229{_}"I did not think of that.
10082-8{_}4230{_}But if I tell you everything, and you have to tell the police agents so as to help Madame, will you promise that the police agents do not come and arrest _me_?"
10082-8{_}4231{_}"Provided you have not committed murder or been in any way accessory to it, I think I can promise you that," rejoined Crewe.
10082-8{_}4232{_}"Monsieur, I do not understand you, but I can almost divine your meaning.
10082-8{_}4233{_}Your promise is what you call a guarded one.
10082-8{_}4234{_}Nevertheless, I like your face, and I will trust you."
10082-8{_}4235{_}Gabrielle relapsed into silence for some moments, looking at Crewe earnestly.
10082-8{_}4236{_}"Monsieur," she said at length, "it is a terrible story I have to relate, and it is difficult for me to tell a stranger what I know.
10082-8{_}4237{_}Nevertheless, I will begin.
10082-8{_}4238{_}I knew the great judge well."
10082-8{_}4239{_}"You knew Sir Horace Fewbanks?"
10082-8{_}4240{_}exclaimed Crewe.
10082-8{_}4241{_}"He was--my lover, monsieur."
10082-8{_}4242{_}She brought the last two words out defiantly, with a quick glance at Crewe to see how he took the avowal.
10082-8{_}4243{_}She seemed to find something reassuring in his answering glance, and she continued, in more even tones:  "I had often seen him at the house of Madame Holymead when I came to London to visit her.
10082-8{_}4244{_}I admired Sir Horace when I saw him--often he used to call and dine, for he was the friend of Monsieur Holymead.
10082-8{_}4245{_}But Madame told me that the great judge was what in England you call a lover of the ladies--that he was dangerous--so I must be careful of him.
10082-8{_}4246{_}I used to look at him when he called, and thought he was handsome in the English way, and sometimes he looked at me when he was unobserved, and smiled at me.
10082-8{_}4247{_}But Madame did not like me looking at him; she said I was foolish; she warned me to be careful."
10082-8{_}4248{_}Gabrielle shrugged her shoulders expressively.
10082-8{_}4249{_}"Of what use was Madame's warning?
10082-8{_}4250{_}It did but make me wish to know more of this great lover of my sex.
10082-8{_}4251{_}He saw that, and made the opportunity, and made love to me.
10082-8{_}4252{_}He was so ardent, so fervid a lover that I was conquered.
10082-8{_}4253{_}"After we had been lovers I told him my secret--that I was married.
10082-8{_}4254{_}Pierre Simon, my husband, was a bad man, and so I left him.
10082-8{_}4255{_}But Madame must not know that I was married, for that is my secret.
10082-8{_}4256{_}It does not do to tell everything--besides, it would have distressed her.
10082-8{_}4257{_}"Monsieur, I was happy with my lover, the great judge.
10082-8{_}4258{_}He was charming.
10082-8{_}4259{_}He had that charm of manner which you English lack.
10082-8{_}4260{_}Faithful?
10082-8{_}4261{_}I do not know.
10082-8{_}4262{_}Often we were together, and often we wrote letters when to meet was impossible.
10082-8{_}4263{_}He kept my letters--they amused him so, he said--they were so French, so piquant, so different to English ladies' letters.
10082-8{_}4264{_}Alas, monsieur, there had been others--many others there must have been, for he understood my sex so well.
10082-8{_}4265{_}"One afternoon I was out for a walk looking in the great shops in Regent Street, when I felt a hand placed on my shoulder, and looking round I saw Pierre, my husband.
10082-8{_}4266{_}He was pleased at the meeting, but I was not pleased.
10082-8{_}4267{_}He took me to a caf where we could talk.
10082-8{_}4268{_}It was what he always did talk about--money, money, money.
10082-8{_}4269{_}He always wanted money.
10082-8{_}4270{_}He said I must find him some, and when I told him I had none he said I must find some way of getting it, or he would come to the house and expose my secret.
10082-8{_}4271{_}I walked away out of the caf and left him there.
10082-8{_}4272{_}But I soon saw him again, and again.
10082-8{_}4273{_}He followed me and talked to me against my will.
10082-8{_}4274{_}"Monsieur, I was very much distressed, and for a long time I tried to think of a way to get rid of Pierre, for I was afraid that he would come to the house and tell Madame Holymead I was married.
10082-8{_}4275{_}Then I thought of the great judge, my lover.
10082-8{_}4276{_}He would know how to send Pierre away, for Pierre would be frightened of him.
10082-8{_}4277{_}But Sir Horace was in Scotland, shooting the poor birds.
10082-8{_}4278{_}But I wrote to him and asked him for my sake to come at once, because I was in distress and needed help.
10082-8{_}4279{_}Monsieur, he came--but he came to his death.
10082-8{_}4280{_}He sent me a letter to meet him at Riversbrook at half-past ten o'clock.
10082-8{_}4281{_}He was sorry it was so late, but he thought it would be safer not to come to the house till after dark in the long summer evening, for people were so censorious.
10082-8{_}4282{_}I was to tell Madame Holymead that I was going to the theatre with a friend.
10082-8{_}4283{_}"I was so pleased to think that I would get rid of Pierre, that on the morning, when he stopped me to ask me again about the money, I showed him the letter of the great judge, and told him I would make the judge put him in prison if he did not go away and leave me alone.
10082-8{_}4284{_}'He is your lover,' said Pierre.
10082-8{_}4285{_}'I will kill him.'
10082-8{_}4286{_}But I laughed, for I knew Pierre did not care if I had many lovers.
10082-8{_}4287{_}I said to him, 'Pierre, you would extort the money'--blackmail, the English call it, do they not, Monsieur Crewe?--'but you would not kill.
10082-8{_}4288{_}Sir Horace is not afraid of you.
10082-8{_}4289{_}If you go near him he would have you taken off to gaol,' But Pierre he was deep in thought.
10082-8{_}4290{_}Several times he said, 'I want money,' Each time I said to him, 'Then you must work for it,' 'That is no way to get money,' he answered.
10082-8{_}4291{_}'This great judge, he has much money, is it not so?'
10082-8{_}4292{_}"I left him, monsieur, thinking of money.
10082-8{_}4293{_}But I did not know how bad his thoughts were.
10082-8{_}4294{_}I returned home, and I told Madame Holymead I would go to the theatre that night.
10082-8{_}4295{_}I left the house at eight o'clock, and after walking along Piccadilly and Regent Street took the train to Hampstead.
10082-8{_}4296{_}Then I walked up to the house of Sir Horace so as not to be too early.
10082-8{_}4297{_}The gate was open and I thought that strange, but I had no thought of murder.
10082-8{_}4298{_}As I walked up the garden I heard a shot--two shots--and then a cry, and the sound of something falling on the floor.
10082-8{_}4299{_}The door of the house was open, and the light was burning in the hall.
10082-8{_}4300{_}Upstairs I heard the noise of footsteps--quick footsteps--and then I heard them coming down the staircase.
10082-8{_}4301{_}I was afraid, and I hid myself behind the curtains in the hall.
10082-8{_}4302{_}The footsteps came down, and nearer and nearer, and when they passed me I looked out to see.
10082-8{_}4303{_}Monsieur, it was Pierre.
10082-8{_}4304{_}I called to him softly, 'Pierre, Pierre!'
10082-8{_}4305{_}He looked round, and his face, it was so different--so dreadful.
10082-8{_}4306{_}He did not know my voice, and he ran away from me with a cry.
10082-8{_}4307{_}"Monsieur, my heart is a brave one.
10082-8{_}4308{_}I have not what you call nerves, but when I knew I was alone in the great house with I knew not what, a great fear clutched me.
10082-8{_}4309{_}I stood still in the hall with my eyes fixed on the stairs above.
10082-8{_}4310{_}At first all was silent, then I heard a dreadful sound--a groan.
10082-8{_}4311{_}I wanted to run away then, monsieur, but the good God commanded me to go up and into the room, where a fellow creature needed me.
10082-8{_}4312{_}I went upstairs, and along to the door of a room which was half open.
10082-8{_}4313{_}I pushed it wide open and went in.
10082-8{_}4314{_}"_Mon Dieu!_ the judge was alone there, dying.
10082-8{_}4315{_}Pierre had shot him.
10082-8{_}4316{_}He lay along the floor, gasping, groaning, and the blood dripping from his breast.
10082-8{_}4317{_}When I saw this I ran forward and took his poor head on my knee, and tried to stop the blood with my handkerchief.
10082-8{_}4318{_}But as I did this the judge groaned once more.
10082-8{_}4319{_}He knew me not, though I called him by name.
10082-8{_}4320{_}In terrible agony he writhed his head off my breast.
10082-8{_}4321{_}His hand clutched at the hole in his breast, closing on my handkerchief.
10082-8{_}4322{_}And so he died.
10082-8{_}4323{_}"Monsieur, strange it may seem, but I do assure you that I became calm again when he was dead.
10082-8{_}4324{_}I rose to my feet and looked round me in the room.
10082-8{_}4325{_}On the floor near him I saw a revolver.
10082-8{_}4326{_}I picked it up and hid it in my bag.
10082-8{_}4327{_}The tube of it was warm.
10082-8{_}4328{_}Then I sat down in a chair and thought what I must do.
10082-8{_}4329{_}The police must not know I was there.
10082-8{_}4330{_}They must not know he was my lover.
10082-8{_}4331{_}I thought of my letters that I wrote to him.
10082-8{_}4332{_}He had them hidden in a little drawer at the back of his desk--a secret drawer.
10082-8{_}4333{_}Often had he showed me my letters there, and once he had showed me where to find the spring that opened the drawer.
10082-8{_}4334{_}So I searched for the spring and I found it.
10082-8{_}4335{_}The drawer opened and there were my letters tied together.
10082-8{_}4336{_}I took them all and hid them in my bag, and then I closed the hiding place.
10082-8{_}4337{_}There remained but the handkerchief which my lover held in his hand.
10082-8{_}4338{_}I tried to get it out, but I could not.
10082-8{_}4339{_}In my hurry I dragged it out--it came away then, but left a little bit in his hand.
10082-8{_}4340{_}It did not show.
10082-8{_}4341{_}I dared not wait longer.
10082-8{_}4342{_}I turned out the light, and hurried out of the room and downstairs.
10082-8{_}4343{_}Again I turned out the light, and closed the door, and hurried away.
10082-8{_}4344{_}"That, monsieur, is my story."
10082-8{_}4345{_}CHAPTER XXIII   As Gabrielle finished her story, she cast a quick glance at Crewe's face as though seeking to divine his decision.
10082-8{_}4346{_}But apparently she could read nothing there, and with an imperious gesture she exclaimed:  "You will do what I ask now that I have exposed my secret--my shame to you--and told everything?
10082-8{_}4347{_}You will save Madame Holymead from being persecuted by these police agents?"
10082-8{_}4348{_}"I must ask you a few questions first."
10082-8{_}4349{_}The contrast between the detective's quiet English tones and the Frenchwoman's impetuous appeal was accentuated by the methodical way in which Crewe slowly jotted down an entry in his open notebook.
10082-8{_}4350{_}Her dark eyes sparkled in an agony of impatience as she watched him.
10082-8{_}4351{_}"Ask them quick, monsieur, for I burn in the suspense."
10082-8{_}4352{_}"In the first place, then, have you any--"  "Hold, monsieur!
10082-8{_}4353{_}I know what you would ask!
10082-8{_}4354{_}You would say if I have any proofs?
10082-8{_}4355{_}Stupid that I am to forget things so important.
10082-8{_}4356{_}I have brought you the proofs."
10082-8{_}4357{_}She fumbled at the clasp of her hand-bag, as she spoke, and before she had finished speaking she had torn it open and emptied its contents on the table in front of Crewe--a dainty handkerchief and a revolver.
10082-8{_}4358{_}"See, monsieur!"
10082-8{_}4359{_}she cried; "here is the handkerchief of which I told you.
10082-8{_}4360{_}It is that which the judge seized when I tried to stop the blood flowing in his breast--look at the corner and you will see that a little bit has been torn off by his almost dead hand.
10082-8{_}4361{_}And the revolver--it is that which I picked up on the floor near him.
10082-8{_}4362{_}I have had it locked up ever since."
10082-8{_}4363{_}Crewe examined both articles closely.
10082-8{_}4364{_}The revolver was a small, nickel-plated weapon with silver chasing, with the murdered man's initials engraved in the handle.
10082-8{_}4365{_}It had five chambers, and one of the cartridges had been discharged.
10082-8{_}4366{_}The other four chambers were still loaded.
10082-8{_}4367{_}Crewe carefully extracted the cartridges, and examined them closely.
10082-8{_}4368{_}One of them he held up to the light in order to inspect it more minutely.
10082-8{_}4369{_}"Did you do this?"
10082-8{_}4370{_}he asked: "Have you been trying to fire off the revolver?"
10082-8{_}4371{_}"No, no, monsieur," she exclaimed quickly.
10082-8{_}4372{_}"I would not fire it, I do not understand it.
10082-8{_}4373{_}I have been careful not to touch the little thing that sets it going."
10082-8{_}4374{_}"The trigger," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}4375{_}He again studied the cartridge that had attracted his attention.
10082-8{_}4376{_}It had missed fire, for on the cap was a dint where the hammer had struck it.
10082-8{_}4377{_}He placed the four cartridges on the table and turning his attention to the handkerchief examined it minutely.
10082-8{_}4378{_}It was one of those filmy scraps of muslin and lace which ladies call a handkerchief--an article whose cost is out of all proportion to its usefulness.
10082-8{_}4379{_}Gabrielle, who was watching him keenly as he examined it, exclaimed:  "The handkerchief--a box of them--were given me by Sir Horace because he knew I love pretty things."
10082-8{_}4380{_}She laid a finger on the missing corner, which might indeed have been torn off in the manner described.
10082-8{_}4381{_}A scrap of the lace was missing, and it was evident that it had been removed with violence, for the lace around the gap was loosened, and the muslin slightly frayed.
10082-8{_}4382{_}"You say that the corner was torn off when you wrenched the handkerchief from the dead man's hold?"
10082-8{_}4383{_}said Crewe.
10082-8{_}4384{_}"But it was not found in his hand by the police or anyone else.
10082-8{_}4385{_}And he was not buried with it, for I examined the body carefully.
10082-8{_}4386{_}What became of it?"
10082-8{_}4387{_}Gabrielle looked at him quickly as though she suspected some trap.
10082-8{_}4388{_}"You would play with me," she said at length.
10082-8{_}4389{_}"What became of it?
10082-8{_}4390{_}Why, you must surely know that the police of Scot--Scotland Yard have it.
10082-8{_}4391{_}The police agent who called on Madame had it.
10082-8{_}4392{_}What is his name--Rudolf?"
10082-8{_}4393{_}"Rolfe?"
10082-8{_}4394{_}exclaimed Crewe.
10082-8{_}4395{_}"Has he got it?"
10082-8{_}4396{_}"Yes," she replied.
10082-8{_}4397{_}"He did not show it to me, but I saw it nevertheless.
10082-8{_}4398{_}I dropped my handkerchief when I spoke at the telephone and Monsieur Rolfe picked it up.
10082-8{_}4399{_}Quickly he studied my handkerchief--not this one, monsieur, but one of the same kind--and from his pocket-book he took out the missing piece that was in the dead man's hand and he studied them side by side.
10082-8{_}4400{_}He thought I did not see--that my back was turned--but I saw in the mirror which hung on the wall.
10082-8{_}4401{_}Then, when I finished my telephone, he bowed and said, 'Your handkerchief, mademoiselle.'
10082-8{_}4402{_}It was not so badly done--for a clumsy police agent."
10082-8{_}4403{_}She was not able to recognise how keen was Crewe's interest in her statement, but she saw that she had pleased him.
10082-8{_}4404{_}"It is because of this that he will come again," she continued.
10082-8{_}4405{_}"It is because of this that he would question Madame Holymead.
10082-8{_}4406{_}And then what will happen?
10082-8{_}4407{_}I do not know.
10082-8{_}4408{_}The police make so many mistakes--blunders you English call them.
10082-8{_}4409{_}Would they arrest her with their blunders?
10082-8{_}4410{_}That is why I come to you to ask you to save her."
10082-8{_}4411{_}"May I have the revolver and the handkerchief?"
10082-8{_}4412{_}asked Crewe.
10082-8{_}4413{_}"I will take great care of them."
10082-8{_}4414{_}"They are at your disposal, for you will use them to confront the police agent."
10082-8{_}4415{_}Crewe again examined the articles in silence before taking them to his secrtaire and locking them up in one of the pigeon-holes.
10082-8{_}4416{_}Then he turned to Gabrielle, whose large luminous eyes met his unhesitatingly.
10082-8{_}4417{_}She even smiled slightly--a frank engaging smile, as she remarked:  "And now, monsieur, any more questions?"
10082-8{_}4418{_}Crewe smiled back at her.
10082-8{_}4419{_}"You have told a remarkable story, mademoiselle, and corroborated it with two important pieces of evidence, which are in themselves almost sufficient to carry conviction," he said.
10082-8{_}4420{_}"But the Scotland Yard police are a suspicious lot, and it is necessary for me to have further information in order to convince them--if I am to help you as you wish."
10082-8{_}4421{_}Gabrielle flashed a look of gratitude at Crewe.
10082-8{_}4422{_}She understood from his words that he believed her story and was disposed to help her, although the police of Scotland Yard might prove harder to convince than him.
10082-8{_}4423{_}"Bah!
10082-8{_}4424{_}those police agents--they are the same everywhere," she exclaimed.
10082-8{_}4425{_}"They deal so much with crime that their minds get the taint, and between the false and true they cannot tell the difference.
10082-8{_}4426{_}_Que voulez-vous?_ They are but small in brains.
10082-8{_}4427{_}With you, the case is different.
10082-8{_}4428{_}You have it here--and there."
10082-8{_}4429{_}She touched her temples lightly with a finger of each hand.
10082-8{_}4430{_}"Proceed, monsieur: ask me what questions you will.
10082-8{_}4431{_}I shall endeavour to answer them."
10082-8{_}4432{_}"You said that as you were hiding behind the curtains on the stairway landing, Pierre, your husband, rushed down past you.
10082-8{_}4433{_}You are quite sure it was he?"
10082-8{_}4434{_}"Of that, monsieur, unfortunately there is no doubt.
10082-8{_}4435{_}I saw his face quite distinctly when he passed me, and when he turned round."
10082-8{_}4436{_}"The light would be shining from behind, and would not reveal his face very closely," suggested Crewe.
10082-8{_}4437{_}"Nevertheless, monsieur, it was quite sufficient for me to see Pierre clearly.
10082-8{_}4438{_}His head was half-turned as he ran, as though he was looking back expecting to see the judge rise up and punish him for his dreadful deed, and I saw him _en silhouette_, oh, most distinctly--impossible him to mistake.
10082-8{_}4439{_}I called softly--'Pierre!'
10082-8{_}4440{_}just like that, and he turned his face right round, and then with a cry he disappeared along the path."
10082-8{_}4441{_}"About what time was this?"
10082-8{_}4442{_}"The time--it was half-past ten, for that was the time I was to be there according to the letter the judge sent me."
10082-8{_}4443{_}"But are you sure it was half-past ten?
10082-8{_}4444{_}Weren't you early?
10082-8{_}4445{_}Wasn't it just about ten o'clock?"
10082-8{_}4446{_}"No, monsieur," she replied sadly.
10082-8{_}4447{_}"If it had been ten o'clock I would have been in time to save the life of my lover--to prevent this great tragedy which brings grief to so many."
10082-8{_}4448{_}Crewe looked at her sharply, and then nodded his head in acquiescence of the fact that much misery would have been averted if she had been in time to save the life of Sir Horace Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}4449{_}"When you went into the room, Sir Horace Fewbanks, you say, was lying on the floor, dying.
10082-8{_}4450{_}Whereabouts in the room was he?"
10082-8{_}4451{_}"If he had been in this room he would have been lying just behind you, with his head to the wall and his feet pointing towards that window.
10082-8{_}4452{_}He struggled and groaned after I went in, and altered his position a little, but not much.
10082-8{_}4453{_}He died so."
10082-8{_}4454{_}Crewe rapidly reviewed his recollection of the room in which the judge had been killed.
10082-8{_}4455{_}Once again Gabrielle's statement tallied with his own reconstruction of the crime and the manner of its perpetration.
10082-8{_}4456{_}If the murder had been committed in his office the second bullet would have gone through the window instead of imbedding itself in the wall, and the judge would have fallen in the spot where she indicated.
10082-8{_}4457{_}"And where was the writing-desk from where you got your letters?"
10082-8{_}4458{_}was Crewe's next question.
10082-8{_}4459{_}"It was over there--almost by that--your little bookcase there."
10082-8{_}4460{_}She pointed to a small oaken bookstand which stood slightly in advance of the more imposing shelves in which reposed the portentous volumes of newspaper clippings and photographs which constituted Crewe's "Rogues' Library."
10082-8{_}4461{_}"Now we come to the letters.
10082-8{_}4462{_}You took them from the secret drawer in the desk.
10082-8{_}4463{_}Why did you remove them?"
10082-8{_}4464{_}"Because I would not have the police agents find them, for then they would want to know so much."
10082-8{_}4465{_}"And what did you do with them?"
10082-8{_}4466{_}"Monsieur Crewe, I destroyed them.
10082-8{_}4467{_}When I got home I burnt them all--I was so frightened."
10082-8{_}4468{_}"You mean you were frightened to keep them in your possession after the judge was killed?"
10082-8{_}4469{_}"Yes.
10082-8{_}4470{_}What place had I to keep them safe from prying eyes?
10082-8{_}4471{_}So, monsieur, I burnt them all--one by one--and the charred fragments I kept and took into the Park next day, where I scattered them unobserved."
10082-8{_}4472{_}"And what became of the letter you wrote to Sir Horace Fewbanks at Craigleith Hall, asking him to come to London and save you from your husband's persecutions?"
10082-8{_}4473{_}She looked at him earnestly in the endeavour to ascertain if he had laid a trap for her.
10082-8{_}4474{_}"Sir Horace destroyed it in Scotland, I suppose, if the police did not find it."
10082-8{_}4475{_}"Strange that he should have kept all your other letters so carefully and destroyed that one.
10082-8{_}4476{_}Perhaps it was in his pocket-book that was stolen."
10082-8{_}4477{_}"I do not know.
10082-8{_}4478{_}What does it matter?
10082-8{_}4479{_}It has gone."
10082-8{_}4480{_}She shrugged her shoulders lightly and indifferently.
10082-8{_}4481{_}"Do you know who stole the pocket-book?"
10082-8{_}4482{_}"No, monsieur.
10082-8{_}4483{_}I thought it was stolen in the train."
10082-8{_}4484{_}"That is the police theory," replied Crewe.
10082-8{_}4485{_}"But let that go.
10082-8{_}4486{_}Have you, since the night of the murder, seen anything of Pierre?"
10082-8{_}4487{_}"Monsieur, I have not.
10082-8{_}4488{_}It is as though the earth has him swallowed.
10082-8{_}4489{_}He keeps silent with the silence of the grave."
10082-8{_}4490{_}"He is wise to do so," responded Crewe.
10082-8{_}4491{_}"Now, mademoiselle, I have no more questions to ask you.
10082-8{_}4492{_}Your confidence is safe; you need be under no apprehensions on that score."
10082-8{_}4493{_}"I care not for myself, Monsieur Crewe, so long as Madame Holymead is freed from the persecutions of the police agents," replied Gabrielle, rising from her seat as she spoke.
10082-8{_}4494{_}"If, after hearing my story, you could but give me the assurance--"  "I think I can safely promise you that Mrs. Holymead will not be troubled with any further police attentions," said Crewe, after a moment's pause.
10082-8{_}4495{_}Gabrielle broke into profuse expressions of gratitude as she turned to go.
10082-8{_}4496{_}"For the rest then, I care not what happens.
10082-8{_}4497{_}I am--how do you say it--I am overjoyed.
10082-8{_}4498{_}_Je vous remercie_, monsieur, I beg you not, I can find my way out unattended."
10082-8{_}4499{_}But Crewe showed her to the stairs, where again he had to listen to her profuse thanks before she finally departed.
10082-8{_}4500{_}He watched her graceful figure till it was lost to sight in the winding staircase, and then he turned back to his office.
10082-8{_}4501{_}In the outer office he stopped to speak to Joe, who, perched on an office-footstool, was tapping quickly on the office-table with his pen-knife, swaying backwards and forwards dangerously on his perch in the intensity of his emotions as he played the hero's part in the drama of saving the runaway engine from dashing into the 4.40 express by calling up the Red Gulch station on the wire.
10082-8{_}4502{_}"Joe," said Crewe, "I'll see nobody for an hour at least--nobody.
10082-8{_}4503{_}You understand?"
10082-8{_}4504{_}Joe came out of the cinema world long enough to nod his head in emphatic understanding of the instructions.
10082-8{_}4505{_}In his own room Crewe pulled out his notebook and once more gave himself up to the study of the baffling Riversbrook mystery, in the new light of Gabrielle's confession.
10082-8{_}4506{_}Part of her story, he reflected, must be true.
10082-8{_}4507{_}She had produced Sir Horace's revolver, and, still more important, a handkerchief which he had clutched in his dying struggles.
10082-8{_}4508{_}It was obvious that she or some other woman had been at Riversbrook the night of the murder, and in the room with the murdered man before he died.
10082-8{_}4509{_}That tallied with Birchill's statement to Hill that he had seen a woman close the front door and walk along the garden path while he was hiding in the garden.
10082-8{_}4510{_}Crewe, recalling Gabrielle's description of the room, came to the conclusion that it was probably she who had been with the judge in his dying moments.
10082-8{_}4511{_}No one but a person who had actually seen it could have described the room with such minuteness.
10082-8{_}4512{_}She had been in the room, then.
10082-8{_}4513{_}For what object?
10082-8{_}4514{_}For the reasons stated in her confession?
10082-8{_}4515{_}Crewe shook his head doubtfully.
10082-8{_}4516{_}"She evaded the trap about the pocket-book, but she made one bad mistake," he mused.
10082-8{_}4517{_}"The letters in the secret drawer were taken away, and I have no doubt were burnt as she says.
10082-8{_}4518{_}But were they her letters?
10082-8{_}4519{_}Was Sir Horace her lover?
10082-8{_}4520{_}At any rate, she did not get hold of them in the way she said.
10082-8{_}4521{_}They were not taken away on the night Sir Horace was murdered, for the simple reason that they were not in the secret drawer at the time."
10082-8{_}4522{_}CHAPTER XXIV   Rolfe was spending a quiet evening in his room after a trying day's inquiries into a confidence trick case; inquiries so fruitless that they had brought down on his head an official reproof from Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}4523{_}Rolfe had left Scotland Yard that evening in a somewhat despondent frame of mind in consequence, but a brisk walk home and a good supper had done him so much good, that with a tranquil mind and his pipe in his mouth, he was able to devote himself to the hobby of his leisure hours with keen enjoyment.
10082-8{_}4524{_}This hobby would have excited the wondering contempt of Joe Leaver, whose frequent attendance at cinema theatres had led him to the conclusion that police detectives--who, unlike his master, had to take the rough with the smooth--spent their spare time practising revolver shooting, and throwing daggers at an ace of hearts on the wall.
10082-8{_}4525{_}Rolfe's hobby was nothing more exciting than stamp collecting.
10082-8{_}4526{_}He was deeply versed in the lore of stamps, and his private ambition was to become the possessor of a "blue Mauritius."
10082-8{_}4527{_}His collection, though extensive, was by no means of fabulous value, being made up chiefly of modest purchases from the stamp collecting shops, and finds in the waste-paper-baskets at Scotland Yard after the arrival of the foreign mails.
10082-8{_}4528{_}That day he had made a particularly good haul from the waste-paper-baskets, for his "catch" included several comparatively good specimens from Japan and Fiji.
10082-8{_}4529{_}He sat gloating over these treasures, examining them carefully and holding each one up to the light as he separated it from the piece of paper to which it had been affixed.
10082-8{_}4530{_}He pasted them one by one in his stamp album with loving, lingering fingers, adjusting each stamp in its little square in the book with meticulous care.
10082-8{_}4531{_}He was so absorbed in this occupation that he did not hear the ascending footsteps drawing nearer to his door, and did not see a visitor at the door when the footsteps ceased.
10082-8{_}4532{_}It was Crewe's voice that recalled him back from the stamp collector's imaginary world.
10082-8{_}4533{_}"Why, Mr. Crewe," said Rolfe, with evident pleasure, "who'd have thought of seeing you?"
10082-8{_}4534{_}"Your landlady asked me if I'd come up myself," said Crewe, in explaining his intrusion.
10082-8{_}4535{_}"She's 'too much worried and put about, to say nothing of having a bad back,' to show me upstairs."
10082-8{_}4536{_}"I've never known her to be well," said Rolfe, with a laugh.
10082-8{_}4537{_}"Every morning when she brings up my breakfast I've got to hear details of her bad back which should be kept for the confidential ear of the doctor.
10082-8{_}4538{_}But she regards me as a son, I think--I've been here so long.
10082-8{_}4539{_}But now you are here, Mr. Crewe--" Rolfe waited in polite expectation that his visitor would disclose the object of his visit.
10082-8{_}4540{_}But Crewe seemed in no hurry to do so.
10082-8{_}4541{_}He produced his cigar case and offered Rolfe a cigar, which the latter accepted with a pleasant recollection of the excellent flavour of the cigars the private detective kept.
10082-8{_}4542{_}When each of them had his cigar well alight, Crewe glanced at the open stamp album and commenced talking about stamps.
10082-8{_}4543{_}It was a subject which Rolfe was always willing to discuss.
10082-8{_}4544{_}Crewe declared that he was an ignorant outsider as far as stamps were concerned, but he professed to have a respectful admiration for those who immersed themselves in such a fascinating subject.
10082-8{_}4545{_}Rolfe, with the fervid egoism of the collector, talked about stamps for half an hour without recalling that his visitor must have come to talk about something else.
10082-8{_}4546{_}"I've got a small stamp collection in my office," said Crewe, when Rolfe paused for a moment.
10082-8{_}4547{_}"It belonged to that Jewish diamond merchant who was shot in Hatton Gardens two years ago.
10082-8{_}4548{_}You remember his case?"
10082-8{_}4549{_}"Rather!
10082-8{_}4550{_}That was a smart bit of work of yours, Mr. Crewe, in laying your hands on the woman who did it and getting back the diamond."
10082-8{_}4551{_}Crewe smiled in response.
10082-8{_}4552{_}"The Jew was very grateful, poor fellow.
10082-8{_}4553{_}He died in the hospital after the trial, so she was lucky to escape with twelve years.
10082-8{_}4554{_}He left me a diamond ring and a stamp album that had come into his possession."
10082-8{_}4555{_}"I should like to see it," said Rolfe eagerly.
10082-8{_}4556{_}"It is more than likely that there are some good specimens in it.
10082-8{_}4557{_}The Jews are keen collectors.
10082-8{_}4558{_}If you let me have a look at it, I'll tell you what the collection is worth."
10082-8{_}4559{_}"You can have it altogether," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}4560{_}"I'll send my boy Joe round with it in the morning."
10082-8{_}4561{_}"Oh, Mr. Crewe, it's very good of you," said Rolfe, with the covetousness of the collector shining in his eyes.
10082-8{_}4562{_}"Nonsense!
10082-8{_}4563{_}Why shouldn't you have it?
10082-8{_}4564{_}But I didn't come round here solely to talk about stamps, Rolfe.
10082-8{_}4565{_}I came to have a little chat about the Riversbrook case.
10082-8{_}4566{_}How are you getting on with it?"
10082-8{_}4567{_}"Why, really," said Rolfe, "I've not done much with it since, since--"  "Since Birchill was acquitted, eh!
10082-8{_}4568{_}But you are not letting it drop altogether, are you?
10082-8{_}4569{_}That would be a pity--such an interesting case.
10082-8{_}4570{_}Whom have you your eye on now as the right man?"
10082-8{_}4571{_}Rolfe, who thought he detected a suspicion of banter in Crewe's remarks, evaded the latter question by answering the first part of Crewe's inquiry.
10082-8{_}4572{_}"Why hardly that, Mr. Crewe.
10082-8{_}4573{_}But the chief is not very keen on the case.
10082-8{_}4574{_}Birchill's acquittal was too much of a blow to him.
10082-8{_}4575{_}He reckons that nowadays juries are too soft-hearted to convict on a capital charge."
10082-8{_}4576{_}"It's just as well that they are too soft-hearted to convict the wrong man," said Crewe.
10082-8{_}4577{_}"Yes; you told me from the first that we were on the wrong track," was the reply.
10082-8{_}4578{_}"I haven't forgotten that and the chief is not allowed to forget it, either.
10082-8{_}4579{_}All the men at the Yard know that you held the opinion that we had got hold of the wrong man when we arrested Birchill, and he has had to stand so much chaff in the office, that he's pretty raw about it."
10082-8{_}4580{_}Rolfe spoke in the detached tone of a junior who had no share in his chief's mistakes or their attendant humiliation, and he added, "That's once more that you've scored over Scotland Yard, Mr. Crewe, and you ought to be proud of it."
10082-8{_}4581{_}He glanced covertly at Crewe to see how he took the flattery.
10082-8{_}4582{_}"So you've done very little about the case since Birchill was acquitted?"
10082-8{_}4583{_}was his only remark.
10082-8{_}4584{_}"I've been so busy," replied Rolfe, again evading the question, and avoiding meeting Crewe's glance by turning over the leaves of his stamp album.
10082-8{_}4585{_}"You see, there has been a rush of work at Scotland Yard lately.
10082-8{_}4586{_}There is that big burglary at Lord Emden's, and the case of the woman whose body was found in the river lock at Peyton, and half a dozen other cases, all important in their way.
10082-8{_}4587{_}There has been quite an epidemic of crime lately, as you know, Mr. Crewe.
10082-8{_}4588{_}I don't seem to get a minute to myself these times."
10082-8{_}4589{_}"Rolfe," said Crewe drily, "you protest too much.
10082-8{_}4590{_}You don't suppose that after coming over here to see you that I can be deceived by such talk?"
10082-8{_}4591{_}Rolfe flushed at these uncompromising words, but before he could speak Crewe proceeded in a milder tone.
10082-8{_}4592{_}"I don't blame you a bit for trying to put me off.
10082-8{_}4593{_}It's all part of the game.
10082-8{_}4594{_}We're rivals, in a sense, and you are quite right not to lose sight of that fact.
10082-8{_}4595{_}But as a detective, Rolfe, your methods lack polish.
10082-8{_}4596{_}Really, I blush for them.
10082-8{_}4597{_}You might have known that I came over here to see you to-night because I had an important object in view, and you should have tried to find out what it was before playing your own cards,--and such cards, too!
10082-8{_}4598{_}You're sadly lacking in finesse, Rolfe.
10082-8{_}4599{_}You'd never make a chess player; your concealed intentions are too easily discovered.
10082-8{_}4600{_}You must try not to be so transparent if you want to succeed in your profession."
10082-8{_}4601{_}Crewe delivered his reproof with such good humour that Rolfe stared at him, as if unable to make out what his visitor was driving at.
10082-8{_}4602{_}"I don't know what you are talking about, Mr. Crewe," he said at length.

10082-8{_}4808{_}CHAPTER XXV   Joe Leaver, worn out after nearly a week's work of watching the movements of Mr. Holymead, had fallen asleep in an empty loft above a garage which overlooked Verney's Hotel in Mayfair.
10082-8{_}4809{_}He had seen Mr. Holymead disappear into the hotel, and he knew from the experience gained in his watch that the K.C.
10082-8{_}4810{_}would spend the next couple of hours in dressing for dinner, sitting down to that meal, and smoking a cigar in the lounge.
10082-8{_}4811{_}So Joe had relaxed, for the time being, the new task which his master had set him, and had flung himself on some straw in the loft to rest.
10082-8{_}4812{_}He did not intend to go to sleep, but he was very tired, and in a few minutes he was in a profound slumber.
10082-8{_}4813{_}In his sleep Joe dreamed that he had attained the summit of his ambition, and was being paid a huge salary by an American film company to display himself in emotional dramas for the educational improvement of the British working classes.
10082-8{_}4814{_}In his dream he had to rescue the heroine from the clutches of the villains who had carried her off.
10082-8{_}4815{_}They had imprisoned her at the top of a "skyscraper" building and locked the lift, but Joe climbed the fire escape and caught the beautiful girl in his arms.
10082-8{_}4816{_}The villains, who were on the watch, set fire to the building, and when Joe attempted to climb out of the window with the heroine clinging round his neck, the flames drove him back.
10082-8{_}4817{_}As he stood there the wind swept a sheet of flame towards Joe until it scorched his face.
10082-8{_}4818{_}The pain was so real that Joe opened his eyes and sprang up with a cry.
10082-8{_}4819{_}A man was standing over him, a man past middle age, short and broad in figure, whose clean-shaven face directed attention to his protruding jaw.
10082-8{_}4820{_}He was wearing a blue serge suit which had seen much use.
10082-8{_}4821{_}"You are a sound sleeper, sonny," said the man, grinning at Joe's alarm.
10082-8{_}4822{_}"But when you wake--why you wake up properly; I'll say that for you.
10082-8{_}4823{_}You nearly broke my pipe, you woke up that sudden."
10082-8{_}4824{_}He made this remark with such a malicious grin that Joe, whose face was still smarting, had no hesitation in connecting his sudden awakening with the hot bowl of the man's pipe.
10082-8{_}4825{_}It was a joke Joe had often seen played on drunken men in Islington public-houses in his young days.
10082-8{_}4826{_}"You just leave me alone, will you?"
10082-8{_}4827{_}he said, rubbing his cheek ruefully.
10082-8{_}4828{_}"It's nothing to do with you whether I'm a sound sleeper or not."
10082-8{_}4829{_}"That's just where you're wrong, young fellow," was the reply.
10082-8{_}4830{_}"It's a lot to do with me.
10082-8{_}4831{_}Ain't your name Joe Leaver?"
10082-8{_}4832{_}Joe nodded his head.
10082-8{_}4833{_}"How did you find out?"
10082-8{_}4834{_}he asked.
10082-8{_}4835{_}"Perhaps a friend of mine pointed you out to me."
10082-8{_}4836{_}"Perhaps he did, and perhaps he didn't," said Joe.
10082-8{_}4837{_}"Anyway, what is your name?"
10082-8{_}4838{_}"Mr. Kemp is my name, my boy.
10082-8{_}4839{_}And unless you're pretty civil I'll give you cause to remember it."
10082-8{_}4840{_}"What have you got to do with me?"
10082-8{_}4841{_}asked the boy in an injured tone.
10082-8{_}4842{_}"I've never done nothing to you."
10082-8{_}4843{_}"You mind your P's and Q's and me and you'll get along all right," said Mr. Kemp, in a somewhat softer tone.
10082-8{_}4844{_}"When you ask me what I've got to do with you, my answer is I've got a lot to do with you, for I'm your guardian, so to speak."
10082-8{_}4845{_}Joe looked at Mr. Kemp with a gleam of comprehension in his amazement.
10082-8{_}4846{_}He had had some experience in his Islington days of the strange phenomena produced by drink.
10082-8{_}4847{_}"Rats!"
10082-8{_}4848{_}he retorted rudely.
10082-8{_}4849{_}"I've never had a guardian and I don't want none.
10082-8{_}4850{_}What made you a guardian, I'd like to know?"
10082-8{_}4851{_}"Your father did," was the reply.
10082-8{_}4852{_}"Oh, him!"
10082-8{_}4853{_}said Joe, in a tone which indicated pronounced antipathy to his parent.

10082-8{_}4877{_}asked Mr. Kemp contemptuously.

10082-8{_}4889{_}he said, as he turned to go down the stairs.
10082-8{_}4890{_}Half an hour later Mr. Kemp entered the lounge of Verney's Hotel as though in quest of some one.
10082-8{_}4891{_}Most of the hotel guests had finished their after-dinner coffee and liqueurs, and the hall was comparatively empty, but a few who remained raised their eyes in well-bred protest at the intrusion of a member of the lower orders into the corridor of an exclusive hotel.

10082-8{_}4898{_}"I want to see Mr. Holymead," said Mr. Kemp in a gruff voice.
10082-8{_}4899{_}Verney's was such a high-class hotel that seedy-looking persons seldom dared to put a foot within the palatial entrance.
10082-8{_}4900{_}The porter, unused to dealing with the obtrusive impecunious type to which he believed Mr. Kemp to belong, made the mistake of trying to argue with him.

10082-8{_}5009{_}Kemp scowled at the manager as if he were a valued patron of the hotel and said, "It seems to me that you don't know how to treat people properly when they come here."
10082-8{_}5010{_}CHAPTER XXVI   It was the first occasion on which Mrs. Holymead had visited her husband's chambers in the Middle Temple.
10082-8{_}5011{_}Mr. Mattingford, who had been Mr. Holymead's clerk for nearly twenty years, seemed to realise that the visit was important, though as a married man he knew that a meeting between husband and wife in town was usually so commonplace as to verge on boredom for the husband.
10082-8{_}5012{_}There were occasions when he had to meet Mrs. Mattingford, but these meetings were generally for the purpose of handing over to the lady her weekly dress allowance of ten shillings out of his salary, so that she might attend the sales at the big drapery shops in the West End and inspect the windows containing expensive articles that she could not hope to buy.
10082-8{_}5013{_}Mr. Mattingford was an exceedingly thrifty man, and his wife possessed some of the qualities of a spendthrift.
10082-8{_}5014{_}Thus it came about that Mr. Mattingford kept up the fiction that he had no savings and that each week's salary must see him through till the next week.
10082-8{_}5015{_}Mrs. Mattingford knew that her husband had saved money, and theoretically she would have given a great deal to know how much.
10082-8{_}5016{_}She repeatedly accused him of being a miser, but this is a wifely denunciation which in all classes of life is lightly made when the purchase of feminine finery is under discussion.
10082-8{_}5017{_}There are some men who resent it, but Mr. Mattingford was not one of these.
10082-8{_}5018{_}Protests and prayers, abuse and cajolery, were alike powerless to win his consent to his wife's perpetual proposal that she should be allowed to draw her dress allowance for some months, or even some weeks ahead.
10082-8{_}5019{_}Mr. Mattingford had a horror of bad debts.
10082-8{_}5020{_}He endeavoured to show his wife that the transaction she proposed was unsound from a business point of view and reckless from a legal point of view.
10082-8{_}5021{_}She had no security to offer for the repayment of the advance--even if he were in a financial position to make the advance--and he stoutly declared that he was not.
10082-8{_}5022{_}She might die at any moment, and then he would be left with no means of redress against her estate because she had no estate.
10082-8{_}5023{_}Of course, if she first insured her life out of her dress allowance and handed the policy to him it would constitute protection for the repayment of the advance, in the event of her death, but it was not any real protection in the event of her continuing to live, for a newly-executed policy had no surrender value.
10082-8{_}5024{_}As his own legal adviser, Mr. Mattingford strongly urged himself not to consider his wife's proposal, and such was his respect for the law and for those who had been brought up in a legal atmosphere that he had no hesitation in accepting the advice.
10082-8{_}5025{_}He was a little man of nearly fifty years, with a very bald head and an extremely long moustache, which when waxed at the ends made him look as fierce as a clipped poodle.
10082-8{_}5026{_}He knew Mrs. Holymead from his having called frequently at his chief's house in Princes Gate on business matters, and he admired her for her good looks, but still more for her good taste in staying away from her husband's chambers.
10082-8{_}5027{_}There were some ladies, the wives of barristers, who almost haunted their husbands' chambers--a practice of which Mr. Mattingford strongly disapproved.
10082-8{_}5028{_}It seemed to him an insidious attempt on the part of an insidious sex to force the legal profession to throw open its doors to women.
10082-8{_}5029{_}As a man who lived in the mouldy atmosphere of precedent, Mr. Mattingford hated the idea of change, and to him the thought of a lady in wig and gown pleading in the law courts indicated not merely change but a revolution which might well usher in the end of the world.
10082-8{_}5030{_}So strict was he in keeping the precincts of the law sacred from the violating tread of women that he never allowed his wife to set foot in the Middle Temple.
10082-8{_}5031{_}Their meetings on those urgent occasions when Mrs. Mattingford came to town for her dress allowance in order to go bargain-hunting took place at one of the cheap tearooms in Fleet Street.
10082-8{_}5032{_}Although Mr. Mattingford was somewhat flustered by the unexpected appearance of Mrs. Holymead, he did not depart from precedent to the extent of regarding her as entitled to any other treatment than that accorded to clients who called on business.
10082-8{_}5033{_}He asked her if she wanted to see Mr. Holymead, placed a chair for her, then knocked deferentially at his chief's door, went inside to announce Mrs. Holymead to her husband, and came out with the information that Mr. Holymead would see her.
10082-8{_}5034{_}He held open the door leading into his chief's private room, and after Mrs. Holymead had entered closed it softly and firmly.
10082-8{_}5035{_}But the formal business manner of Mr. Mattingford to his chief's wife seemed to her friendly and cordial compared with the strained greetings she received from her husband.
10082-8{_}5036{_}He motioned her to a chair and then got up from his own.
10082-8{_}5037{_}"I wrote to you to come and see me here instead of going to the house to see you," he said, "because I thought it would be better for both.
10082-8{_}5038{_}It would have given the servants something to talk about.
10082-8{_}5039{_}I hope you don't mind?"
10082-8{_}5040{_}She looked at him with her large dark eyes in which there was more than a suggestion of tears.
10082-8{_}5041{_}What she had read into his note, when she received it, was his determination not to go to his home to see her for fear she would interpret that as a first step towards reconciliation.
10082-8{_}5042{_}"What I wanted to speak to you about is this detective Crewe whom Miss Fewbanks has employed in connection with her father's death," he continued.
10082-8{_}5043{_}Her breath came quickly at this unwelcome information.
10082-8{_}5044{_}She noted that he had spoken of Sir Horace's death and not his murder.
10082-8{_}5045{_}He began pacing backwards and forwards across the room as if with the purpose of avoiding looking at her.
10082-8{_}5046{_}"This man Crewe is a nuisance--I might even say a danger.
10082-8{_}5047{_}I don't know what he has found out, but I object to his ferreting into my affairs.
10082-8{_}5048{_}He must be stopped."
10082-8{_}5049{_}She nodded her assent, for she could not trust herself to speak.
10082-8{_}5050{_}Each time he turned his back on her as he crossed the room her eyes followed him, but as he faced her she turned her gaze on the floor.
10082-8{_}5051{_}"There is no legal redress--no legal means of dealing with his impertinent curiosity," he went on.
10082-8{_}5052{_}"He is within his rights in trying to find out all he can.
10082-8{_}5053{_}But if he is allowed to go on unchecked the thing may reach a disastrous stage.
10082-8{_}5054{_}I have no doubt that he knows that I was at Riversbrook the night that man was killed.
10082-8{_}5055{_}He was not long in getting on the track of that.
10082-8{_}5056{_}And the more mysterious my visit seems to him--and the fact that I have not disclosed to the police that I went up to Riversbrook and saw Sir Horace on the night of the tragedy is to his way of thinking very significant--the more reason is there for suspecting me of complicity in the crime."
10082-8{_}5057{_}When he turned to cross the room her eyes lingered on him and she glanced quickly at his face.
10082-8{_}5058{_}"I don't want to dwell on matters that must pain you--that must pain us both," he said slowly, "but it is necessary that you should be made acquainted with the danger that threatens me from this man.
10082-8{_}5059{_}I am anxious to avoid anything in the nature of a public scandal--I am anxious quite as much if not more on your account than my own.
10082-8{_}5060{_}But if this wretched man is allowed to go on trying to build up a case against me--and I must admit that he would probably obtain circumstantial evidence of a kind which would make some sort of a case for the prosecution--there is grave danger of everything coming out.
10082-8{_}5061{_}If he went to the length of having me arrested and charged with the crime, there are bound to be some disclosures and the newspapers would make the most of them.
10082-8{_}5062{_}It is impossible to foresee the exact nature of them, but I do not see how I could adopt any line of defence which would not hint at things that are best unrevealed.
10082-8{_}5063{_}You yourself might be so ill-advised as to tell the whole story in the end.
10082-8{_}5064{_}Of course, I would try to prevent you, and as far as the trial is concerned, I think I could use means to prevent you.
10082-8{_}5065{_}But if the result was unfavourable--and knowing what eccentric things juries do, we must recognise the possibility of an unfavourable verdict--you might consider it advisable to disclose everything in the hope of having the conviction quashed by an appeal."
10082-8{_}5066{_}For the first time since she had sat down he looked at her, and as he caught her upward gaze he flushed.
10082-8{_}5067{_}"I would tell everything if you were arrested," she said, in a low voice.
10082-8{_}5068{_}"Ah, so I thought," he said, in a tone of disapproval.
10082-8{_}5069{_}"The question now is what means can be adopted to prevent a catastrophe.
10082-8{_}5070{_}I have thought earnestly about it, and as you are almost as much concerned in preventing public disclosures as I am, I desired to consult you before taking any definite course.
10082-8{_}5071{_}It is this man Crewe who is the danger, and the question is how are we to stop him proceeding to extremes.
10082-8{_}5072{_}One way is for me to see him and take him into my confidence--to explain fully to him what happened.
10082-8{_}5073{_}He would not be satisfied with less than the full story.
10082-8{_}5074{_}If I kept anything back his suspicions would remain; in fact, they would be strengthened.
10082-8{_}5075{_}I would have to explain to him why and how I induced Sir Horace to return unexpectedly from Scotland on that fatal night, and what took place at Riversbrook.
10082-8{_}5076{_}You will understand why I have hesitated to adopt that course.
10082-8{_}5077{_}I would not suggest it to you now except that I see it would save you from the danger of something a great deal worse.
10082-8{_}5078{_}Of course it would save me from the annoyance of being suspected of knowing something about the actual murder, but it is your interests that come first in the matter.
10082-8{_}5079{_}It would be effective in putting an end to all our fears--all my fears.
10082-8{_}5080{_}I would bind him to secrecy, of course.
10082-8{_}5081{_}I do not ask you to come to a decision immediately, but I do ask you to think it over and let me know.

10082-8{_}5174{_}CHAPTER XXVII   She left her husband's chambers with her brain in a whirl, hardly knowing where she was going until she found herself held up with a stream of pedestrians at the island intersection of Waterloo Bridge and the Strand.
10082-8{_}5175{_}She thought the policeman who was regulating the traffic eyed her curiously, and, more with the object of evading his eye than with any set plan in her mind, she stepped into an empty taxi-cab which was waiting to cross the street.
10082-8{_}5176{_}"Where to, ma'am?"
10082-8{_}5177{_}asked the driver.
10082-8{_}5178{_}"Where to?"
10082-8{_}5179{_}she repeated vacantly.

10082-8{_}5181{_}No--wait--driver, first take me to the nearest bookstall."

10082-8{_}5194{_}"It's almost half-past twelve now.

10082-8{_}5196{_}she asked.
10082-8{_}5197{_}The driver looked dubious.
10082-8{_}5198{_}"I'll try, ma'am, but it'll take some doing.
10082-8{_}5199{_}It depends whether I get a clear run at Trafalgar Square."

10082-8{_}5201{_}she cried.
10082-8{_}5202{_}"Catch it, and I will double your fare."
10082-8{_}5203{_}She caught the train with a few seconds to spare.
10082-8{_}5204{_}She had a first-class compartment to herself, and as the train rushed out of London, and the grimy environs of the metropolis gradually gave place to green fields, she endeavoured to compose her mind and collect her thoughts for her coming interview with the daughter of the murdered man.
10082-8{_}5205{_}But her mind was in such a distraught condition that she could think of no plan but to sacrifice herself in order to save her husband.

10082-8{_}5207{_}I will tell her everything."
10082-8{_}5208{_}The train ran into Horleydene shortly after two, and Mrs. Holymead was the only passenger who alighted at the lonely little wayside station which stood in a small wood in a solitude as profound as though it had been in the American prairie, instead of the heart of an English county.
10082-8{_}5209{_}The only sign of life was a dilapidated vehicle with an elderly man in charge, which stood outside the station yard all day waiting for chance visitors.
10082-8{_}5210{_}"Cab, ma'am?"

10082-8{_}5262{_}Mr. Holymead had discovered it after Sir Horace had gone to Scotland and husband and wife were away in the country.
10082-8{_}5263{_}He was at first distracted at finding that his lifelong friend had seduced his wife, then he made her promise not to see or communicate with Sir Horace until he made up his mind what course of action to take.
10082-8{_}5264{_}Three days later he caught an evening train to London and told her he was not returning, but would write to her.
10082-8{_}5265{_}It crossed her mind that he had gone up to London to meet Sir Horace, and in her distress at the thought of what might happen when they met she consulted her cousin Gabrielle, who had always been in her confidence.
10082-8{_}5266{_}Gabrielle had offered to go to Riversbrook to see if Sir Horace had returned from Scotland, or was expected back.
10082-8{_}5267{_}Her train was delayed by an accident, and when she arrived at Riversbrook it was after half-past ten.
10082-8{_}5268{_}She arrived a few minutes too late to prevent the tragedy.
10082-8{_}5269{_}She found the front door open and the electric light burning in the hall.

10082-8{_}5283{_}The door opened and the parlourmaid entered.

10082-8{_}5291{_}"I will save your husband if I can."
10082-8{_}5292{_}CHAPTER XXVIII   She was conscious that the revelation that her father had been killed by Mr. Holymead was a less shock than the revelation that her father had dishonoured the great friendship of his life by seducing his friend's wife.
10082-8{_}5293{_}Her father had been dead three months, and her grief had run its course.
10082-8{_}5294{_}The shock caused by the discovery that he had been murdered had passed away, and she had begun to accept his violent death as part of her own experience of life.
10082-8{_}5295{_}But the discovery that he had betrayed his best friend, in a way that a pure-minded woman regards as the most dishonourable way possible, was a fresh revelation to her of human infamy.
10082-8{_}5296{_}The knowledge that her father had been a man of immoral habits was not new to her.
10082-8{_}5297{_}His predilection for fast women had long ago made it impossible for her to live in the same house with him for more than a week at a time.
10082-8{_}5298{_}But that he had trampled in the mire the lifelong friendship of an honourable man for the sake of an ignoble passion revealed an unexpected depth of shame.
10082-8{_}5299{_}That Mr. Holymead had killed him seemed almost a natural result of the situation.
10082-8{_}5300{_}It was not that she felt that a just retribution had overtaken her father, but rather that she was glad his shameful conduct had come to an end.
10082-8{_}5301{_}As she thought of her dead father--dead these three months--she gave a sigh of relief.
10082-8{_}5302{_}The wretched guilty woman, who had shared with him the shame of his ignoble intrigue, had said that if her father could make his wishes known he would plead for the life of the friend he had dishonoured.
10082-8{_}5303{_}But it was not her father's plea for the life of his friend that would have impressed her so much as a plea to bury the whole unsavoury scandal from the light.
10082-8{_}5304{_}She had promised to save Mr. Holymead if she could, but that promise had sprung less from the spirit of mercy than from the desire to save her father's name from a scandal, which would hold him up to public obloquy.
10082-8{_}5305{_}She greeted Crewe with friendly warmth in spite of the feeling of oppression caused by the consciousness of the situation in front of her.
10082-8{_}5306{_}He did not sit down again after greeting her, but stood with one hand resting on an inlaid chess table, with wonderful carved red and white Japanese chessmen ranged on each side, which he had been examining when she entered the room.

10082-8{_}5308{_}"You have found out who killed my father?"
10082-8{_}5309{_}she asked quietly.
10082-8{_}5310{_}Crewe had sufficient personal pride to feel a little hurt when he saw the calm way in which she accepted the result of his investigations, instead of congratulating him on his success in a difficult task.

10082-8{_}5319{_}"I had to be told."
10082-8{_}5320{_}"And who told you, Miss Fewbanks?"
10082-8{_}5321{_}he asked.
10082-8{_}5322{_}"Has he confessed to you?
10082-8{_}5323{_}How long have you known it?"
10082-8{_}5324{_}"I have known it only a few minutes," she said.
10082-8{_}5325{_}"Will you tell me how you got on the track and all you have done?
10082-8{_}5326{_}I am greatly interested.
10082-8{_}5327{_}You have been wonderfully clever to find out.

10082-8{_}5329{_}When you have finished I will tell you how I came to know.
10082-8{_}5330{_}The story is extremely simple--and sordid."
10082-8{_}5331{_}The fact that the key of the mystery had been in her hands only a few minutes was a solace to Crewe, as it detracted but little from the story he had to tell of patient investigations extending over weeks.
10082-8{_}5332{_}He pieced together the story of the tragedy as he had unravelled it.
10082-8{_}5333{_}Hill, he said, had conceived the idea of blackmailing her father after he had discovered the existence of some letters in a secret drawer of Sir Horace's desk.
10082-8{_}5334{_}The fact that Sir Horace had kept these letters instead of destroying them as he had destroyed other letters of a somewhat similar kind showed that he was very much infatuated with the lady who wrote them.
10082-8{_}5335{_}That lady, as doubtless Miss Fewbanks had guessed, was Mrs. Holymead--a lady with whom Sir Horace had been on very friendly terms before she married Mr. Holymead.
10082-8{_}5336{_}"What became of the letters?"
10082-8{_}5337{_}asked Miss Fewbanks.
10082-8{_}5338{_}"Have you got them?"
10082-8{_}5339{_}"I think they are destroyed," he said.
10082-8{_}5340{_}"Mrs. Holymead removed them from the secret drawer the day after the discovery of the murder.
10082-8{_}5341{_}She removed them when the police had charge of the house, and almost from under the eyes of Inspector Chippenfield.
10082-8{_}5342{_}It was a daring plan and well carried out."
10082-8{_}5343{_}Miss Fewbanks heaved a sigh of relief on learning the fate of the letters.
10082-8{_}5344{_}It had been her intention to endeavour to obtain them if they were in Crewe's possession, and destroy them.
10082-8{_}5345{_}Crewe explained that Hill was afraid to take the letters and then boldly blackmail Sir Horace.
10082-8{_}5346{_}The butler conceived the plan of getting Birchill to break into the house.
10082-8{_}5347{_}He did not take Birchill into his confidence with regard to the blackmailing scheme, but in order to induce Sir Horace to believe the burglar had stolen the letters he told Birchill to force open the desk, as he would probably find money or papers of value there.
10082-8{_}5348{_}But in order to prevent Birchill getting the letters if he should happen to stumble across the secret drawer, Hill removed them the day before.
10082-8{_}5349{_}His plan was to go to Riversbrook in the morning after the burglary, and after leaving open the secret drawer which had contained the letters, to report the burglary to the police.
10082-8{_}5350{_}When Sir Horace came home unexpectedly Hill had just removed the letters and had them in his possession.
10082-8{_}5351{_}Hill was greatly perturbed at his master's unexpected return, and had to get an opportunity to replace the letters in the secret drawer, but Sir Horace told him to go home, as he was not wanted till the morning.
10082-8{_}5352{_}Hill went to that girl's flat in Westminster, and there saw Birchill.
10082-8{_}5353{_}He told Birchill that Sir Horace had returned unexpectedly, but he urged Birchill to carry out the burglary as arranged, and assured him that as Sir Horace was a heavy sleeper there would be no risk if he waited until Sir Horace went to bed.
10082-8{_}5354{_}Hill's position was that if the burglary was postponed Sir Horace might make the discovery that the letters had been stolen from the secret drawer.
10082-8{_}5355{_}In that case Sir Horace would immediately suspect Hill, who, he knew, was an ex-convict.
10082-8{_}5356{_}It was just possible that Sir Horace, before going to bed, would discover that the letters had been stolen--that is, if he went to bed before Birchill got into the place--but Hill had to take that risk.
10082-8{_}5357{_}It was the fact that the burglary Hill had arranged with Birchill took place on the night Sir Horace was killed that had given rise to the false clues which had misled the police.
10082-8{_}5358{_}Crewe, as he himself modestly put it, was so fortunate as to get on the right track from the start.
